Prophecy and Certainty: Ghazali's Refutation of the Dream Theory
Second refutation: reducing revelation to dreams destroys certainty and the Sharia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second refutation: that reducing prophetic revelation to imagination like dreams undermines the certainty of knowledge and the entire Sharia.

Core Idea
If revelation is just a dream, the prophet can never be certain.

The philosophers not only distort divine speech but also undermine the very foundation of prophethood. Ghazali argues that equating revelation with dream imagery destroys the epistemic security of the prophet. If the prophet cannot distinguish between true revelation and a mere dream, he has no certainty, and the entire religious law collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the second refutation: 'والثاني: أنَّ ما ذكروه ردُّ للشرع كله...'
2
Understand the analogy to dreams
Ghazali says what the sleeper perceives is imagination with no reality. If revelation is similar, it's unreliable.
Note the phrase 'أضغاث الأحلام' meaning confused dreams.
3
Consider the consequence for certainty
The prophet would not have certainty because he can't distinguish revelation from imagination.
4
Reflect on the wider impact
This would reject the entire Sharia, as argued by Ghazali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة : فإنهم ناقضوا في الكلام، وهو باطل من وجهين: أحدهما: قولهم: إن الله تعالى متكلم مع أنهم لا يثبتون كلام النفس ولا يثبتون الأصوات في الوجود، وإنما يثبتون سماع الصوت بالخلْقِ في أُذُنِ النبي من غير صوت من خارج، فلو جاز أن يكون بما يحدث في دماغ غيره موصوفاً بأنه متكلم .. لجاز أن يكون موصوفاً بأنه مُصوّت ومتحرك بوجود الصوت والحركة في غيره، وذلك محال. والثاني: أنَّ ما ذكروه ردُّ للشرع كله؛ فإن ما يدركه النائم خيال لا حقيقة له، فإذا ردَّ معرفة النبي بكلام الله تعالى إلى التخيل الذي يشبه أضغاث الأحلام.. فلا يثق به النبي، ولا يكون ذلك علم
As for the philosophers, they have contradicted themselves regarding speech, and it is false in two ways: First: their statement that God is speaking while they do not affirm internal speech, nor do they affirm sounds in existence; they only affirm hearing of sound by creation in the ear of the prophet without any sound from outside. If it were permissible that one is described as speaking due to what occurs in the brain of another, then it would be permissible that he be described as sounding and moving by the existence of sound and movement in another, and that is impossible. Second: what they have mentioned is a rejection of the entire Law; for what the sleeper perceives is imagination with no reality. If the prophet's knowledge of God's speech is reduced to imagination resembling confused dreams, then the prophet would not be certain of it, and that would not be knowledge.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' theory of divine speech and revelation. Ghazali gives two arguments. First, the philosophers claim God is 'speaking' but deny both internal speech (kalam nafsi) and external sounds; they only allow that God creates a sound in the prophet's ear without an external source. Ghazali argues that if being described as 'speaking' is based on something happening in someone else's brain, then by the same logic God could be described as 'sounding' or 'moving' based on sounds or movements in others, which is absurd. Second, the philosophers reduce prophetic experience to mere imagination like dreams; this undermines the certainty of revelation. If the prophet cannot distinguish revelation from a dream, he has no real knowledge, and the entire Sharia is rejected.

Key Terms
نبي
وحي
أضغاث الأحلام
خيال
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers' view preserves the authenticity of revelation.
Correction: Ghazali argues it destroys certainty because revelation would be indistinguishable from unreliable dreams.

Checkpoint
What reason does Ghazali give that reducing prophecy to dreams undermines certainty?
A) Dreams are from Satan
B) The prophet cannot distinguish revelation from imagination
C) Philosophers deny God's existence
D) Dreams have no meaning

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second refutation shows that equating revelation with dreams robs the prophet of certainty and thereby rejects the entire divine law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were in the place of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and could not be certain that what you received was from God, would you still give your life to convey it? How does Ghazali's argument make you appreciate the certainty that the Prophet actually had?
?
Have you ever treated a vivid dream or a strong feeling as if it were a divine message? After reading this passage, how do you view the difference between such experiences and the true revelation that God sent down with full certainty?
?
When you read the Quran, do you sometimes approach it with doubt, as if it could be a product of a human mind? How does Ghazali's refutation challenge you to examine the basis of your own certainty in the Quran as the word of God, and what will you do to strengthen that certainty today?

Divine Attributes: Neither Identical Nor Other
Ghazali responds to the question of whether attributes are other than Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects both saying attributes are 'other than' Allah and saying they are identical to the essence, and what his proposed middle path is.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not other than Allah nor identical to Him; they are like parts of a whole or properties of a person.

The relationship between God's essence and attributes is a delicate theological topic. Some say the attributes are identical to the essence; others say they are separate additions. Ghazali navigates between these extremes using everyday analogies. In this passage, he explains why the simple binary of 'other or not other' fails when we consider how names signify.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of Ghazali's argument.
Notice that the passage begins with a question and answer.
2
Identify the rejected extremes
Ghazali rejects the statement that attributes are 'other than' Allah. He also implicitly rejects that they are identical. Note how he says a hand is not Zayd nor other than Zayd.
The binary of 'other vs. identical' is insufficient for parts of a whole.
3
Understand the analogies
Examine the hand-Zayd and fiqh-faqih analogies. They show that a part is not a separate entity but also not identical to the whole.
The name 'Zayd' includes his hand; similarly, 'Allah' includes the attributes.
4
Grasp the conceptual distinction and conditions
Ghazali allows saying 'attribute is other than essence' in a conceptual sense, like accident vs. substance, but with two conditions: (1) the Law does not forbid, (2) (second condition missing).
The passage cuts off before mentioning the second condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : أفتقولون : إن صفاتِ الله تعالى غير الله ؟ قلنا : هذا خطأ، فإنا إذا قلنا : ( الله تعالى ) . . فقد دللنا على الذات الصفات، لا على الذات بمجردها؛ إذ اسم الله تعالى لا يصدق على ذاتٍ قُدرَ خلوها عن صفات الإلهية؛ كما لا يقال: الفقه غيرُ الفقيه، ويد زيد غير زيد، ويد النجار غيرُ النجار؛ لأن بعض الداخل في الاسم لا يكون غير الداخل في الاسم.. فيد زيد ليست هي زيداً ولا هي غير زيد، بل كلا اللفظين محال، وهكذا كلُّ بعض؛ فليس هو غير الكل ولا هو بعينه الكلُّ، فلو قيل: الفقه غيرُ الإنسان .. فهذا يجوز، ولا يجوز أن يقال: غيرُ الفقيه؛ فإن الإنسان لا يدلُّ على صفة الفقه، فلا جرم يجوز أن يقال: الصفة غيرُ الذات التي تقوم بها الصفة؛ كما يقال: العرضُ القائم بالجوهر غير الجوهر؛ على معنى أن مفهوم اسمه غير مفهوم اسم الآخر، وهذا جائز بشرطين: أحدهما: ألا يمنع الشرع من إطلاقه، وهذا يختص بالله تعالى
If it is said: Do you say that the attributes of Allah are other than Allah? We say: this is wrong. For when we say 'Allah', we signify the essence [with] the attributes, not the essence alone. Indeed, the name 'Allah' does not apply to an essence supposed to be empty of divine attributes. Just as it is not said: 'fiqh is other than the faqih', nor 'Zayd's hand is other than Zayd', nor 'the carpenter's hand is other than the carpenter'; because a part included in the name is not other than what is included in the name. So Zayd's hand is not Zayd, nor is it other than Zayd; rather, both expressions are impossible. And likewise every part: it is not other than the whole, nor is it exactly the whole. If it were said: 'fiqh is other than man', that is permissible, but it is not permissible to say: 'other than the faqih', because 'man' does not indicate the attribute of fiqh. Hence it is permissible to say: the attribute is other than the essence in which the attribute inheres, just as it is said: the accident subsisting in the substance is other than the substance, meaning that the concept of its name is other than the concept of the other. And this is permissible on two conditions: one of them: that the Law does not forbid its usage, and this is specific to Allah.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer clarifying the relationship between God's essence and attributes. The questioner asks if attributes are 'other than' Allah. The answer is that this formulation is mistaken because the name 'Allah' denotes the essence together with attributes, not a bare essence. Just as we cannot say a hand is 'other than' the person, because the hand is part of the person, but it is not identical to the person either. Likewise, attributes are not identical to the essence nor entirely separate; they are like parts of a whole. However, when the attribute's concept differs from the essence's concept (like an accident vs. substance), it is permissible to say they are 'other' in terms of concept, but only if the Law does not prohibit such language, especially regarding Allah. Thus, the relationship is nuanced: attributes are neither exactly the essence nor completely other; they are aspects of the same reality.

Key Terms
صفات
ذات
غير
فقيه
عرض
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That saying 'attributes are other than Allah' means they are completely separate from Him.
Correction: Ghazali shows that 'other' need not mean separate; in the case of parts and wholes, a part is not identical to the whole nor entirely other. Similarly, attributes are aspects of the divine reality, not separate entities.

Checkpoint
Why is it incorrect to say 'the attributes of Allah are other than Allah'?
A) Because attributes do not exist
B) Because the name 'Allah' signifies the essence with attributes, not the essence alone
C) Because attributes are identical to the essence
D) Because the Law forbids it unconditionally

Key Takeaway
Ghazali teaches that divine attributes are neither identical to the essence nor completely other; they are like parts of a whole, conceptually distinct but not separate realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah, do you truly believe that His attributes are inseparable from His essence, and that you are calling upon the One who possesses mercy and power? Or do you sometimes think of His attributes as separate? How does this affect your worship?
?
The passage uses analogies like the hand is not other than Zayd. In what areas of your life do you separate aspects of Allah's character that should be held together? For instance, do you fear His justice without trusting His mercy?
?
This lesson teaches that we must not say attributes are other than Allah, but also we must not say they are identical. This requires a nuanced understanding. What is your heart's response? Are you willing to accept that some truths about Allah are beyond simple yes/no, requiring a 'both/and' posture that humbles your intellect before His majesty?

Are the Attributes Other Than the Essence?
Al-Ghazali answers whether attributes are other than the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's nuanced answer to whether divine attributes are 'other than' the essence, including the conceptual distinction and the two conditions for such language.

Core Idea
Attributes are conceptually distinct from the essence, not separate entities.

Ghazali tackles a classic kalam question: Are God's attributes 'other than' God? He warns against simplistic yes/no answers. Through everyday analogies, he shows that 'otherness' can mean conceptual distinction without real separation. The passage then lays down two crucial conditions for applying this term to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Notice how the objector asks a seemingly straightforward question. Ghazali first corrects the phrasing: 'this is an error.' Understand why he rejects the framing.
2
Analyze the analogies
Ghazali uses three analogies: jurisprudence/jurist, hand/Zayd, accident/substance. What do they teach about part-whole and attribute-essence relationships?
These analogies show that something can be neither identical nor entirely other.
3
Learn the two conditions
Ghazali permits saying 'attribute is other than essence' only if two conditions are met. Identify and memorize both conditions.
The first condition is legal (no prohibition), the second is metaphysical (no implication of independent existence).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali ends by distinguishing between meaning and wording. How does this tie back to the initial error?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : أفتقولون : إن صفاتِ الله تعالى غير الله ؟ قلنا : هذا خطاً، فإنا إذا قلنا : ( الله تعالى ) . . فقد دللنا على الذات الصفات، لا على الذات بمجردها؛ إذ اسم الله تعالى لا يصدق على ذاتٍ قُدرَ خلوها عن صفات الإلهية؛ كما لا يقال : الفقه غيرُ الفقيه، ويد زيد غير زيد، ويد النجار غيرُ النجار؛ لأن بعض الداخل في الاسم لا يكون الصفات ليس بعين غير الداخل في الاسم.. فيد زيد ليست هي زيداً ولا هي غير زيد، بل كلا الذات ولا غيرها اللفظين محال، وهكذا كلُّ بعض؛ فليس هو غير الكل ولا هو بعينه الكلُّ، فلو قيل : الفقه غيرُ الإنسان .. فهذا يجوز، ولا يجوز أن يقال : غيرُ الفقيه؛ فإن الإنسان لا يدلُّ على صفة الفقه، فلا جرم يجوز أن يقال : الصفة غيرُ الذات التي تقوم بها الصفة؛ كما يقال : العرضُ القائم بالجوهر غير الجوهر؛ على معنى أن مفهوم اسمه غير مفهوم اسم الآخر، وهذا جائز بشرطين : أحدهما : ألا يمنع الشرع من إطلاقه، وهذا يختص بالله تعالى. والثاني : ألا يفهم من الغيرية ما يجوز وجوده دونَ الذي هو غير بالإضافة إليه؛ فإنه إن فهم ذلك .. لم يمكن أن يقال : سواد زيد غير زيد؛ لأنه لا يوجد دون زيد. فإذن؛ قد انكشف بهاذا ما هو حظ المعنى وما هو حظ اللفظ، فلا معنى للتطويل في الجليات.
If it is said: 'Do you say that the attributes of Allah, the Exalted, are other than Allah?' We say: This is an error, for when we say 'Allah, the Exalted', we have denoted the essence [with] the attributes, not the essence alone; because the name 'Allah, the Exalted' is not true of an essence that is conceived to be devoid of the attributes of divinity; just as it is not said: 'Jurisprudence is other than the jurist', and 'Zayd's hand is other than Zayd', and 'the carpenter's hand is other than the carpenter'; because a part that enters into the name is not [??] not the same as what does not enter into the name... so Zayd's hand is neither Zayd nor other than Zayd, but rather [the phrase] 'both the essence and not other than it' is an absurd verbal expression. And thus every part: it is not other than the whole nor is it exactly the whole. So if it is said: 'Jurisprudence is other than the human', this is permissible, but it is not permissible to say: 'other than the jurist'; for 'human' does not denote the attribute of jurisprudence. Hence it is permissible to say: 'The attribute is other than the essence in which the attribute subsists', just as it is said: 'The accident subsisting in the substance is other than the substance', meaning that the concept of its name is other than the concept of the other's name. And this is permissible on two conditions: One: that the Law does not prohibit its usage, and this pertains specifically to Allah, the Exalted. The second: that otherness should not be understood as that which can exist without that to which it is other; for if that is understood, then it cannot be said 'Zayd's blackness is other than Zayd' because it does not exist without Zayd. Therefore, through this, what belongs to meaning and what belongs to wording have become clear, and there is no point in prolonging discussion on self-evident matters.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by an exposition of the proper understanding of 'otherness' concerning divine attributes. The objector asks whether Ghazali and the Ash'aris affirm that God's attributes are 'other than God.' Ghazali replies that this phrasing is incorrect. He explains that when we say 'Allah,' we denote the essence together with its attributes, not the essence in abstraction. The name 'Allah' cannot apply to an essence devoid of divine attributes. Then he draws analogies from everyday language: we do not say 'jurisprudence is other than the jurist' because the jurist is defined by jurisprudence; similarly, we do not say 'Zayd's hand is other than Zayd' because the hand is part of Zayd and not a separate entity. The relation of a part to the whole is neither identity nor complete otherness. Likewise, an attribute subsisting in an essence is neither the same as nor wholly other than that essence. So it is permissible to say 'the attribute is other than the essence' in the sense that the concept of the attribute (e.g., power) is distinct from the concept of the essence, just as an accident in a substance is conceptually different from the substance. However, two conditions apply when speaking of 'otherness' regarding God: first, Islamic law must not prohibit such language (and for God, the term 'other' might imply separation or multiplicity, which is why it is discouraged); second, the otherness must not imply that the attribute could exist independently of the essence, because that would be impossible (like blackness not existing without its substrate). Thus, the discussion clarifies the semantic and metaphysical nuances, and further elaboration on this obvious point is unnecessary.

Key Terms
صفات
غير
الذات
العرض
الجوهر
غيرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'attributes are other than the essence' implies a division or multiplicity in God.
Correction: Ghazali shows it only means conceptual distinction, not real separation, and it is subject to legal and metaphysical conditions.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the name 'Allah' denote?
A) The essence alone, free from attributes
B) The essence along with its attributes
C) The attributes alone
D) Neither the essence nor the attributes

Key Takeaway
Attributes are conceptually distinct from the essence, but such 'otherness' does not imply real separation and is subject to legal and metaphysical constraints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is the All-Knowing', do you mentally picture His knowledge as something added to Him, or as His very essence being knowledge? How does that mental picture affect your trust in His omniscience? Will you commit to correcting your mental image today?
?
This lesson shows that 'otherness' of attributes is only a conceptual distinction. Is there any attribute of Allah (e.g., His wrath, His mercy) that you secretly imagine as a separate 'thing' that could exist without Him? What does that reveal about your heart's comfort with His absolute oneness?
?
If the name 'Allah' includes all His attributes, then no aspect of God is independent of His essence. Are there parts of your life where you act as if God's mercy exists without His justice, or His power without His wisdom? How will you realign your understanding to reflect the truth of this lesson?

Attributes Subsist in God's Essence
Al-Ghazali asserts that all attributes subsist in God's essence and refutes Mu'tazila views on will and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ghazali asserts that all divine attributes subsist in God's essence and how he refutes the Mu'tazila positions on will and speech.

Core Idea
All divine attributes must subsist in God's essence; denying this leads to absurdities.

Al-Ghazali continues his exposition of the divine attributes with a second ruling: every attribute subsists in God's essence. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila, who, in their attempt to avoid attributing temporal events to God, end up positing will without a substrate and speech in an inanimate body. In this passage, he challenges the coherence of these alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the structure: a claim, two Mu'tazila positions, and a reference to a prior proof.
The passage ends mid-sentence; focus on the complete argument presented.
2
Identify the claim and contrasts
Identify Ghazali's claim that all attributes subsist in God's essence. Then note how he describes the Mu'tazila views on will and speech.
Pay attention to the reasoning: will cannot subsist elsewhere, speech cannot be in an inanimate body.
3
Reflect on the absurdities
Consider why Ghazali finds the Mu'tazila positions problematic: will without a substrate, speech in a non-speaking object.
This reinforces the necessity of attributes subsisting in the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الثاني للصفات : ندعي : أنَّ هذه الصفاتِ كلَّها قائمة بذاته ، لا يجوز أن يقوم شيءٌ منها بغير ذاته ؛ سواء كان في محل ، أو لم يكن في محل . وأما المعتزلة : فإنهم حكموا بأن الإرادة لا تقوم بذاته ؛ فإنها حادثة ، وليس هو محلاً للحوادث ، ولا تقوم بمحل آخر ؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى أن يكونَ ذلك المحل هو المريد بها ، فهي توجد لا في محل! وزعموا أن الكلامَ لا يقوم بذاته ؛ لأنه حادث ، ولكن يقوم بجسم هو جماد ؛ حتى لا يكون المتكلم به ، بل المتكلم به هو الله سبحانه . أما البرهان على أنَّ الصفاتِ ينبغي أن تقوم بالذات .. فهو عند من فهم برهان قيام الصفات بالذات ما قدمناه مستغنى عنه ؛ فإنَّ الدليل لمّا دلّ على وجود الصانع .. دل بعده على أن الصانع بصفة كذا ، ولا نعني بأنه تعالى على صفة كذا إلا أنه على
The second ruling on the attributes: We claim that all these attributes subsist in His essence; it is not permissible for any of them to subsist in other than His essence, whether it be in a substrate or not. As for the Mu'tazila, they ruled that the will does not subsist in His essence because it is temporal, and He is not a locus for temporal events, and it does not subsist in another substrate because that would lead to that substrate being the one who wills by it, so it exists without a substrate! And they claimed that speech does not subsist in His essence because it is temporal, but subsists in a body that is inanimate, so that He is not the speaker by it, rather the speaker by it is Allah, Exalted. As for the proof that the attributes should subsist in the essence, it is—for one who understands the proof of the subsistence of attributes in the essence that we have presented—dispensable; for when the evidence indicated the existence of the Maker, it afterwards indicated that the Maker is such-and-such by attribute, and we do not mean by 'He, Exalted, is such-and-such by attribute' except that He is...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the second ruling regarding the divine attributes: all attributes subsist in God's essence. Ghazali states categorically that it is impossible for any attribute to subsist in something other than His essence, whether that something be a substrate or not. He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position. According to the Mu'tazila, God's will does not subsist in His essence because it is temporal (hadith) and God cannot be a locus for temporal events. They also claim that it cannot subsist in another substrate because that would make that substrate the actual subject of will; thus they end up positing will existing without any substrate—a problematic conclusion. Similarly, they claim that God's speech does not subsist in His essence, but rather in a body (a particular created object) that is inanimate, so that God is not the speaker of that speech directly but only indirectly. Ghazali then says that the proof for attributes subsisting in the essence is already given and understood: when we prove the existence of the Creator, that same proof shows that He possesses attributes, and the meaning of 'He is such-and-such by attribute' is that the attribute subsists in Him. The passage ends incomplete, but the argument is clear: the Mu'tazila positions lead to absurdities (will without a substrate, speech in an inanimate object), while the Ash'ari position is consistent with the proof of God's existence and attributes.

Key Terms
قائمة بذاته
محل
إرادة
كلام
المعتزلة
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if attributes are temporal, they cannot be attributed to God, leading to denying they subsist in Him.
Correction: Ghazali argues that God's attributes are eternal, but the Mu'tazila incorrectly treat them as temporal and then face absurdities like will without a substrate.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the Mu'tazila position on God's will?
A) It subsists in God's essence
B) It exists without a substrate
C) It subsists in a created body
D) It does not exist at all

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali affirms that all attributes subsist in God's essence, and he refutes Mu'tazila alternatives as leading to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, Ghazali insists that all attributes subsist in God's essence. When you pray to God as 'The Merciful' or 'The All-Knowing,' do you truly believe these attributes are inseparable from Him, or do you sometimes imagine them as temporary moods? What would change in your worship if you fully embraced that His mercy is as eternal as He is? Will you consciously affirm in your next prayer that His attributes are part of His unchanging essence?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to protect God's transcendence by denying that temporal attributes could be in Him, but ended up with absurd positions. Are there areas of your faith where you rationalize away what seems uncomfortable about God (e.g., His wrath, His decree) to make Him more 'acceptable'? How can you submit to the full portrait of God as revealed, even when it challenges your sensibilities? Name one attribute you struggle with and commit to learning more about it today.
?
This passage shows that denying the subsistence of attributes leads to logical contradictions. Have you ever found yourself caught in an intellectual contradiction about God that you brushed aside? How might your relationship with God deepen if you resolved such contradictions by accepting the teachings of the scholars rather than your own reasoning? What step will you take this week to trust the scholars' understanding over your own?

The Second Ruling: Attributes Subsist in the Essence
Ghazali states the second ruling and presents the Mu'tazilite position on will and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second ruling that all divine attributes subsist in God's essence and the Mu'tazilite alternative regarding will and speech.

Core Idea
All attributes must subsist in God; no attribute can exist on its own.

Ghazali now turns to a crucial ruling: the attributes we affirmed earlier cannot exist independently—they must inhere in God's essence. But the Mu'tazilites disagreed, especially concerning will and speech. This passage states the claim and presents their opposing view, setting the stage for proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice how Ghazali states his claim directly and then presents the Mu'tazilite position.
2
Identify Ghazali's claim
Locate the sentence where Ghazali asserts that all attributes subsist in God's essence. Understand the phrase 'قائمة بذاته'.
This is the core positive doctrine.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite position
Read the part about will and speech. Note where each attribute subsists according to the Mu'tazila, and why they reject subsistence in God's essence.
The Mu'tazilite view is presented as a contrast to Ghazali's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الثاني للصفات :
ندعي : أنَّ هذه الصفاتِ كلَّها قائمة بذاته ، لا يجوز أن يقوم شيءٌ منها بغير ذاته ؛ سواء كان في محل ، أو لم يكن في محل .
وأما المعتزلة : فإنهم حكموا بأن الإرادة لا تقوم بذاته ؛ فإنها حادثة ، وليس هو محلاً للحوادث ، ولا تقوم بمحل آخر ؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى أن يكونَ ذلك المحل هو المريد بها ، فهي توجد لا في محل !
وزعموا أن الكلامَ لا يقوم بذاته ؛ لأنه حادث ، ولكن يقوم بجسم هو جماد ؛ حتى لا يكون المتكلم به ، بل المتكلم به هو الله سبحانه
The second ruling concerning the attributes: We maintain that all these attributes subsist in His essence. It is not permissible that any of them subsist in other than His essence, whether in a locus or not. As for the Mu'tazila, they ruled that the will does not subsist in His essence because it is originated, and He is not a locus for originated things, nor does it subsist in another locus because that would lead to that locus being the willing one by it. So it exists not in a locus. And they claimed that speech does not subsist in His essence because it is originated, but it subsists in a body that is inanimate, so that it is not the speaker by it; rather, the speaker by it is God, exalted.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines the second ruling (الحكم الثاني) regarding the divine attributes. Ghazali states the claim: all attributes subsist in God's essence (قائمة بذاته), and it is impermissible for any attribute to subsist in something else, whether that something is a locus or not. Then he contrasts this with the Mu'tazilite position: they hold that the will (الإرادة) cannot subsist in God because it is originated (حادثة) and God is not a locus for originated events; nor can it subsist in another locus because that would make that locus the willing subject. So they claim it exists without any locus. Similarly, they say speech (الكلام) does not subsist in God but in an inanimate body, so that body is not the speaker; rather, God is the speaker by that speech. This sets up the need to prove that attributes must subsist in the essence.

قائمة بذاته
محل
الإرادة
الكلام
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is merely stating a creed, but he is presenting a theological argument that will be proved.
Correction: Ghazali is setting up a claim that requires proof; the passage is part of argumentation, not just doxology.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as presented here, where does God's speech subsist?
A) In God's essence
B) In an inanimate body
C) In no locus at all
D) In the Preserved Tablet

Key Takeaway
Ghazali affirms that all attributes subsist in God's essence, while the Mu'tazila deny this for will and speech, claiming will has no locus and speech subsists in an inanimate body.

?

Proof That Attributes Must Subsist in the Essence
Ghazali proves that attributes must subsist in the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical proof that divine attributes must subsist in God's essence, and why denying this leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Describing God with an attribute is identical to that attribute subsisting in His essence.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents a clear rational argument for why attributes must subsist in God's essence. He shows that terms like 'knowing' and 'willing' are not empty labels; they necessarily refer to real qualities dwelling in the essence. Without subsistence, such descriptions would become meaningless, like calling something 'moving' without motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Note how Ghazali begins by referencing a prior proof and then makes his point about conceptual identity.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا فرق بين' (no difference between).
2
Identify the proof structure
Ghazali uses three examples: knowing (عالم), willing (مريد), and speaking (متكلم). For each, he states that the description and the attribute's subsistence are one concept. Trace this pattern.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Notice what happens if subsistence is denied: calling something 'moving' without motion is absurd. Apply this to will: if will doesn't subsist, saying 'willing' is meaningless. This shows the necessity of subsistence.
Ghazali says: 'فإن صدق على الله تعالى قولنا : لم يقم بذاته كلام .. صدق قولنا : ليس بمتكلم' – if speech doesn't subsist, He is not speaking.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof is foundational: it establishes that attributes are real and not merely names. This counters those who deny divine attributes.
This connects to the broader discussion of divine attributes in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما البرهان على أنَّ الصفاتِ ينبغي أن تقوم بالذات .. فهو عند من فهم برهان قيام الصفات بالذات ما قدمناه مستغنى عنه ؛ فإنَّ الدليل لمّا دلّ على وجود الصانع .. دل بعده على أن الصانع بصفة كذا ، ولا نعني بأنه تعالى على صفة كذا إلا أنه على تيك الصفة ، ولا فرق بين كونه على تيك الصفة وبين قيام الصفة بذاته .
وقد بينا أن مفهوم قولنا : عالمٌ ، وفي ذاته علم .. واحدٌ ؛ فمفهوم قولنا : مريد ، وقامت بذاته إرادة .. واحدٌ ، ومفهوم قولنا : لم تقم بذاته إرادة ، وليس بمريد .. واحدٌ ؛ فتسمية الذاتِ مريداً بإرادة لم تقم به كتسميته متحركاً بحركة لم تقم به ، وإذا لم تقم الإرادة به .. فسواء كانت موجودة أو معدومة .
فقول القائل : إنه مريد .. لفظ خطاً لا معنى له ، وهكذا المتكلم ، فإنه متكلم باعتبار كونه محلاً للكلام ؛ إذ لا فرق بين قولنا : هو متكلم ، وبين قولنا : قام الكلام به ، ولا فرق بين قولنا : ليس بمتكلم ، وبين قولنا : لم يقم بذاته كلامٌ ، كما في كونه مصوتاً ومتحركاً ، فإن صدق على الله تعالى قولنا : لم يقم بذاته كلام .. صدق قولنا : ليس بمتكلم ؛ لأنهما عبارتان عن معنى واحد .
As for the proof that the attributes must subsist in the essence, for one who understands the proof of the subsistence of attributes in the essence it is rendered superfluous by what we have already presented. For when the proof indicated the existence of the Maker, it thereafter indicated that the Maker is of such a description, and we mean by 'He, exalted, is of such a description' nothing other than that He is upon that attribute, and there is no difference between His being upon that attribute and the subsistence of the attribute in His essence. And we have already explained that the concept of our saying 'knowing' and 'in His essence knowledge' is one. So the concept of our saying 'willing' and 'will subsisted in His essence' is one, and the concept of our saying 'will did not subsist in His essence' and 'He is not willing' is one. So naming the essence 'willing' by a will that does not subsist in it is like naming it 'moving' by a motion that does not subsist in it. And if the will does not subsist in Him, then it is the same whether it exists or is non-existent. So the statement of one who says 'He is willing' is a word in vain with no meaning. And likewise for the speaker (al-mutakallim), for He is a speaker by virtue of being a locus for speech; for there is no difference between our saying 'He is a speaker' and our saying 'speech subsisted in Him', and no difference between our saying 'He is not a speaker' and our saying 'speech did not subsist in His essence', just as in His being a sound-producer (musawwit) and moving. For if our saying 'speech did not subsist in His essence' is true of God, then our saying 'He is not a speaker' is true, because they are two expressions for a single meaning.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage provides the rational proof (البرهان) that attributes must subsist in God's essence. Ghazali begins by noting that for one who already understands the proof, the current discussion is superfluous, but he presents it for clarity. The proof proceeds from the fact that our knowledge of God's existence leads to His description with attributes. The meaning of saying 'God is knowing' is precisely that knowledge subsists in His essence. There is no difference between the two statements. Similarly, 'willing' means will subsists in His essence. If one denies that the will subsists in Him, then calling Him 'willing' becomes meaningless, just as calling something 'moving' without motion subsisting in it is absurd. He extends this to speech: 'speaker' means speech subsists in Him. If speech does not subsist in Him, then He is not a speaker. The proof shows that the attribution of a description to God necessarily implies that the description (attribute) subsists in Him.

Key Terms
البرهان
قيام الصفات بالذات
مفهوم
عالم
مريد
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali is merely rephrasing concepts, not proving a necessary relationship.
Correction: Ghazali is demonstrating that the attribute's subsistence is logically entailed by the description; without it the description is meaningless.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about the relationship between 'God is willing' and 'will subsists in His essence'?
A) They are two different things
B) They are the same concept
C) The first implies the second
D) The second is more fundamental

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that describing God with an attribute necessarily implies that attribute subsists in His essence; otherwise the description is meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you say 'God is Knowing,' do you truly affirm that knowledge subsists in His essence, or do you treat it as a mere label? If Ghazali's argument shows that denying subsistence makes the description meaningless, what does that mean for how you speak of God every day?
?
Ghazali compares calling God 'willing' without an attribute of will to calling something 'moving' without motion. Is there any area of your faith where you describe God without fully accepting the reality of His attributes? What would it cost you to take that reality seriously?
?
The proof demands that 'He is not a speaker' follows from 'speech did not subsist in His essence.' When you listen to the Qur'an, do you recognize it as God's speech subsisting in His essence, or do you unconsciously detach it from Him? What will you do differently the next time you recite or hear the Qur'an?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite View on Will
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that will exists without a locus and explains their reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the Mu'tazilite position that divine will exists without a locus, and the reasoning behind their error.

Core Idea
Will cannot exist without a locus; Mu'tazila contradicted themselves.

In this passage, Imam Ghazali tackles a problematic Mu'tazilite claim: that God's will exists without a locus. He exposes the inconsistency of this view by comparing it to other attributes and sounds, and then reveals the underlying reason for their error—their refusal to admit originated accidents in God's essence. Let's read the passage and see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice that it has two clear parts: first a reductio ad absurdum, then an explanation of the cause.
The first phrase may be corrupted; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim and Ghazali's objection
The Mu'tazila say the will exists without a locus. Ghazali asks: if that is allowed, why not speech? Then extends to knowledge, power, blackness, motion.
He uses a 'slippery slope' argument.
3
Compare with sound and other attributes
Ghazali points out that the Mu'tazila do require a locus for sound (a similar accident). He asks why they don't apply the same logic consistently.
Sound is an accident like will; consistency demands the same rule.
4
Understand the reason for their error
Ghazali explains: since the first creation requires will, and any locus for will would itself be created, the only possible prior locus is God's essence. But they refuse to make God a locus for originated things. Hence they concluded will exists without a locus.
This reveals the underlying theological constraint driving their view.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: does Ghazali's reductio show that the Mu'tazilite position is impossible? What must be the correct view?
The correct view is that will subsists in God's essence as an eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كانت الإرادة في ثم العجب من قولهم : إن الإرادة توجد لا في محل! فإنْ جازَ وجود غير محل .. فلم لا يكون الكلام كذلك ؟ صفة من الصفات لا في محل.. فليجز وجود العلم والقدرة والسواد والحركة بل الكلام لا في محل ، فلِمَ قالوا بخلق الأصوات في محل ؟ فليخلق في غير محل ! وإن لم يعقل الصوتُ إلا في محل ؛ لأنه عرض وصفة.. فكذا الإرادة ، ولو عكس هذا وقيل : إنه خلق كلاماً لا في محل ، وخلق إرادة في محل .. لكان العكس كالطرد .
سبب قول المعتزلة : ولكن لما كان أول المخلوقات يحتاج إلى الإرادة والمحل مخلوق .. لم يمكنهم تقديرُ محل للإرادة موجود قبل الإرادة ؛ فإنه لا محل قبل الإرادة إلا ذاتُ الله تعالى ، ولم يجعلوه محلاً للحوادث
If the will is in [then?] the wonder from their statement that the will exists not in a locus! If existence without a locus is permissible, then why is not speech likewise? An attribute from among the attributes not in a locus... then let the existence of knowledge, power, blackness, and motion, nay speech, be permissible not in a locus. Why did they say that sounds are created in a locus? Let them be created without a locus! And if sound is only conceivable in a locus because it is an accident and attribute, then likewise the will. And if this were reversed and it were said: He created speech not in a locus and created will in a locus, then the reversal would be as the direct. The reason for the Mu'tazila's statement: But since the first of the created things requires will and the locus is created, they could not estimate a locus for the will existing before the will; for there is no locus before the will except the essence of God, and they did not make it a locus for originated things.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage contains two parts: first, a reductio ad absurdum of the Mu'tazilite position that will exists without a locus; second, an explanation of the cause of their error. Ghazali asks: If it is permissible for the will to exist without a locus, why should speech not be similarly without a locus? In fact, why not all attributes like knowledge, power, even blackness and motion? He points out that they do not allow sound to exist without a locus, but sound is an accident/attribute like will. So consistency demands that if will can be without a locus, then sound and other attributes can be too. But they contradict themselves by requiring a locus for sound. Then he explains the reason for their position: since the first created thing requires will, and any locus for will would itself be created, the only possible locus before the will is God's essence. But they refuse to make God a locus for originated things. Therefore, they concluded that will exists not in a locus. Ghazali implies this reasoning is flawed because it forces an impossibility—either will exists without a locus (contrary to its nature as an accident) or it would have to subsist in God, which they reject. So they end up with an untenable position.

Key Terms
لا في محل
الإرادة
الصوت
العرض
المخلوقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will can exist without a locus, i.e., not as an attribute subsisting in a subject.
Correction: According to Ghazali, the will, like other accidents/attributes, requires a locus. The Mu'tazilite view leads to contradictions and was motivated by a desire to avoid originated accidents in God.

Checkpoint
What is the reason Ghazali gives for why the Mu'tazila said the will exists without a locus?
A) They believed attributes cannot be accidents
B) They thought will is not an attribute
C) They could not find a locus for will before the first creation except God, whom they denied as locus for originated things
D) They followed earlier philosophers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite attempt to avoid originated accidents in God forced them into an untenable position; will must have a locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Inseparability of Attributes from the Essence: Will and Speech
Ghazali argues that calling God 'willing' without a will subsisting in Him is meaningless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that attributes like will and speech must subsist in God's essence for the descriptions 'willing' and 'speaker' to be meaningful.

Core Idea
Without subsistence in the essence, attribute terms are empty words.

Ghazali presents a logical argument that attributes cannot be mere names; they must be realities subsisting in the essence. This passage uses the examples of will and speech to show that being 'willing' or 'speaker' is identical to having will or speech in the essence. Without subsistence, the terms become meaningless, like calling something 'moving' without movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice how Ghazali uses logical equivalences ('there is no difference between').
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key equivalences
Look for the statements equating 'willing' with 'will subsists' and 'speaker' with 'speech subsists'. Also note the negation equivalences.
These are the core of the argument.
3
Understand the analogy and implication
Consider the analogy of 'moving' without movement. If will does not subsist, the term 'willing' is as meaningless as 'moving' without movement. Apply the same to speech.
This shows why subsistence is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك الصفة، ولا فرق بين كونه على تلك الصفة وبين قيام الصفة بذاته وقد بينا أن مفهوم قولنا : عالمٌ، وفي ذاته علم .. واحد؛ فمفهوم قولنا : مريد، وقامت بذاته إرادة .. واحدٌ، ومفهوم قولنا : لم تقم بذاته إرادة، وليس بمريد .. واحدٌ؛ فتسمية الذاتِ مريداً بإرادة لم تقم به كتسميته متحركاً بحركة لم تقم به، وإذا لم تقم الإرادة به .. فسواء كانت موجودة أو معدومة. فقول القائل : إنه مريد .. لفظ خطاً لا معنى له، وهكذا المتكلم، فإنه متكلم باعتبار كونه محلاً للكلام؛ إذ لا فرق بين قولنا : هو متكلم، وبين قولنا : قام الكلام به، ولا فرق بين قولنا : ليس بمتكلم، وبين قولنا : لم يقم بذاته كلامٌ، كما في كونه مصوتاً ومتحركاً، فإن صدق على الله تعالى قولنا : لم يقم بذاته كلام .. صدق قولنا : ليس بمتكلم؛ لأنهما عبارتان عن معنى واحد.
That attribute, and there is no difference between His being upon that attribute and the attribute subsisting in His essence. And we have shown that the concept of our saying 'knowing' and 'in His essence is knowledge' is one; so the concept of our saying 'willing' and 'will subsisted in His essence' is one; and the concept of our saying 'no will subsisted in His essence' and 'He is not willing' is one. So calling the essence 'willing' by a will that does not subsist in it is like calling it 'moving' by a movement that does not subsist in it. And if will does not subsist in Him, then it is the same whether it exists or is non-existent. So the statement of one who says 'He is willing' is an erroneous word with no meaning. And likewise the 'speaker', for He is a speaker by virtue of being a locus for speech; for there is no difference between our saying 'He is a speaker' and our saying 'speech subsisted in Him', and no difference between our saying 'He is not a speaker' and our saying 'no speech subsisted in His essence', just as in His being sound-producing and moving. For if our statement 'no speech subsisted in His essence' is true of God Almighty, then our statement 'He is not a speaker' is true; because they are two expressions for one meaning.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a definition-like argument establishing that attributes such as will and speech must subsist in the essence for the attribute term to be meaningful. Ghazali begins by stating that there is no difference between being described with a certain quality and that quality subsisting in the essence. He makes an analogy: just as 'knowing' is identical to 'having knowledge in the essence', so is 'willing' identical to 'having will in the essence'. Therefore, calling the essence 'willing' by virtue of a will that does not subsist in it is like calling it 'moving' by a movement that does not subsist in it. If the will does not subsist, then whether it exists or not makes no difference; the statement is an empty word. The same applies to speech: being 'speaking' means that speech subsists in the essence. If it is true that no speech subsists in God, then He is not 'speaking', because both phrases express the same meaning.

Key Terms

كلام
محل
صفة
ذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God 'willing' or 'speaker' does not require a real attribute subsisting in Him; these can be mere names.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that such attribute terms are logically equivalent to the subsistence of the attribute in the essence. Without subsistence, the terms are meaningless—like calling something 'moving' without movement.

Key Takeaway
Attributes like will and speech must subsist in the essence for the terms to have real meaning.

The Absurdity of the Mu'tazilite Claim on Will Without a Locus
Ghazali uses analogy to refute the Mu'tazilite claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that will can exist without a locus, using an analogy with speech and other attributes.

Core Idea
If one attribute can exist without a locus, then all can—but that leads to absurdity.

Ghazali begins his refutation by pointing out the inconsistency in the Mu'tazilite position. They accept that speech cannot exist without a locus, yet they claim will can. By drawing an analogy, he exposes the logical flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites claim that will (irāda) can exist without a locus (maḥall). Ghazali finds this astonishing and immediately challenges it.
Notice the word 'ajab (wonder) indicating how self-evidently absurd this is to Ghazali.
2
Follow the analogy with speech and other attributes
Ghazali argues that if existence without a locus is possible, then the same should apply to speech, knowledge, power, blackness, and movement. But the Mu'tazilites do not accept that speech can exist without a locus—they say it is created sounds in a locus.
The analogy forces consistency: either all attributes can exist without a locus, or none can.
3
Understand the conclusion: the reversal is equally absurd
Ghazali points out that if one were to reverse the claim (speech without locus, will in a locus), it would be just as absurd. This shows the Mu'tazilite position is arbitrary and logically flawed.
The phrase 'al-aksu kal-ṭard' means the reversal is equally nonsensical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كانت الإرادة في ثم العجب من قولهم : إن الإرادة توجد لا في محل! فإنْ جازَ وجود غير محل .. فلم لا يكون الكلام كذلك ؟ صفة من الصفات لا في محل.. فليجز وجود العلم والقدرة والسواد والحركة بل الكلام لا في محل، فلِمَ قالوا بخلق الأصوات في محل ؟ فليخلق في غير محل ! وإن لم يعقل الصوتُ إلا في محل؛ لأنه عرض وصفة.. فكذا الإرادة، ولو عكس هذا وقيل : إنه خلق كلاماً لا في محل، وخلق إرادة في محل .. لكان العكس كالطرد . سبب قول المعتزلة : ولكن لما كان أول المخلوقات يحتاج إلى الإرادة والمحل مخلوق .. لم يمكنهم تقديرُ محل للإرادة موجود قبل الإرادة؛ فإنه لا محل قبل الإرادة إلا ذاتُ الله تعالى، ولم يجعلوه محلاً للحوادث، ومن جعله محلاً للحوادث أقربُ حالاً منهم؛ فإن استحالة وجودِ إرادة في غير محل، واستحالة كونه مريداً بإرادة لا تقوم به، واستحالة حدوثِ إرادة حادثة بلا إرادة .. تدرك ببديهة العقل أو نظره الجليّ، فهذه ثلاث استحالات جلية، وأما استحاله كونه محلاً للحوادث.. فلا يدرك إلا بنظر دقيق كما سنذكره.
If the will [can exist without a locus], then there is wonder at their statement that will exists without a locus! For if existence without a locus is possible, then why is speech not likewise? An attribute among attributes without a locus—so let the existence of knowledge, power, blackness, movement, even speech, be allowed without a locus. Why did they say that sounds are created in a locus? Let them be created without a locus! And if sound is only conceivable in a locus because it is an accident and attribute, then similarly will [requires a locus]. And if this were reversed and it were said: He created speech without a locus and created will in a locus, then the reversal would be equally absurd. The reason for the Mu'tazilite statement: but because the first of created things needs will, and the locus is created, they were unable to posit a locus for will existing before will; for there is no locus before will except the essence of God Almighty, and they did not make it a locus for originated things. And whoever makes it a locus for originated things is closer to the truth than them; for the impossibility of the existence of will without a locus, and the impossibility of His being willing by a will not subsisting in Him, and the impossibility of the occurrence of an originated will without a will—these are perceived by the intuition of reason or by clear speculation. So these are three clear impossibilities. As for the impossibility of His being a locus for originated things, it is not perceived except by subtle speculation, as we will mention later.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will can exist without a locus. Ghazali first presents the Mu'tazilite objection: they are amazed that will could be said to exist without a locus. He argues by analogy: if it is possible for an attribute to exist without a locus, then why not allow speech, knowledge, power, blackness, and movement to exist without a locus? But the Mu'tazila do not accept that speech can exist without a locus (they say speech is created sounds in a locus), so they should apply the same rule to will; if they reverse the positions, the result would be equally absurd. Then Ghazali explains the reason for the Mu'tazilite stance: since the first created thing requires will, and the locus (the world) is created, they could not posit a locus for will before creation except God's essence, but they refuse to make God a locus for accidents. However, whoever does make God a locus for accidents is closer to the truth, because the impossibility of will in no locus, of being willing without a will, and of an originated will coming into being without a will, are evident to basic reason or clear speculation. In contrast, the impossibility of God being a locus for accidents requires subtle speculation, as will be mentioned later.

Key Terms
إرادة
محل
معتزلة
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite position that will can exist without a locus is logically consistent.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the same logic would allow all attributes to exist without a locus, which the Mu'tazilites themselves reject, so their position is inconsistent and absurd.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali use to demonstrate the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite claim?
A) A proof from scripture
B) An analogy with speech and other attributes
C) A historical argument
D) A consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that will can exist without a locus is absurd because it would logically require all attributes to do so, which the Mu'tazilites themselves reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold about God that you have never examined for logical consistency, even when it conflicts with other beliefs you hold? Will you commit to honestly questioning that belief today?
?
When someone points out an inconsistency in your understanding of faith, do you usually defend your position or humbly reconsider? How might the Mu'tazilites' refusal to apply their own logic to all attributes mirror your own responses?
?
This lesson reveals that a single arbitrary exception can unravel an entire theological system. Is there an area of your life—not just theology—where you are making an exception for yourself that you wouldn't grant to others? What would it cost you to be consistent?

Three Clear Impossibilities and One Subtle Speculation
Ghazali enumerates three clear impossibilities and one subtle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three self-evident impossibilities that Ghazali presents against the Mu'tazilite view, and why the fourth requires subtle speculation.

Core Idea
Three impossibilities are clear to reason; one requires deeper thought.

After showing the absurdity of the analogy, Ghazali explains the Mu'tazilite motivation for rejecting a locus for will: they cannot posit a locus before creation except God's essence, which they deny as a locus for accidents. Then he lists three rationally impossible consequences of their view, contrasting them with the subtle issue of God being a locus for originated things.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite reason for rejecting a locus
The first created thing needs will, but the locus (the world) is created, so they cannot posit a locus before will except God's essence. Since they deny that God can be a locus for accidents, they claim will exists without a locus.
This explains their motivation but does not justify the claim.
2
Identify the three clear impossibilities
Ghazali states that three things are impossible and known by intuition or clear reason: (1) will existing without a locus, (2) God being willing by a will not subsisting in Him, (3) an originated will coming into being without a preceding will.
These are presented as self-evident ('badīhat al-'aql').
3
Note the subtle impossibility that requires further inquiry
The fourth point—that God cannot be a locus for originated things—is not self-evident; it requires subtle speculation (naẓar daqīq), which Ghazali promises to address later.
This sets up a later discussion, showing Ghazali's systematic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كانت الإرادة في ثم العجب من قولهم : إن الإرادة توجد لا في محل! فإنْ جازَ وجود غير محل .. فلم لا يكون الكلام كذلك ؟ صفة من الصفات لا في محل.. فليجز وجود العلم والقدرة والسواد والحركة بل الكلام لا في محل، فلِمَ قالوا بخلق الأصوات في محل ؟ فليخلق في غير محل ! وإن لم يعقل الصوتُ إلا في محل؛ لأنه عرض وصفة.. فكذا الإرادة، ولو عكس هذا وقيل : إنه خلق كلاماً لا في محل، وخلق إرادة في محل .. لكان العكس كالطرد . سبب قول المعتزلة : ولكن لما كان أول المخلوقات يحتاج إلى الإرادة والمحل مخلوق .. لم يمكنهم تقديرُ محل للإرادة موجود قبل الإرادة؛ فإنه لا محل قبل الإرادة إلا ذاتُ الله تعالى، ولم يجعلوه محلاً للحوادث، ومن جعله محلاً للحوادث أقربُ حالاً منهم؛ فإن استحالة وجودِ إرادة في غير محل، واستحالة كونه مريداً بإرادة لا تقوم به، واستحالة حدوثِ إرادة حادثة بلا إرادة .. تدرك ببديهة العقل أو نظره الجليّ، فهذه ثلاث استحالات جلية، وأما استحاله كونه محلاً للحوادث.. فلا يدرك إلا بنظر دقيق كما سنذكره.

So these are three clear impossibilities. As for the impossibility of His being a locus for originated things, it is not perceived except by subtle speculation, as we will mention later.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a theological belief, do you assume it is obviously rational? Ghazali shows that some truths require subtle speculation. Are you humble enough to admit that your convictions might need deeper examination? Will you commit to seeking that depth before dismissing opposing views?
?
The Mu'tazilites were so attached to their principle that they ended up with clear impossibilities. Is there any belief you cling to that, on closer inspection, leads to a contradiction about Allah's attributes? Will you let go of that attachment and submit to the truth, even if it challenges your intellectual comfort?

Divine Attributes

The Eternity of All Divine Attributes
Ghazali states the eternity of all attributes and the impossibility of origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali asserts that all divine attributes are eternal and why their origination is impossible.

Core Idea
God's attributes must be eternal like His essence, or He would be a locus for change.

Ghazali begins the third ruling by affirming that all attributes are eternal. He argues that if any attribute were originated, it would either not subsist in God or make Him a locus for change, both impossible. He notes that even opponents agree on life and power; the dispute concerns knowledge, will, and speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the ruling to the two hypothetical scenarios.
2
Understand the two impossible scenarios
If an attribute were originated, either (1) it would not subsist in God, which is impossible because an attribute must inhere in a subject, or (2) God would be a locus for originated things, implying change in God. Both are impossible.
Ghazali says the first scenario is 'more evidently impossible' as previously discussed.
3
Note the scope of disagreement
No one disputes the eternity of life and power. The disagreement is about knowledge of particulars, will, and speech. This sets the stage for the proofs to come.
This clarifies that the debate is limited to certain attributes.
4
Reflect on why origination is impossible
Consider why change in God is impossible. This connects to the earlier lesson on God not being a locus for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الثالث : كل صفاته سبحانه قديمة كذاته أن الصفاتِ كلَّها قديمةٌ؛ فإنها لو كانت حادثة .. لكان القديم تعالى محلاً للحوادث، وهو محال، أو كان يتصف بصفة لا تقوم به، وذلك أظهر استحالة كما سبق، ولم يذهب أحدٌ إلى حدوث الحياة والقدرة، وإنما اعتقدوا ذلك في العلم بالحوادث وفي الإرادة وفي الكلام، ونحن نستدل ثلاثة دلائل على قدمها واستحالة كونه سبحانه محلاً للحوادث من ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن كل حادث فهو جائز الوجود، والقديم الأزلي واجب، والحادث جائز، وصفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود، ولو تطرَّقَ الجواز إلى صفاته .. لكان ذلك مناقضاً لوجوب وجوده؛ فإنَّ الجواز والوجوب يتناقضان، فكلُّ ما هو واجبُ الذات فمن المحال أن يكون جائز الصفات، وهذا واضح بنفسه.
The third ruling: All His attributes, exalted is He, are eternal like His essence. [That means] all the attributes are eternal; for if they were originated, then the Eternal Almighty would be a locus for originated things, which is impossible, or He would be described by an attribute not subsisting in Him, and that is a more evident impossibility as previously mentioned. And no one has held the origination of life and power; they only held that concerning knowledge of particulars, will, and speech. And we adduce three proofs for their eternity and the impossibility of His being, exalted is He, a locus for originated things in three respects: The first: that every originated thing is possible of existence, while the pre-eternal ancient is necessary, and the originated is possible, and His attributes are necessary of existence. If possibility were to enter upon His attributes, that would contradict the necessity of His existence; for possibility and necessity are contradictory. So everything whose essence is necessary is impossible to have possible attributes, and this is clear in itself.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the third ruling: all attributes are eternal like the essence. The proof: if they were originated, then either (1) they would not subsist in the essence (which is impossible as previously discussed) or (2) the eternal would become a locus for originated things, which is impossible. No one claims origination of life and power; the dispute is about knowledge of particulars, will, and speech. Ghazali then says he will present three proofs for the eternity of attributes and the impossibility of God being a locus for accidents. The first proof: every originated thing is possible, while the eternal is necessary. The attributes of the necessary are also necessary. If possibility (contingency) applied to His attributes, it would contradict the necessity of His existence. The contrast between possibility and necessity is clear: whatever has necessary essence cannot have contingent attributes.

Key Terms
صفات
قديم
حادث
محل للحوادث
حياة
قدرة
علم
إرادة
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some thinkers hold that certain attributes like will and speech are created in time.
Correction: Ghazali argues that all attributes are eternal; even will and speech are not originated. The impossibility of origination applies to all attributes uniformly.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say would happen if an attribute were originated?
A) It would be separate from God and thus impossible.
B) God would become a locus for originated things or the attribute would not subsist in Him.
C) The attribute would be like a human attribute.
D) Only knowledge of particulars would be affected.

Key Takeaway
All divine attributes are eternal because their origination would entail impossibilities related to God's nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every one of God's attributes—including His mercy, justice, and knowledge of your sins—is eternal and unchanging, how would your fear of His justice change? Are you living as though God's attributes could change based on your actions?
?
The passage argues that an originated attribute in God would make Him a 'locus for change.' Have you ever subconsciously thought of God as 'reacting' to your prayers or deeds? How can you train yourself to see His attributes as fixed and perfect, not dependent on you?
?
If God's will is eternal, then everything He wills was willed from eternity. Does this truth bring you comfort or terror? How should it affect your acceptance of His decree in your life right now?

First Proof: Necessity and Possibility
Ghazali gives the first proof from necessity vs possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first proof for the eternity of attributes, based on the contradiction between necessary existence and possible attributes.

Core Idea
If attributes were possible, they would contradict God's necessary existence.

Ghazali presents the first of three proofs for the eternity of attributes. He uses the fundamental distinction between necessary existence (wajib al-wujud) and possible existence (ja'iz al-wujud). Every originated thing is possible, while the pre-eternal is necessary. Since God's attributes are inseparable from His essence, they too must be necessary, not possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical structure: premises and conclusion.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify 'ja'iz al-wujud' (possible existence) and 'wajib al-wujud' (necessary existence). Understand that 'hadith' (originated) implies possibility.
The pre-eternal (qadim azali) is necessary; the originated is possible.
3
Follow the argument
Premise 1: Every originated thing is possible. Premise 2: The pre-eternal is necessary. Premise 3: Attributes of the necessary are also necessary. Conclusion: If attributes were possible, that would contradict necessity.
The contradiction is that possibility and necessity cannot coexist in the same subject.
4
Reflect on the clarity of the proof
Ghazali says this is clear in itself. Why? Because the essence is necessary, so its attributes cannot be contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الثالث : كل صفاته سبحانه قديمة كذاته أن الصفاتِ كلَّها قديمةٌ؛ فإنها لو كانت حادثة .. لكان القديم تعالى محلاً للحوادث، وهو محال، أو كان يتصف بصفة لا تقوم به، وذلك أظهر استحالة كما سبق، ولم يذهب أحدٌ إلى حدوث الحياة والقدرة، وإنما اعتقدوا ذلك في العلم بالحوادث وفي الإرادة وفي الكلام، ونحن نستدل ثلاثة دلائل على قدمها واستحالة كونه سبحانه محلاً للحوادث من ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن كل حادث فهو جائز الوجود، والقديم الأزلي واجب، والحادث جائز، وصفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود، ولو تطرَّقَ الجواز إلى صفاته .. لكان ذلك مناقضاً لوجوب وجوده؛ فإنَّ الجواز والوجوب يتناقضان، فكلُّ ما هو واجبُ الذات فمن المحال أن يكون جائز الصفات، وهذا واضح بنفسه.
The third ruling: All His attributes, exalted is He, are eternal like His essence. [That means] all the attributes are eternal; for if they were originated, then the Eternal Almighty would be a locus for originated things, which is impossible, or He would be described by an attribute not subsisting in Him, and that is a more evident impossibility as previously mentioned. And no one has held the origination of life and power; they only held that concerning knowledge of particulars, will, and speech. If possibility were to enter upon His attributes, that would contradict the necessity of His existence; for possibility and necessity are contradictory. So everything whose essence is necessary is impossible to have possible attributes, and this is clear in itself.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the third ruling: all attributes are eternal like the essence. The proof: if they were originated, then either (1) they would not subsist in the essence (which is impossible as previously discussed) or (2) the eternal would become a locus for originated things, which is impossible. No one claims origination of life and power; the dispute is about knowledge of particulars, will, and speech. Ghazali then says he will present three proofs for the eternity of attributes and the impossibility of God being a locus for accidents. The first proof: every originated thing is possible, while the eternal is necessary. The attributes of the necessary are also necessary. If possibility (contingency) applied to His attributes, it would contradict the necessity of His existence. The contrast between possibility and necessity is clear: whatever has necessary essence cannot have contingent attributes.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributes are possible in themselves and only necessary because of their connection to the essence.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that attributes of the necessary are themselves necessary; they are not merely derived necessity. The possibility of attributes would directly contradict the necessity of the essence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's first proof, what would happen if possibility entered upon God's attributes?
A) It would show that God is powerful.
B) It would contradict the necessity of His existence.
C) It would mean the attributes are originated.
D) It would be acceptable as long as the essence is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that because God's essence is necessary, His attributes must also be necessary and eternal; contingency in attributes is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every attribute of God is necessary and eternal, unchanging and unfailing, how does that confront the moments when you feel God is distant or uncaring? What would it mean to live as if His mercy and justice are as necessary as His existence?
?
Consider a time when you doubted a specific attribute of God—perhaps His knowledge of your situation or His power to change it. How does this proof that possibility cannot enter His attributes challenge that doubt? What is it asking you to trust right now?
?
If God's attributes are necessary and therefore cannot be added to or taken away, then every prayer you offer is directed to a being whose attributes are fully and eternally active. Does that reality change how you approach your next prayer? What must you let go of to truly rely on that unchanging nature?

The Second Proof: Impossibility of Infinite Regression of Accidents
Ghazali presents a dilemma between a first accident and infinite regression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second proof that God cannot be a locus for accidents, based on the impossibility of an infinite regression of originated events.

Core Idea
Infinite regression of accidents is impossible, so God cannot house them.

This passage introduces Ghazali's second and stronger proof against the possibility of accidents subsisting in the divine essence. He presents a logical dilemma: either there is a first accident with no prior cause, or there is an infinite chain of accidents—both are impossible. This argument solidifies the divine transcendence over temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali begins by calling this the second and stronger proof.
Focus on the two possibilities he presents.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents a dilemma: either the imagination ascends to an accident with no predecessor, or it never reaches such a limit. Identify these two branches.
These correspond to a first accident or infinite regression.
3
Understand why each is problematic
Consider why each option is impossible: a first accident would imply an uncaused beginning, and infinite regression is absurd. Think about how this supports Ghazali's conclusion.
The proof assumes that both options are untenable.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since both possibilities are impossible, the initial assumption—that an accident subsists in God—must be false. This is the force of the proof.
This argument is stronger than the first because it directly targets the possibility of any accident in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - وهو الأقوى - : أنه لو قُدرَ حلول حادث بذاته.. لكان لا يخلو : إما أن يرتقي الوهم إلى حادث يستحيل قبله حادث، أو لا يرتقي إليه، بل كل حادث فيجوز أن يكون قبلَهُ حادث.
The second — and it is the strongest — is that if the indwelling of an originated thing in His essence were supposed, it would be unavoidable: either the imagination would ascend to an originated thing before which an originated thing is impossible, or it would not ascend to it, rather every originated thing may have an originated thing before it.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage begins the second proof, which Ghazali says is the stronger. He argues: if it were supposed that an originated thing subsists in the essence, then the imagination would either reach an accident before which there is no accident (i.e., a first accident), or it would not reach such a point, meaning every accident has another accident before it (infinite regression). The proof is incomplete in the text, but the two options are: a first accident with no prior cause, or an infinite chain of accidents. Both are impossible, so God cannot be a locus for accidents.

Key Terms

ترقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regression of accidents is logically possible.
Correction: Ghazali argues that infinite regression is impossible, making God's essence free from accidents.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities Ghazali presents in his second proof?
A) Either a first accident with no prior, or an infinite regression.
B) Either accidents are eternal or created.
C) Either will is eternal or created.
D) Either attributes are separate from essence or not.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second proof shows that God cannot be a locus for accidents because doing so would lead to an impossible infinite regression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not subject to accidents or temporal change, then your actions do not cause a change in His essence. How does this truth challenge your ego — especially the part of you that thinks your obedience 'adds' something to God or that your disobedience 'diminishes' Him? What would it mean for you to worship without any expectation that God is affected by your worship?
?
Ghazali says that assuming accidents in God leads to an infinite regression that is impossible. But in your daily life, you often treat God as if He is in time — reacting to your prayers, changing His decree based on your deeds. When will you truly allow yourself to believe that God is beyond time and that your relationship with Him is not transactional but based on His eternal, unchanging will?
?
If infinite regression of accidents is impossible, then there must be a first uncaused cause — God. Yet you live as if the causes in your life are infinite and beyond God's control. What is one specific worry or fear that you are currently handling as if it has no ultimate cause? How will you consciously attribute it to God as the sole first cause starting today?
