The Third Ruling: All Divine Attributes Are Eternal – First Proof
Ghazali states that all attributes are eternal and gives the first proof based on necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first proof for the eternity of divine attributes, based on the impossibility of contingency in God's attributes.

Core Idea
God's attributes are as necessary as His essence; contingency would contradict His necessity.

This lesson establishes Ghazali's third ruling: that all of God's attributes are eternal like His essence. Ghazali provides a first proof from the concept of necessity and contingency. He argues that since God's existence is necessary, His attributes cannot be contingent, as that would contradict His very nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the ruling
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the third ruling: all of God's attributes are eternal like His essence. Notice how Ghazali begins with this definitive statement.
The passage starts with 'الحكم الثالث' – this is the third ruling in a series.
2
Understand the initial argument
Ghazali argues that if attributes were temporal, it would lead to an impossibility: either God becomes a locus of temporal events (which is impossible) or He is described by an attribute not subsisting in Him (even more impossible). Reflect on why these are unacceptable.
This argument sets up the need for proofs.
3
Focus on the first proof
Now examine the first proof: every temporal thing is contingent (jā'iz al-wujūd), while the eternal is necessary (wājib al-wujūd). Since God's essence is necessary, His attributes must also be necessary. If contingency applied to His attributes, it would contradict the necessity of His existence because necessity and contingency are contradictory.
Ghazali considers this proof self-evident ('واضح بنفسه').
4
Reflect on the scope of the dispute
Note Ghazali's observation: no one claims that life and power are temporal; the dispute is only over knowledge of temporal events, will, and speech. This helps to understand the specific focus of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الثالث : كل صفاته سبحانه قديمة كذاته، أن الصفات كلها قديمة ; فإنها لو كانت حادثة .. لكان القديم تعالى محلاً للحوادث ، وهو محال ، أو كان يتصف بصفة لا تقوم به ، وذلك أظهر استحالة كما سبق ، ولم يذهب أحدٌ إلى حدوث الحياة والقدرة ، وإنما اعتقدوا ذلك في العلم بالحوادث وفي الإرادة وفي الكلام، ونحن نستدل ثلاثة دلائل على قدم الصفات على استحالة كونه سبحانه محلاً للحوادث من ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : أن كل حادث فهو جائز الوجود ، والقديم الأزلي واجب الوجود، والحادث جائز الوجود، وصفاته تعالى واجبة الوجود ، ولو تطرق الجواز إلى صفاته .. لكان ذلك مناقضاً لوجوب وجوده ; فإن الجواز والوجوب يتناقضان ، فكل ما هو واجب الذات فمن المحال أن يكون جائز الصفات ، وهذا واضح بنفسه
The third ruling: all His attributes, exalted is He, are eternal like His essence. All attributes are eternal; for if they were temporal, then the Eternal, exalted, would be a locus of temporal events, which is impossible, or He would be described by an attribute not subsisting in Him, and that is more clearly impossible as previously stated. No one has claimed that life and power are temporal; rather they believed that regarding knowledge of temporal events, will, and speech. And we adduce three proofs for the eternity of attributes based on the impossibility of His being a locus of temporal events from three aspects: The first: every temporal thing is contingent in existence, and the eternal pre-eternal is necessary in existence, and the temporal is contingent in existence, and His attributes, exalted, are necessary in existence. If contingency were to apply to His attributes, that would contradict the necessity of His existence; for contingency and necessity are contradictory. So everything that is necessary in essence, it is impossible that it be contingent in attributes. This is self-evident.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the third ruling concerning divine attributes. Ghazali begins by affirming that all of God's attributes are eternal like His essence. He explains that if attributes were temporal, it would imply either that God becomes a locus for temporal events, or that He is described by an attribute not subsisting in Him—both impossibilities. He notes that no one claims life and power are temporal; the dispute is only over knowledge, will, and speech. Ghazali then introduces three proofs for the eternity of attributes based on the impossibility of God being a locus of temporal events. The first proof is straightforward: every temporal thing is contingent (jāʾiz al-wujūd), while the eternal is necessary (wājib al-wujūd). Since God's essence is necessary, His attributes must also be necessary. If contingency applied to His attributes, it would contradict the necessity of His existence, because necessity and contingency are mutually exclusive. Thus, a being that is necessary in essence cannot have contingent attributes. Ghazali considers this proof self-evident.

قديم
حادث
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
محل للحوادث
صفات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that attributes are accidental or distinct from God's essence, implying they could be temporal.
Correction: Ghazali argues that attributes are as necessary as God's essence; contingency in attributes would contradict the necessity of God's existence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's first proof, why must God's attributes be eternal?
A) Because temporal attributes would imply a change in God's essence.
B) Because every temporal thing is necessary, and God is contingent.
C) Because contingency in attributes would contradict the necessity of God's existence.
D) Because God cannot be described by any attributes at all.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are eternal because, like His essence, they are necessary; contingency in attributes would contradict the necessity of His existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider a moment when you felt God was distant or angry with you. Did you assume His attribute of love or mercy had temporarily changed? How does the impossibility of contingency in God's attributes force you to re-examine that feeling? What is one thing you will actively remind yourself of this week to align with the truth that His attributes are eternal?
?
The lesson states that if attributes were temporal, God would be a locus of change, which is impossible. In your daily life, do you act as if God is affected by your actions—surprised, disappointed, or pleased in a changing way? How does the self-evident necessity of His attributes challenge you to see Him as immutable? What personal habit can you start to cultivate this truth in your heart?

The Dilemma and Essential Impossibility of Accidents in God
Ghazali presents a dilemma and argues for essential impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's dilemma argument that God cannot accept accidents because the impossibility is essential to His nature and cannot change.

Core Idea
God's rejection of accidents is essential, not external, and thus immutable.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a powerful dilemma: if we cannot conceive of a first accident God accepts, then accidents would be infinite; if we can, then the impossibility must be essential. He then argues that essential impossibility can never become possibility, using the analogy of God's inability to accept color.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the first part of the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the two branches: imagination does not ascend to a first accident, or it does. Understand the consequence of each.
The first branch leads to an infinite regress of accidents, which is impossible.
2
Analyze the second branch
In the second branch, Ghazali argues that the impossibility of accepting accidents must be either essential or due to something external. Trace why the external option is impossible.
Any external factor could be absent, leading back to perpetual accidents.
3
Understand essential impossibility
Realize that once essential impossibility is established, it cannot change. Ghazali gives the color analogy: God essentially cannot accept color, and that never changes. Apply the same to all accidents.
4
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider the implications: God's essence is immutable in its attributes. This protects divine transcendence.
This is a cornerstone of Sunni kalam against those who claim God changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لم يرتق الوهم إليه .. لزمَ جواز اتصافه بالحوادث أبداً ، ولزم منه حوادث لا أول لها ، وقد قام الدليل على استحالته (۱) ، وهذا القسم ما ذهب إليه أحدٌ من العقلاء (٢)
من
وإن ارتقى الوهم إلى حادث يستحيل قبله حدوث حادث .. فتلك الاستحالة لقبول الحادثات في ذاته لا تخلو : إما أن تكون لذاته ، أو لزائد عليه ، وباطل أن تكون لزائد عليه ؛ فإنَّ كلَّ زائدِ يُفْرِضُ يمكن تقديرُ عدمه ، فيلزم منه تواصل الحوادث أبداً ، وهو محال ، فلم يبق إلا أن استحالته حيث إن واجب الوجود يكون على صفة يستحيل معها قبول الحوادث لذاته ، فإذا كان ذلك مستحيلاً في ذاته أزلاً .. استحال أن ينقلب المحال جائزاً ، ويُنزَّلُ ذلك منزلة استحالتِهِ لقبول اللون أزلاً ؛ فإن ذلك يبقى فيما لا يزال ؛ لأنه لذاته لا يقبل الألوان باتفاق العقلاء ، ولم يجز أن تتغير تلك الاستحالة إلى الجواز ؛ فكذلك سائر الحوادث
تحريجة: فيبطل فإن قيل : فهذا يبطل بحدوث العالم ؛ فإنه كان ممكناً قبل حدوثه ولم قولكم بحدوث يكن الوهم يرتقى إلى وقت يستحيل حدوثه قبله ، ومع ذلك يستحيل حدوثه
العالم
أزلاً ولم يستحل على الجملة حدوثه ؟
قلنا : هذا الإلزام فاسد ؛ فإنا إنما نحيلُ إثبات ذات تنبو عن قبول حادث لكونها واجبة الوجود ، ثم تنقلبُ إلى جواز قبول الحوادث ، والعالم ليس له ذات قبل الحدوث موصوفةٌ بأنها قابلة للحدوث أو غير قابلة حتى ينقلب إلى قبول جواز الحدوث، فيلزم ذلك على مساق دليلنا
نعم ؛ يلزم ذلك المعتزلة ، حيث قالوا : للعالم ذات في العدم قديمة قابلة للحدوث يطرأ عليها الحدوث بعد أن لم يكن (۳) ، فأما على أصلنا ..
فغير لازم ، وإنما الذي نقوله في العالم : إنه فعل ، والفعل القديم محال
If the imagination does not ascend to it, then it would be necessary that He is perpetually characterized by accidents, and from that would follow accidents with no beginning, and proof has been established of its impossibility. And this is a position held by none of the rational people. And if the imagination ascends to an accident before which no accident could occur, then that impossibility of accepting accidents in His essence is either essential or from something external. It is false that it is from something external, because every external thing that is assumed can be imagined to be absent, leading to continuity of accidents perpetually, which is impossible. So nothing remains except that its impossibility is because the Necessary Existent is in a state with which accepting accidents is impossible essentially. So since that is impossible in His essence eternally, it is impossible for what is impossible to become possible. And that is analogous to the impossibility of His accepting color eternally; that remains perpetually because He essentially does not accept colors by agreement of the rational people, and it is not allowed that that impossibility changes to possibility. Similarly for all accidents. Objection: Is it not invalidated by the temporal origination of the world? For it was possible before its origination, and the imagination does not ascend to a time before which its occurrence was impossible, yet its occurrence is impossible eternally and is not impossible absolutely? We say: This objection is flawed; for we only deem it impossible to posit an essence that is exempt from accepting an accident because it is necessary existence, then changes to allowing accidents. The world has no essence before origination described as capable of origination or not, so that it changes to allowing origination, so that would follow from the course of our proof. Yes, it does follow for the Mu'tazila, who say that the world has an essence in non-existence, eternal, capable of origination, which origination occurs to it after it was not. But according to our principle, it is not necessary. And what we say about the world is that it is an act, and an eternal act is impossible.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the possibility of accidents (hawadith) in God's essence. It is a dilemma argument. First, Ghazali states that if the mind cannot conceive of a first accident that God could accept, then God would be perpetually subject to accidents, leading to an infinite series of accidents, which is impossible. If the mind can conceive of a first accident before which no accident is possible, then the impossibility of accepting accidents must be either essential (li dhatihi) or due to something external (za'id 'alayh). It cannot be due to something external, because any external factor could be imagined absent, which would again allow accidents and lead to infinite regress. Therefore, the impossibility is essential. Once something is essentially impossible, it cannot become possible. Ghazali draws an analogy: God essentially cannot accept color, and that impossibility never changes. Similarly, He cannot accept any accident. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning seems to invalidate belief in the temporal origination of the world, which was possible before it occurred, yet there was no time before which its occurrence was impossible. Ghazali responds that the world has no essence prior to its existence—it is not a subject that changes from impossible to possible. For God, the supposed change is from an essence that rejects accidents to one that receives them, which is impossible. He notes that the objection would only apply if one held (like the Mu'tazila) that the world has an eternal essence in non-existence capable of receiving existence. But on our (Ash'ari) principle, the world is simply an act of God, and an eternal act is impossible, so the analogy fails.

Key Terms
الحوادث (accidents)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
استحالة ذاتية (essential impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's inability to accept accidents is temporary or could change.
Correction: Ghazali proves that the impossibility is essential and therefore eternal and immutable, just like God's inability to accept color.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why cannot the impossibility of God accepting accidents be due to something external?
A) Because any external factor could be absent, leading to infinite accidents.
B) Because God has no external factors.
C) Because accidents are not real.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny it.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is inherently transcendent and cannot accept any accident, a truth that is eternal and unchangeable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes catch yourself imagining that God changes His mind or is affected by your actions? How does this passage challenge that tendency, and what would it mean for your reliance on His immutability?
?
If God's rejection of accidents is essential and eternal, then your sins do not cause Him to change. Does that make you feel more secure in His mercy or more accountable for your actions? Sit with that tension.
?
This argument uses an analogy: God cannot accept color, and that impossibility never changes. Is there an area in your life where you secretly hope God will become 'something He is not'—more lenient, more reactive—to suit your desires? Confess that to yourself and realign.

Responding to the Objection from the World's Origination
Ghazali answers an objection about the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the objection that his argument against accidents in God would also prevent the world's creation from non-existence.

Core Idea
The world has no essence before creation, so it can change from possible to actual, unlike God.

An objector argues that if essential impossibility cannot change, then the world could not have been created, since it was possible before. Ghazali responds by distinguishing between God, who has an essence, and the world, which has no essence before existence. He also highlights the difference between the Ash'ari view and the Mu'tazila view.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's point: the world's origination seems similar—it was possible before, but there was no time when it was impossible, yet it was impossible eternally? Understand the challenge.
The objector tries to apply the same logic to the world: if essential impossibility cannot change, how did the world come to be?
2
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali says the objection is flawed because the world has no essence prior to existence. The impossibility of an eternal world is not about an essence changing from impossible to possible; rather, the world is an act of God.
The key is that for God, we posit an essence that cannot accept accidents. For the world, there is no essence before existence.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila view
Ghazali notes that the objection would apply to the Mu'tazila who believe the world has an eternal essence in non-existence capable of receiving existence. But on Ash'ari principles, the world is simply an act, and an eternal act is impossible.
This shows how different theological commitments lead to different responses.
4
Reflect on the argument
Consider why Ghazali's response preserves the argument against accidents in God while allowing the world's creation. It highlights the unique nature of God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It cannot be due to something external, because any external factor could be imagined absent, which would again allow accidents and lead to infinite regress. Therefore, the impossibility is essential. Once something is essentially impossible, it cannot become possible. Ghazali draws an analogy: God essentially cannot accept color, and that impossibility never changes. Similarly, He cannot accept any accident. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning seems to invalidate belief in the temporal origination of the world, which was possible before it occurred, yet there was no time before which its occurrence was impossible. Ghazali responds that the world has no essence prior to its existence—it is not a subject that changes from impossible to possible. For God, the supposed change is from an essence that rejects accidents to one that receives them, which is impossible. He notes that the objection would only apply if one held (like the Mu'tazila) that the world has an eternal essence in non-existence capable of receiving existence. But on our (Ash'ari) principle, the world is simply an act of God, and an eternal act is impossible, so the analogy fails.

Key Terms
الفعل القديم (eternal act)
الحدوث (origination)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument against accidents in God would also prevent the world's creation.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the world has no essence prior to existence, so the reasoning does not apply. The world is an act, not a subject that changes.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say the objection about the world's origination does not apply to his argument?
A) Because the world was always impossible.
B) Because the world has no essence before existence.
C) Because the Mu'tazila are wrong.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's argument against accidents in God is consistent with the world's creation because the world has no pre-existent essence that changes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever imagine God as being subject to change like created things, perhaps unconsciously expecting Him to 'react' to your actions? How does Ghazali's distinction between an essence and an act challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to trust in God's unchanging nature today?
?
Ghazali says the world has no essence before God creates it. If your entire existence is an act of God, not a self-existing essence, what does that mean for your sense of entitlement or independence? In what area of your life do you need to acknowledge that you are not an essence but a constant act of Allah?
?
The objector's confusion came from assuming the world had an essence in non-existence. Do you sometimes assume that your own 'potential' or 'self' exists independently of God's creation? How can you remind yourself daily that before God created you, you were nothing, and that every moment you continue to exist only by His act?

The Third Proof: Accidents Cannot Subsist in God's Essence
Ghazali presents third proof and rebuts objection that silence is mere absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third proof against accidents in God's essence, which argues that an accident would require an eternal opposite or infinite regress, and you will see the initial objection that silence is mere absence.

Core Idea
If an accident occurs in God, its opposite must be eternal or lead to infinite regress—both impossible.

Ghazali continues his proofs against accidents (ḥawādith) subsisting in God. This third proof is a conditional argument: before any hypothetical accident, God must have had either its opposite or its absence. Both options lead to impossibility. Ghazali illustrates with the example of speech and silence. He then anticipates an objection that silence is not a real attribute but mere absence, and begins to answer it. This lesson covers the main proof, the objection, and the start of the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation slowly. Note the conditional structure: If an accident subsists in God, then before that He must have had either the opposite of that accident or dissociation from it.
The proof is built on a dilemma: either the prior state is eternal or temporal.
2
Examine the examples
Ghazali clarifies with speech (Karramiyya) and knowledge (Jahm). See how he applies the same reasoning to these specific attributes.
Notice the consequence: if silence or ignorance is eternal, it cannot cease; if temporal, it leads to infinite regress.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that silence is not a real attribute—it is merely the absence of speech. So when speech appears, nothing is destroyed; only a new existent is added.
This objection tries to break the dilemma by denying that the prior state is a real attribute.
4
See the beginning of the response
Ghazali starts his response with two aspects. The first aspect challenges the claim that silence is mere absence, hinting that it is a real attribute. The passage cuts off before completing the response.
The complete response would be needed to fully appreciate the argument.

The third proof: We say: If we suppose an accident subsisting in His essence, then before that He is either characterized by the opposite of that accident, or by dissociation from that accident. That opposite or dissociation: if it is eternal, its nullification and removal is impossible, because the eternal cannot be annihilated; and if it is temporal, then before it there must be an accident, and likewise before that accident, leading to accidents with no beginning, which is impossible. This is clarified by a specific attribute, such as speech: The Karramiyya said that He is eternally speaking in the sense that He is able to create speech in His essence, and whenever He creates something outside His essence, He creates His statement in His essence. So before creating this statement He must have been silent, and His silence is eternal. And Jahm said that He creates knowledge in His essence; so before that He must have been ignorant, and His ignorance is eternal. So we say: Eternal silence and eternal ignorance are impossible to nullify, due to the foregoing proof of the impossibility of annihilating the eternal. If it is said: Silence is nothing; it is merely the absence of speech, and ignorance is the absence of knowledge and their opposites. So when speech comes to be, nothing is nullified, because there was nothing except the eternal essence, which remains, and another existent is added (speech and knowledge). As for saying something is nullified—no. And this is analogous to the existence of the world: it nullifies eternal non-existence, but non-existence is not a thing such that it is described as eternal and its nullification can be supposed. The answer is from two aspects: One of them: that the saying of one who says 'Silence is absence of speech and not an attribute'...
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against accidents in God's essence and begins the response to an objection. The proof is based on a conditional: if an accident were to occur in God's essence, before its occurrence God must have been either characterized by its opposite or by the absence of that accident. If that opposite or absence is eternal, it cannot cease, because the eternal cannot be annihilated. If it is temporal, then it itself is an accident, leading to an infinite regress of accidents, which is impossible. Ghazali illustrates with speech: the Karramiyya claim God creates speech in His essence, so before that He must have been silent eternally. Similarly, Jahm claims God creates knowledge, implying eternal ignorance. But eternal silence/ignorance cannot cease. The objector says: silence is not a real thing—it is merely the absence of speech. So when speech appears, nothing is destroyed; the eternal essence remains and speech is simply added. The objector compares this to the world's creation: it nullifies eternal non-existence, but non-existence is not a thing. Ghazali begins his response with two aspects, but the page cuts off after mentioning the first aspect: that the claim 'silence is absence of speech, not an attribute' is problematic (to be continued).

Key Terms
القديم (the eternal)
الضد (opposite)
الانفكاك (dissociation)
السكوت (silence)
الغفلة (ignorance/heedlessness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Silence is merely the absence of speech, so it is not a real attribute and its ‘removal’ when speech appears is not a genuine change.
Correction: Ghazali's response (first aspect) challenges this: even if silence is not a positive attribute, it still has a real status (perhaps a negation) that cannot be dismissed; otherwise, the argument's logic would apply equally to non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what is the consequence if the opposite or dissociation of a hypothetical accident in God is eternal?
A) It can be removed by creating a new accident.
B) It cannot be nullified because the eternal cannot be annihilated.
C) It leads to infinite regress of accidents.
D) It proves that God has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
Accidents in God's essence are impossible because they would necessitate an eternal opposite that cannot cease, or an infinite regress of accidents.

Third Proof: Impossibility of Accidents in God's Essence
Ghazali enumerates a logical dilemma and illustrates it with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's third proof that no temporal accident can subsist in God's essence, and why both possibilities (eternal prior state or temporal prior state) lead to impossibility.

Core Idea
A temporal accident in God would require an impossible prior state.

Ghazali continues his arguments against the possibility of accidents in God's essence. In this third proof, he presents a logical dilemma: before any purported accident, God must have been in a state opposite or devoid of that accident. If that prior state is eternal, it cannot cease; if temporal, it leads to infinite regress. Both are impossible, so the premise is false. The proof is illustrated with examples from the Karramiyya and Jahmiyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the general argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where Ghazali presents the logical dilemma. Understand the two possibilities for the prior state.
2
Work through the two branches
Examine each branch: (1) if the prior state is eternal, it cannot cease; (2) if temporal, infinite regress. Both lead to contradiction.
3
Study the concrete examples
Read the illustrations with speech (Karramiyya) and knowledge (Jahmiyya). See how the same dilemma applies to specific attributes.
These show the proof is general, not limited to speech.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Conclude that no temporal accident can subsist in God's essence because any prior state is impossible. Understand why this supports God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثالث : وهو أنا نقول : إذا قدَّرْنا قيامَ حادث بذاته . . فهو قبل ذلك : إما أن يتصف بضد ذلك الحادث ، أو بالانفكاك عن ذلك الحادث ، وذلك الضدُّ أو ذلك الانفكاك : إن كان قديماً .. استحال بطلانه وزواله ؛ لأن القديم لا يُعدم ، وإن كان حادثاً .. كان قبله حادث لا محالة ، وكذا قبل ذلك الحادث ، ويؤدي إلى حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها وهو محال ، ويتضح هذا بأن يُفرضَ في صفة معينة ؛ كالكلام مثلاً : فإن الكرامية قالوا : إنه في الأزل متكلم على معنى أنه قادر على خَلْقِ الكلام في ذاته ، ومهما أحدث شيئاً في غير ذاته . . ذاته .. أحدث في ذاته قولَهُ و كن الله فلا بد أن يكون قبل إحداث هذا القول ساكتاً ، ويكون سكوته قديماً ، وإذا قال جَهم : إنه يُحدِث في ذاته علماً ؛ فلا بد أن يكون قبله غافلاً ، وتكون غفلته قديمة . . فنقول : السكوتُ القديم والغفلة القديمة يستحيلُ بطلائهما ؛ لما سبق من الدليل على استحالة عدم القديم
The third proof: We say: If we suppose the subsistence of a temporal accident in His essence, then before that, either He is characterized by the opposite of that accident, or by separation from that accident. That opposite or that separation: if it is eternal, then its ceasing and removal is impossible, because the eternal is not annihilated; and if it is temporal, then before it there is necessarily a temporal accident, and likewise before that temporal accident, and this leads to temporal accidents with no beginning, which is impossible. This becomes clear by positing a specific attribute, like speech for example: for the Karramiyya said that He is in pre-eternity speaking in the sense that He is able to create speech in His essence, and whenever He creates something outside His essence... in His essence He creates His saying 'Be', so necessarily before creating this speech He was silent, and His silence would be eternal. And if Jahm said that He creates knowledge in His essence, then necessarily before it He was forgetful, and His forgetfulness would be eternal. So we say: Eternal silence and eternal forgetfulness cannot cease, due to the proof mentioned earlier regarding the impossibility of the eternal's non-existence.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's third proof against the possibility of temporal accidents (hawadith) subsisting in God's essence. The proof proceeds by considering that if a temporal accident occurs in God's essence, then before that accident, God must have been in a state either opposite to that accident or devoid of it. If that prior state (opposite or absence) is eternal, then it cannot cease, because the eternal cannot be annihilated. But we observe the accident occurring, so the prior state must have ceased, which is contradictory. If the prior state is itself temporal, then it too requires a prior state, leading to an infinite regress of temporal states, which is impossible. Ghazali then illustrates with specific examples: the Karramiyya claim that God creates speech in His essence, implying He was silent before; the Jahmiyya claim He creates knowledge, implying He was forgetful before. Silence and forgetfulness (if eternal) cannot cease, yet the speech and knowledge occur, so the eternal would cease—an impossibility. Thus, no temporal accident can subsist in God's essence.

Key Terms
حادث (hādith)
قديم (qadīm)
السكوت القديم (al-sukūt al-qadīm)
الغفلة القديمة (al-ghafla al-qadīma)
بطلان البطلان (buṭlān al-qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof only applies to speech, but it applies to any temporal attribute.
Correction: Ghazali shows the dilemma is general; the examples are just illustrations.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali's third proof demonstrate about the prior state before a supposed temporal accident in God?
A) It must be temporal and lead to infinite regress.
B) It must be eternal and thus impossible to cease.
C) It can be either eternal or temporal, but neither is coherent.
D) It is irrelevant to the argument.

Key Takeaway
No temporal accident can subsist in God's essence because it would entail an impossible prior state, either eternal or infinitely temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The proof shows that any temporal attribute in God leads to absurdity. Is there an attribute in your own life—like patience, anger, or forgiveness—that you treat as if it were fixed and eternal, but actually needs to be transformed? How does God's absolute transcendence call you to reconsider your attachments to temporary states?

Objection and Response on Silence as Non-Existence
Ghazali rejects the claim that silence is merely absence of speech and forgetfulness absence of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the claim that silence and forgetfulness are mere non-existence, and the two arguments he uses to establish them as positive attributes opposite to speech and knowledge.

Core Idea
Silence is not mere absence; it is a real attribute proven by the distinction between states.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a subtle objection: that silence and forgetfulness are not real attributes but merely the absence of speech and knowledge. He must defend his position that these are positive opposites. With two powerful arguments, he shows that silence is as real as rest, and that God's essence is perceived in different modes. Read on to see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection that silence and forgetfulness are nothing but absence. Notice how the objector uses the analogy of the world's creation to argue that nothing ceases when speech arises.
The objector claims that only the eternal essence remains, and speech/knowledge are added as new existents.
2
Examine the first answer
Ghazali's first response uses a parallel: if silence were absence, then rest would be absence of motion, which is impossible. Rest is known to be a positive attribute. Study how he proves this by the perception of distinction between states.
This argument is grounded in our direct experience of two different states of a substance.
3
Examine the second answer
The second response addresses the analogy with the world's non-existence. Unlike the world, God's essence is eternal and is perceived in two different modes (silence and speech). This shows that silence and speech are attributes of the same enduring essence.
This preserves divine simplicity while affirming multiple attributes.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Ghazali uses both empirical perception and logical analogy to defend the reality of silence as a positive attribute. Connect this to the broader Islamic theology of divine attributes.
This passage is crucial for understanding that God's attributes are not mere negations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : السكوت ليس بشيء ، إنما يرجع ذلك إلى عدم الكلام ، وتحريجة : لم تجعلون من عدم الكلام وعدم العلم أشياء تبطل ؟ والغفلة ترجع إلى عدم العلم والجهل وأضداده ، فإذا وُجِدَ الكلام والعلم لم يبطل شيء ؛ إذ لم يكن شيءٌ إلا الذات القديمة ، وهي باقية ، ولكن انضاف إليها موجود آخرُ؛ وهو الكلام والعلم ، فأما أن يقال : انعدم شيء . . فلا ، ويتنزَّلُ ذلك منزلة وجود العالم؛ فإنَّهُ يبطل العدم القديم ، ولكن العدم ليس بشيء حتى يوصف بالقدم ويقدر بطلانه ؟
والجواب من وجهين :
أحدهما : أنَّ قول القائل : السكوتُ هو عدم الكلام وليس بصفة ، والغفلة عدم العلم وليست بصفة .. كقوله : البياضُ هو عدم السواد وسائر الألوان وليس بلون ، والسكون هو عدم الحركة وليس بعرض ، وذلك محال ، والدليل الذي دلَّ على استحالته بعينه يدلُّ على استحالة هذا والخصوم في هذه المسألة معترفون بأن السكون وصف زائد على عدم الحركة؛ فإن كل من يدعي: أن السكونَ هو عدم الحركة .. لا يقدر على إثبات حدث العالم؛ فظهور الحركة بعد السكون إذا دلّ على حدث المتحرك .. فكذلك ظهور الكلام بعد السكوت يدل على حدث المتكلم من غير فرق؛ إذ المسلك الذي به عُرِفَ كون السكون معنى هو مضاد للحركة بعينه يعرفُ به كونُ السكوت معنى يضادُّ الكلام، وكونُ الغفلة معنى يضادُّ العلم، وهو أنا أدركنا تفرقة بين حالتي الذات الساكنة والمتحركة؛ فإن الذات مدركة على الحالتين، والتفرقة مدركة بين الحالتين، ولا ترجع التفرقة إلا إلى زوال أمر وحدوث أمر؛ فإن الشيء لا يفارق نفسَهُ، فدل ذلك على أن كل قابل للشيء فلا يخلو عنه أو عن ضده، وهذا مطرد في الكلام والعلم.
والثاني: أنه لا يلزم على هذا الفرق بين وجود العالم وعدمه؛ فإن ذلك يوجب ذاتين، فإنه لم تُدرك في الحالتين ذات واحدةٌ يطرأ عليها الوجود، بل لا ذات للعالم قبل الحدوث، وللقديم ذات قبل حدوثِ الكلام، عُلِمَ على وجه مخالف للوجه الذي عُلِمَ عليه بعد حدوث الكلام، يعبر عن الوجه بالسكوت وعن هذا بالكلام، فهما وجهان مختلفان أُدركت عليهما ذات مستمرة الوجودِ في الحالتين، وللذات هيئة وصفة وحالة بكونه ساكتاً كما أنَّ له هيئة بكونه متكلماً، وكما له هيئة بكونه ساكناً ومتحركا وأبيض
For it might be said: Silence is not a thing; it only refers to the absence of speech. And an escape: Why do you make from absence of speech and absence of knowledge things that cease? Forgetfulness refers to absence of knowledge and ignorance and their opposites. So when speech and knowledge exist, nothing ceases; because there was nothing except the eternal essence, and it remains, but another existent—namely speech and knowledge—has been added to it. As for saying that something has ceased—no. And this is analogous to the existence of the world: for it nullifies eternal non-existence, but non-existence is not a thing to be described as eternal and its ceasing imagined?
The answer is from two aspects:
One of them: The statement that silence is the absence of speech and not an attribute, and forgetfulness is the absence of knowledge and not an attribute... is like saying whiteness is the absence of blackness and all other colors and is not a color, and rest is the absence of motion and is not an accident. That is impossible, and the very proof that demonstrated its impossibility demonstrates the impossibility of this. And the opponents in this issue acknowledge that rest is an additional attribute beyond the absence of motion; for whoever claims that rest is the absence of motion cannot prove the temporality of the world. For the appearance of motion after rest, if it indicates the temporality of the mover, then likewise the appearance of speech after silence indicates the temporality of the speaker without any difference. For the pathway by which it is known that rest is a meaning opposite to motion is exactly the same by which it is known that silence is a meaning opposite to speech, and forgetfulness is a meaning opposite to knowledge. And that is that we perceive a distinction between the two states of a substance at rest and in motion; for the substance is perceived in both states, and the distinction is perceived between the two states. And the distinction is due only to the removal of something and the occurrence of something; because a thing does not separate from itself. So that indicates that everything that is receptive to a thing is not devoid of it or its opposite. This applies consistently to speech and knowledge.
The second: That this does not entail a distinction between the existence of the world and its non-existence; for that would require two essences. For the world is not perceived as a single essence upon which existence supervenes; rather the world has no essence before origination. But the eternal has an essence before the occurrence of speech, known in a manner different from the manner in which it is known after the occurrence of speech. The first manner is expressed as silence, and this as speech. So they are two different modes in which an essence that continues in existence in both states is perceived. And the essence has a configuration, attribute, and state by being silent, just as it has a configuration by being speaking, and just as it has a configuration by being at rest, in motion, and white.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and Ghazali's two-pronged response. The objector argues that silence and forgetfulness are mere non-existence, so when speech and knowledge arise, nothing ceases; only the eternal essence remains, with an added existent. The analogy is the creation of the world: it nullifies eternal non-existence, but non-existence is not a thing. Ghazali's first answer refutes the claim that silence is non-existence. He uses a parallel: if silence is absence of speech, then by the same logic rest would be absence of motion, and whiteness absence of blackness. But these are known to be positive attributes. The opponents themselves admit rest is an attribute beyond absence of motion, otherwise they couldn't prove the world's temporality from motion after rest. Similarly, perception of a distinction between states of a substance (e.g., at rest vs. moving) indicates that something positive is gained or lost. Thus silence and forgetfulness are positive opposites of speech and knowledge. The second answer addresses the analogy with the world's non-existence. Unlike the world—which had no essence before creation—God's essence is eternal and perceived in two different modes (silence vs. speech). This shows that silence and speech are distinct attributes of the same enduring essence, not mere non-existence.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukūt)
الغفلة (al-ghafla)
عدم (ʿadam)
القديم (al-qadīm)
السكون (al-sukūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Silence and forgetfulness are merely the absence of speech and knowledge, not real attributes.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates that silence is a positive attribute opposite to speech, proven by the perceived distinction between states of a substance, just as rest is a positive attribute opposite to motion.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's first answer to the objection that silence is non-existence?
A) He accepts it and argues the analogy proves God's temporality.
B) He refutes it by comparing silence to rest, which is a positive attribute.
C) He denies that silence is an attribute at all.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that silence and forgetfulness are real attributes, not mere absences, by showing that they are perceived as distinct states of a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are silent about God's greatness or fail to defend His religion, do you treat that silence as a mere absence of speech, or as a real attribute of your soul that you must account for?
?
If forgetfulness is a positive attribute, what does your forgetfulness of God in your daily life reveal about the true state of your heart?
?
Do you consider your own states—such as resting from obedience or being silent about truth—as neutral absences, or as real characteristics that define your relationship with Allah?

Second Response: State of Being Devoid Cannot Be Eternal
Ghazali argues that the state of being devoid of speech cannot be eternal because it is negated by speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the state of being devoid of speech cannot be eternal, and how he distinguishes it from the non-existence of the world.

Core Idea
The eternal cannot cease to exist, so a state that passes away cannot be eternal.

Ghazali offers a second line of defense against the objection that God's silence is not a positive attribute. Even if we grant that silence is merely the absence of speech, the state of being devoid of speech is something that disappears when speech occurs. Since it passes away, it cannot be eternal. This leads to a key distinction between a state inhering in an essence and the non-existence of a non-entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify Ghazali's key premise
Notice that Ghazali states 'the eternal does not cease' regardless of being an essence, state, or attribute. This is the core principle.
The premise is that anything eternal cannot be negated or pass away.
3
Understand the distinction from the world's non-existence
Ghazali addresses a potential objection: the non-existence of the world also passed away but was not eternal. He argues that the world's non-existence is not a state of an essence, so it is different.
The distinction hinges on whether the state is attached to an essence or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الانفصال : هو أنه إن سُلَّم أيضاً أن السكوت ليس بمعنى ، وإنما يرجع ذلك إلى ذات منفكَّة عن الكلام .. فالانفكاك عن الكلام حال للمنفك ـ لا محالة - ينعدم بطريان الكلام ؛ فحال الانفكاك تسمى عدماً أو وجوداً أو صفةً أو هيئة . . قد انتفى بالكلام ، والمنتفي قديم ، وقد ذكرنا أنَّ القديم لا ينتفي ، سواءً كان ذاتاً أو حالاً أو صفة ، وليست الاستحالة لكونه ذاتاً فقط ، بل لكونه قديماً ، ولا يلزم عدم العالم ؛ فإنه انتفى مع القدم ؛ لأن عدم العالم ليس بذاتِ عدم العالم ليس بذات ، ولا حصل منه حال لذات حتى يقدَّر تغيرُها وتبدلُها على الذات ، فالفرق بينهما ظاهر
The second way of disconnection: It is that even if it is granted that silence is not a meaning, and that it reduces to an essence devoid of speech, then the state of being devoid of speech is a state for that which is devoid, inevitably, and it disappears with the arrival of speech. So the state of being devoid [of speech] is called non-existence, or existence, or attribute, or form. It has been negated by speech, and that which is negated is eternal. And we have mentioned that the eternal does not cease, whether it is an essence or a state or an attribute. The impossibility is not because it is an essence only, but because it is eternal. And the non-existence of the world does not follow; for it ceased [only] with eternity? because the non-existence of the world is not an essence, nor did it give rise to a state for an essence such that its change and alteration upon the essence could be supposed. So the difference between them is apparent.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a second line of defense. Ghazali says that even if we concede to the opponent that silence is not a positive attribute but merely the essence being devoid of speech, still this state of being devoid is a state that is negated when speech occurs. Since it is negated, it cannot be eternal (because the eternal cannot cease to exist). The opponent might object that this would imply that the non-existence of the world is also a state that is negated at creation, but Ghazali counters that the non-existence of the world is not a state inhering in an essence, because the world had no essence to begin with. So the difference is clear.

Key Terms
الانفكاك
الحال
القديم
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since the state of being devoid is not a positive attribute, it could be eternal.
Correction: Ghazali argues that any state that is negated cannot be eternal, regardless of whether it is an attribute or a mere absence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why can't the state of being devoid of speech be eternal?
A) Because it is a state that passes away when speech occurs.
B) Because it is not a real attribute.
C) Because it is a non-existence.
D) Because it is not a state of God.

Key Takeaway
The eternal cannot cease, so a state that passes away—even if it is mere absence—cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is eternal and He is never devoid of speech, then His word has always existed. When you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as a temporal book revealed in time, or as the eternal speech of God? How does your belief about its nature change how you approach it daily?
?
Ghazali argues that a state of being devoid cannot be eternal. Have you ever felt that God was silent in your life, that He was not speaking to you through His signs or words? How does the truth that God is eternally speaking reshape that feeling? Will you now actively listen for His eternal speech where you previously assumed silence?

Three Attributes as Accidents: The Opponent's Case
Ghazali reports the opponent's objection narrowing the dispute to three attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the attributes of speech, will, and knowledge must be created and would thereby make God a locus of accidents.

Core Idea
The dispute over God's attributes narrows to three: speech, will, and knowledge.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents the objection of his opponents. They agree that most accidents do not apply to God, but they argue that three particular attributes—speech, will, and knowledge—must be created. If these attributes subsist in God's essence, then God would be a locus of accidents, which is impossible. This sets the stage for Ghazali's response, which we will explore in subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
Notice how the opponent narrows the dispute from all accidents to just seven attributes, then to three.
2
Identify the disputed attributes
The opponent explicitly states that the dispute is about three: speech (الكلام), will (الإرادة), and knowledge (العلم). For some, hearing and sight are included under knowledge.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the opponent's reasoning: these attributes must be created, they cannot subsist in another being, so they must subsist in God, leading to the conclusion that God is a locus of accidents.
The argument is concise but packed; take note of each step.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on why the opponent considers this conclusion impossible. This will be addressed in Ghazali's response later.
This passage only presents the objection, not the answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأعراض كثيرة ، والخصم لا يدعي كونَ الباري سبحانه محلاً لحدوث شيء منها؛ كالألوان والأكوان والآلام واللذات وغيرها، وإنما الكلام في الصفات السبع التي ذكرتموها، ولا نزاع من جملتها في الحياة والقدرة، وإنما النزاع في ثلاثة؛ الكلام والإرادة والعلم، وفي معنى العلم السمع والبصر عند من يثبتهما، وهذه الصفات الثلاثة لا بدَّ أن تكون حادثة، ثم يستحيل أن تقوم بغيره؛ لأنه لا يكون متصفاً بها .. فيجب أن تقوم بذاته؛ فيلزم منه كونه محلاً للحوادث: أما العلم بالحوادث.. فقد ذهب جهم إلى أنها علوم حادثة؛ وذلك
For it was said: Accidents are many, and the opponent does not claim that the Exalted Creator is a locus for the occurrence of any of them, such as colors, spatial phenomena, pains, pleasures, and others. Rather, the discussion concerns the seven attributes that you mentioned, and among them there is no dispute regarding life and power. Rather, the dispute concerns three: speech, will, and knowledge. And in the meaning of knowledge, hearing and sight according to those who affirm them. And these three attributes must be originated, then it is impossible for them to subsist in other than Him, because He would not be described by them. So they must subsist in His essence, necessitating that He is a locus of accidents. As for knowledge of contingents, Jahm held that they are originated sciences. And that...
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's objection. The opponent narrows the debate: only seven attributes are under discussion, and the dispute is over three: speech, will, and knowledge. The opponent argues that these three must be created (because they relate to temporal things, etc.), and since they cannot subsist in another being (like a substrate), they must subsist in God's essence. This would make God a locus of accidents, which is impossible. The opponent then mentions Jahm's view that knowledge of contingent events is created. The passage cuts off, but it sets up the response.

الصفات السبع
الحوادث
العلم
الإرادة
الكلام
جهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think the opponent argues that all attributes are created.
Correction: The opponent expressly narrows the dispute to only three attributes: speech, will, and knowledge (plus hearing and sight for some). Life and power are not in dispute.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage narrows the dispute to three attributes, showing that not all attributes are contested. Are there attributes of God that you find difficult to reconcile with your experience—such as His knowledge of your every thought or His will over your choices? How do you respond when these attributes challenge your sense of freedom or privacy? Will you surrender your discomfort to His perfect wisdom?
?
Jahm held that God's knowledge of contingents is itself originated. If God's knowledge of your future actions were not eternal, would you feel less accountable? Contemplate: does the eternity or temporality of God's attributes change the weight of your responsibility before Him? What one change in your life would reflect a proper awe of His omniscience?

The Eternal State Cannot Be Negated
Ghazali presents the second way to rebut the objection that silence is mere negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second argument that even if silence is not a positive attribute, the state of being disjoined from speech is an eternal state that cannot be negated.

Core Idea
An eternal state cannot be negated, even if it is not a positive attribute.

In the previous aspect, Ghazali argued that silence is a positive attribute (ma'na). Here, he takes a different approach: he concedes for argument's sake that silence is merely an essence devoid of speech. Yet he shows that the state of disjunction from speech is a real state (hal) that attaches to the essence. This state is eternal, and its elimination by the arrival of speech would mean negating something eternal—an impossibility. He then distinguishes this from the non-existence of the world to preempt a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concession
Notice that Ghazali begins by conceding that silence may not be a positive meaning, but rather refers to an essence free from speech. This is a strategic move to address the opponent on their own terms.
The opponent thinks that if silence is not a meaning, then no eternal attribute is negated when God begins to speak. Ghazali will refute this.
2
Follow the reasoning on the state
Ghazali argues that even if silence is not a meaning, the state of being disjoined from speech is a state (hal) that inheres in the essence. This state is eliminated when speech occurs. But if that state were eternal, its elimination would negate something eternal—which is impossible.
The key move: the state is an accident that belongs to the essence, so its removal is a change in the essence's condition.
3
Examine the distinction from the world's non-existence
Ghazali anticipates an objection: why doesn't this argument also prove that the world's non-existence is eternal and cannot be removed? He answers that the world's non-existence is not a state inhering in an essence; it is simply the absence of the world. Thus, its removal does not imply any change in an eternal subject.
This distinction shows that only states that are attributes of an eternal essence are protected from elimination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في الانفصال: هو أنه إن سُلَّم أيضاً أن السكوت ليس بمعنى، وإنما يرجع ذلك إلى ذات منفكَّة عن الكلام.. فالانفكاك عن الكلام حال للمنفك ـ لا محالة - ينعدم بطريان الكلام؛ فحال الانفكاك تسمى عدماً أو وجوداً أو صفةً أو هيئة.. قد انتفى بالكلام، والمنتفي قديم، وقد ذكرنا أنَّ القديم لا ينتفي، سواءً كان ذاتاً أو حالاً أو صفة، وليست الاستحالة لكونه ذاتاً فقط، بل لكونه قديماً، ولا يلزم عدم العالم؛ فإنه انتفى مع القدم؛ لأن عدم العالم ليس بذات، ولا حصل منه حال لذات حتى يقدَّر تغيرُها وتبدلُها على الذات، فالفرق بينهما ظاهر
The second aspect of the disconnection: It is that even if it is granted that silence is not a meaning, but rather that reverts to an essence disjoined from speech... Then the disjunction from speech is a state for the disjoined — inevitably — which is eliminated by the advent of speech; so that state of disjunction is called non-existence or existence or attribute or form... It has been negated by speech, and the negated is eternal. And we have mentioned that the eternal is not negated, whether it is an essence or a state or an attribute. And the impossibility is not because it is an essence only, but because it is eternal. And the non-existence of the world does not follow; for it is negated with eternity. Because the non-existence of the world is not an essence, nor does a state arise from it for an essence such that its change and alteration upon the essence could be conceived. So the difference between the two is apparent.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim. Ghazali presents the second way to respond to the objection that God's silence (i.e., not speaking) before creation is merely a negation and thus nothing eternal could be negated. In this second aspect, he concedes for argument's sake that silence is not a positive attribute (ma'na) but merely an essence devoid of speech. However, he argues that the state of being disjoined from speech is a state (hal) of that essence. This state is eliminated when speech occurs. Now, if that state were eternal, its elimination would mean that something eternal has been negated, which is impossible. He emphasizes that the impossibility stems from its eternity, not from it being an essence or state. He then distinguishes this from the non-existence of the world: the world's non-existence is not a state inhering in an essence, so its elimination does not entail an eternal state being negated. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
انفصال
سكوت
حال
قديم
انتفى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceding that silence is not a positive attribute (ma'na) invalidates the eternal attribute argument.
Correction: Ghazali shows that even without being a meaning, the state of disjunction is an eternal state attached to the essence, and its elimination would negate something eternal—impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's second argument, what is impossible to be negated?
A) A temporal state
B) An eternal essence or state
C) The non-existence of the world
D) A meaning (ma'na)

Key Takeaway
Ghazali defends divine timelessness by showing that even if silence is not a positive attribute, its elimination would require negating an eternal state, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s eternal state cannot be negated, what does that say about your attempts to force God to change His mind through your actions? Have you ever assumed that your worship could 'move' God from silence to speech as if He were mutable? Reflect on how this argument exposes a subtle form of anthropomorphism in your own heart.
?
Ghazali distinguishes between the non-existence of the world (which is not an eternal state) and God’s silence (which if it were a state would be eternal). How does this distinction apply to your own understanding of God’s attributes? Do you treat some of God’s attributes as merely temporary in your mind, especially when He seems silent? Confess any moments when you doubted God’s eternal nature because of changing circumstances.

The Opponent's Objection: Why Three Attributes Must Be Temporal
Ghazali reproduces the opponent's objection that knowledge, will, and speech must be temporal and their arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments raised by the Mu'tazila and Jahm against the eternity of knowledge, will, and speech.

Core Idea
The opponent claims that knowledge, will, and speech must be temporal because their objects are temporal.

Imam Ghazali presents the strongest objections against the divine attributes. The opponents argue that three specific attributes—knowledge, will, and speech—cannot be eternal because they relate to temporal events. Let's examine their reasoning before seeing Ghazali's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed attributes
The passage narrows the dispute to three attributes: knowledge, will, and speech. Notice that life and power are agreed upon as eternal.
2
Understand the knowledge argument
The opponent says that God's knowledge of temporal events must be temporal because He now knows something He didn't know before (e.g., the world's existence after it did not exist).
This is called the 'ta'abbud' argument.
3
Understand the will argument
If will were eternal, its object would have to exist simultaneously, but the world was created later, so will must be temporal.
4
Understand the speech argument
Eternal speech cannot contain past-tense reports or commands for non-existent beings, so speech must be temporal.
The opponent uses verses like 'We sent Noah' to show temporality.
5
Grasp the conclusion
These attributes subsist in God's essence, making Him a locus of temporal events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأعراض كثيرة، والخصم لا يدعي كونَ الباري سبحانه محلاً لجميعها؛ فلا نزاع في حدوث شيء منها؛ كالألوان والأكوان والآلام واللذات وغيرها، وإنما الصفات السبع وهي العلم والإرادة والحياة والقدرة والكلام والسمع والبصر، ولا نزاع من جملتها في الحياة والقدرة، وإنما النزاع في ثلاثة: الكلام والإرادة والعلم، وفي معنى العلم السمع والبصر عند من يثبتهما، وهذه الصفات الثلاثة لا بدَّ أن تكون حادثة، ثم يستحيل أن تقوم بغيره؛ لأنه لا يكون متصفاً بها.. فيجب أن تقوم بذاته؛ فيلزم منه كونه محلاً للحوادث: أما العلم بالحوادث.. فقد ذهب جهم إلى أنها علوم حادثة؛ وذلك لأن الله تعالى الآن عالم بأن العالم كان قد وجد قبل هذا، فهو في الأزل إن كان عالماً بأنه كان قد وجد.. كان هذا جهلاً لا علماً، وإذا لم يكن عالماً وهو الآن عالم.. فقد ظهر حدوث العلم بأن العالم كان قد أوجد قبل هذا، وكذا القول في كل حادث. وأما الإرادة.. فلا بدَّ من حدوثها؛ فإنها لو كانت قديمة.. لكان المراد معها؛ فإن القدرة والإرادة مهما تمَّتا وارتفعت العوائق.. وجب حصول المراد، فكيف يتأخَّرُ المراد عن الإرادة والقدرة من غير عائق؟ فلهذا قالت المعتزلة بحدوث إرادة في غير محل، وقالت الكرامية بحدوثها في ذاته، وربما عبّروا عنه بأنه يخلق إيجاداً في ذاته عند وجودِ كلَّ موجود، وهذا راجع إلى الإرادة. وأما الكلام.. فكيف يكون قديماً وفيه إخبار عما مضى؟ فكيف قال في الأزل: (إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ) ولم يكن قد خلق نوحاً بعد؟ وكيف قال في الأزل لموسى: (فَأَخْلَعْ نَعْلَيْكَ) ولم يخلق بعد موسى؟ وكيف أمر ونهى من غير مأمور ولا منهي؟ فإذا كان ذلك محالاً ثم عُلِمَ بالضرورة أنَّه أمر ناه واستحالَ ذلك في القدم.. عُلِمَ قطعاً أنه صار أمراً ناهياً بعد أن لم يكن، فلا معنى لكونه محلاً للحوادث إلا هذا؟ والجواب: أنّنا نقول: مهما حلَلْنا الشبهة في هذه الصفات الثلاث.. انتهضَ منه دليلٌ مستقل على إبطال كونه محلاً للحوادث؛ إذ لم يذهب إليه أحد إلا بسبب هذه الشبهة، فإذا انكشفت.. كان القول بها باطلاً كالقول بأنه محل للألوان وغيرها، مما لا يدلُّ دليل على الاتصاف بها، فنقول: دفع شبهة حدوث الباري تعالى في الأزل عَلِمَ بوجود العالم في وقت وجوده، وهذا العلم العلم القديم
For it was said: Accidents are many, and the opponent does not claim that the Exalted God is a locus for all of them; so there is no dispute in the temporality of some of them, such as colors, spatialities, pains, pleasures, and others. Rather, the seven attributes are: knowledge, will, life, power, speech, hearing, and sight. There is no dispute among them regarding life and power. The dispute is only in three: speech, will, and knowledge. And hearing and sight are included in the meaning of knowledge for those who affirm them. These three attributes must be temporal, and then it is impossible for them to subsist in other than Him, because it (that other) would not be qualified by them. So they must subsist in His essence, and consequently He becomes a locus of temporal events. As for knowledge of temporal events, Jahm went to the view that they are temporal knowledges. That is because God now knows that the world existed before this. If in eternity He knew that it existed, then that would be ignorance, not knowledge. And if He was not knowing, and now He is knowing, then the temporality of the knowledge that the world was brought into existence before this becomes apparent. And the same applies to every temporal event. As for will, it must be temporal; for if it were eternal, the object of will would be simultaneous with it. For when power and will are complete and obstacles are removed, the existence of the object of will becomes necessary. So how could the object of will be delayed from will and power without obstacle? Hence the Mu'tazila said that will is temporal in a non-locus, and the Karramiyya said it is temporal in His essence, sometimes expressing it as: He creates existence in His essence at the existence of every existent. And that refers to will. As for speech, how can it be eternal when it contains reports about the past? How did He say in eternity: 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people' when He had not yet created Noah? And how did He say in eternity to Moses: 'Take off your sandals' when He had not yet created Moses? And how did He command and forbid without anyone commanded or forbidden? If that is impossible, and it is known necessarily that He is a commander and prohibitor, and that is impossible in eternity, then it is known decisively that He became a commander and prohibitor after not being. So there is no meaning to Him being a locus of temporal events other than this? The answer: We say: Whenever we resolve the doubt concerning these three attributes, an independent proof arises that invalidates Him being a locus of temporal events. For no one has adopted that [view] except due to these doubts. So when they are dispelled, that statement becomes false, like the statement that He is a locus of colors and other things for which there is no evidence of being qualified by them. So we say: Repelling the doubt of temporality of the Exalted God: In eternity He knew the existence of the world at the time of its existence, and this knowledge is eternal knowledge.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the full objection of the opponent (likely Mu'tazila and Jahm) regarding three divine attributes—knowledge, will, and speech—which they argue must be temporal, and then Imam Ghazali's initial response. The objection begins by narrowing the dispute to three attributes: knowledge, will, and speech (with hearing and sight subsumed under knowledge). The opponent argues: (1) Knowledge of temporal events must be temporal because God knows now that the world existed after it did not; if He had known that eternally, it would be ignorance; but now He knows, so His knowledge is temporal. (2) Will must be temporal because if eternal, its object (the creation of the world) would have to exist simultaneously, but it does not. (3) Speech cannot be eternal because it contains past-tense reports and commands for beings that did not yet exist, which would be impossible; hence speech must be temporal. The opponent concludes that these attributes subsist in God's essence, making Him a locus of temporal events. Ghazali's answer begins by asserting that once the doubts about these three attributes are dismantled, the entire claim that God is a locus of temporal events collapses. He then provides the response to the doubt about knowledge: God's eternal knowledge of the world includes knowledge of its temporal existence at the appropriate time; it is not ignorance but perfect knowledge. The response to will and speech is not fully given in this passage, but the structure shows his method.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the opponent's argument is simply that attributes must be temporal because they are related to temporal objects.
Correction: The opponent's argument is more specific: they argue that the attributes themselves must be temporal because their content changes (knowledge), their object is delayed (will), or they contain temporal references (speech).

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's argument, why must God's speech be temporal?
A) Because speech is an accident
B) Because it contains past-tense reports and commands for non-existent beings
C) Because God did not always speak
D) Because speech is created

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that knowledge, will, and speech must be temporal because their objects or contents are temporal, making God a locus of temporal events.

The opponent claims that if God's will were eternal, the world would have existed eternally. Have you ever silently wondered why God created the world when He did, rather than earlier? What does this lesson challenge you to trust about the divine will?

Ghazali's Initial Response: Resolving the Doubt About Knowledge
Ghazali begins his response by asserting that resolving the doubts about these attributes invalidates the claim that God is a locus of temporal events, and then answers the knowledge objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's method for refuting the objections against the eternity of divine attributes, starting with the knowledge objection.

Core Idea
Ghazali argues that once the doubts about the three attributes are resolved, the entire claim that God is a locus of temporal events collapses.

Imam Ghazali now responds to the objections. He first asserts that the whole claim that God is a locus of temporal events is built solely on these three doubts. He then begins to dismantle them by addressing the argument about knowledge. Let's see how he defends the eternity of God's knowledge while accounting for temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ghazali's strategy
Ghazali says that the only reason people say God is a locus of temporal events is due to these three doubts. If these doubts are removed, the claim becomes baseless.
He compares it to saying God is a locus of colors, for which there is no evidence.
2
Focus on the knowledge response
Ghazali states that God's eternal knowledge includes knowledge of the world's existence at the time it exists. This knowledge is eternal, not temporal.
The key is that eternal knowledge can know temporal facts without changing.
3
Reflect on the implication
If God knows in eternity that the world will exist at a certain time, His knowledge is not ignorance; it is perfect knowledge of a future event.
This refutes the opponent's claim that if God knew the world existed before, that would be ignorance.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Ghazali's response here sets the pattern for answering the objections about will and speech later.
The passage only gives the answer for knowledge; the rest are implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الأعراض كثيرة، والخصم لا يدعي كونَ الباري سبحانه محلاً لجميعها؛ فلا نزاع في حدوث شيء منها؛ كالألوان والأكوان والآلام واللذات وغيرها، وإنما الصفات السبع وهي العلم والإرادة والحياة والقدرة والكلام والسمع والبصر، ولا نزاع من جملتها في الحياة والقدرة، وإنما النزاع في ثلاثة: الكلام والإرادة والعلم، وفي معنى العلم السمع والبصر عند من يثبتهما، وهذه الصفات الثلاثة لا بدَّ أن تكون حادثة، ثم يستحيل أن تقوم بغيره؛ لأنه لا يكون متصفاً بها.. فيجب أن تقوم بذاته؛ فيلزم منه كونه محلاً للحوادث: أما العلم بالحوادث.. فقد ذهب جهم إلى أنها علوم حادثة؛ وذلك لأن الله تعالى الآن عالم بأن العالم كان قد وجد قبل هذا، فهو في الأزل إن كان عالماً بأنه كان قد وجد.. كان هذا جهلاً لا علماً، وإذا لم يكن عالماً وهو الآن عالم.. فقد ظهر حدوث العلم بأن العالم كان قد أوجد قبل هذا، وكذا القول في كل حادث. وأما الإرادة.. فلا بدَّ من حدوثها؛ فإنها لو كانت قديمة.. لكان المراد معها؛ فإن القدرة والإرادة مهما تمَّتا وارتفعت العوائق.. وجب حصول المراد، فكيف يتأخَّرُ المراد عن الإرادة والقدرة من غير عائق؟ فلهذا قالت المعتزلة بحدوث إرادة في غير محل، وقالت الكرامية بحدوثها في ذاته، وربما عبّروا عنه بأنه يخلق إيجاداً في ذاته عند وجودِ كلَّ موجود، وهذا راجع إلى الإرادة. وأما الكلام.. فكيف يكون قديماً وفيه إخبار عما مضى؟ فكيف قال في الأزل: (إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ) ولم يكن قد خلق نوحاً بعد؟ وكيف قال في الأزل لموسى: (فَأَخْلَعْ نَعْلَيْكَ) ولم يخلق بعد موسى؟ وكيف أمر ونهى من غير مأمور ولا منهي؟ فإذا كان ذلك محالاً ثم عُلِمَ بالضرورة أنَّه أمر ناه واستحالَ ذلك في القدم.. عُلِمَ قطعاً أنه صار أمراً ناهياً بعد أن لم يكن، فلا معنى لكونه محلاً للحوادث إلا هذا؟ والجواب: أنّنا نقول: مهما حلَلْنا الشبهة في هذه الصفات الثلاث.. انتهضَ منه دليلٌ مستقل على إبطال كونه محلاً للحوادث؛ إذ لم يذهب إليه أحد إلا بسبب هذه الشبهة، فإذا انكشفت.. كان القول بها باطلاً كالقول بأنه محل للألوان وغيرها، مما لا يدلُّ دليل على الاتصاف بها، فنقول: دفع شبهة حدوث الباري تعالى في الأزل عَلِمَ بوجود العالم في وقت وجوده، وهذا العلم العلم القديم
For it was said: Accidents are many, and the opponent does not claim that the Exalted God is a locus for all of them; so there is no dispute in the temporality of some of them, such as colors, spatialities, pains, pleasures, and others. Rather, the seven attributes are: knowledge, will, life, power, speech, hearing, and sight. There is no dispute among them regarding life and power. The dispute is only in three: speech, will, and knowledge. And hearing and sight are included in the meaning of knowledge for those who affirm them. These three attributes must be temporal, and then it is impossible for them to subsist in other than Him, because it (that other) would not be qualified by them. So they must subsist in His essence, and consequently He becomes a locus of temporal events. As for knowledge of temporal events, Jahm went to the view that they are temporal knowledges. That is because God now knows that the world existed before this. If in eternity He knew that it existed, then that would be ignorance, not knowledge. And if He was not knowing, and now He is knowing, then the temporality of the knowledge that the world was brought into existence before this becomes apparent. And the same applies to every temporal event. As for will, it must be temporal; for if it were eternal, the object of will would be simultaneous with it. For when power and will are complete and obstacles are removed, the existence of the object of will becomes necessary. So how could the object of will be delayed from will and power without obstacle? Hence the Mu'tazila said that will is temporal in a non-locus, and the Karramiyya said it is temporal in His essence, sometimes expressing it as: He creates existence in His essence at the existence of every existent. And that refers to will. As for speech, how can it be eternal when it contains reports about the past? How did He say in eternity: 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people' when He had not yet created Noah? And how did He say in eternity to Moses: 'Take off your sandals' when He had not yet created Moses? And how did He command and forbid without anyone commanded or forbidden? If that is impossible, and it is known necessarily that He is a commander and prohibitor, and that is impossible in eternity, then it is known decisively that He became a commander and prohibitor after not being. So there is no meaning to Him being a locus of temporal events other than this? The answer: We say: Whenever we resolve the doubt concerning these three attributes, an independent proof arises that invalidates Him being a locus of temporal events. For no one has adopted that [view] except due to these doubts. So when they are dispelled, that statement becomes false, like the statement that He is a locus of colors and other things for which there is no evidence of being qualified by them. So we say: Repelling the doubt of temporality of the Exalted God: In eternity He knew the existence of the world at the time of its existence, and this knowledge is eternal knowledge.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents the full objection of the opponent (likely Mu'tazila and Jahm) regarding three divine attributes—knowledge, will, and speech—which they argue must be temporal, and then Imam Ghazali's initial response. The objection begins by narrowing the dispute to three attributes: knowledge, will, and speech (with hearing and sight subsumed under knowledge). The opponent argues: (1) Knowledge of temporal events must be temporal because God knows now that the world existed after it did not; if He had known that eternally, it would be ignorance; but now He knows, so His knowledge is temporal. (2) Will must be temporal because if eternal, its object (the creation of the world) would have to exist simultaneously, but it does not. (3) Speech cannot be eternal because it contains past-tense reports and commands for beings that did not yet exist, which would be impossible; hence speech must be temporal. The opponent concludes that these attributes subsist in God's essence, making Him a locus of temporal events. Ghazali's answer begins by asserting that once the doubts about these three attributes are dismantled, the entire claim that God is a locus of temporal events collapses. He then provides the response to the doubt about knowledge: God's eternal knowledge of the world includes knowledge of its temporal existence at the appropriate time; it is not ignorance but perfect knowledge. The response to will and speech is not fully given in this passage, but the structure shows his method.
