The Impossibility of Eternal Commands to Non-Existent Beings
Ghazali objects to eternal speech using the impossibility of eternal commands to non-existent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the attribute of speech cannot be eternal because it contains commands and reports directed at beings who did not yet exist.

Core Idea
Eternal speech would require commanding non-existent beings, which is impossible.

Ghazali presents a powerful objection against the notion of an eternal divine speech. Using concrete Quranic examples, he demonstrates that eternal speech would contain references to events and commands that only make sense in a temporal context. This sets up the key problem: if speech is eternal, how can it refer to temporal realities?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali uses rhetorical questions to build his case.
The passage is a series of 'how could...' statements.
2
Identify the two Quranic examples
Find the examples of Noah and Moses. What is the problem with these statements being eternal?
Both examples involve commands/reports about individuals before their existence.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Follow Ghazali's reasoning: if eternal commands are impossible, and God is necessarily a commander, then commanding must have begun after not existing.
This implies the speech attribute is temporal, not eternal.
4
Reflect on the final point
Consider the last sentence: 'What meaning is there for being a locus of temporal events other than this?' This ties the argument back to the concept of 'mahall al-hawadith'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام .. فكيف يكون قديماً وفيه إخبار عما مضى؟ فكيف قال في الأزل: ( إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ ) ولم يكن قد خلق نوحاً بعد؟ وكيف قال في الأزل لموسى: ( فَأَخْلَعْ نَعْلَيْكَ ) ولم يخلق بعد موسى؟ وكيف أمر ونهى من غير مأمور ولا منهي؟ فإذا كان ذلك محالاً ثم عُلِمَ بالضرورة أنَّه أمر ناه واستحالَ ذلك في القدم .. عُلِمَ قطعاً أنه صار أمراً ناهياً بعد أن لم يكن، فلا معنى لكونه محلاً للحوادث إلا هذا؟
As for speech, how can it be eternal while it contains reports about what has passed? How could He say in eternity: 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people' when Noah had not yet been created? And how could He say in eternity to Moses: 'Take off your sandals' when Moses had not yet been created? And how could He command and forbid when there was no one commanded nor forbidden? So if that is impossible, and it is known necessarily that He is a commander and prohibiter, and that was impossible in pre-eternity, then it is known conclusively that He became a commander and prohibiter after not having been. So what meaning is there for being a locus of temporal events (mahall al-hawadith) other than this?
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the eternity of the divine attribute of speech (kalam). Ghazali uses Quranic examples to show that eternal speech would contain reports of past events (like sending Noah) and commands to specific individuals (like Moses removing his sandals) at a time when those individuals did not yet exist. Such statements would be meaningless or false if eternal. He argues that since it is necessarily known that God is indeed a commander and prohibiter, and that could not be the case in pre-eternity (when no one existed to command), then God's attribute of commanding must have come to be after not being. Thus the speech attribute is temporal. Ghazali ends by noting that if one denies that God is a locus of temporal events, this very reasoning shows that the attribute itself is temporal.

محل الحوادث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that eternal speech could be 'all at once' without temporal reference, so commands to non-existent beings are not problematic.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the specific words (like 'We sent Noah') intrinsically refer to temporal events, making eternal speech impossible.

Key Takeaway

When you hear a prohibition in the Quran, do you immediately submit, or do you delay? What does your response reveal about whether you truly believe God is commanding you here and now?

The Eternity of Divine Knowledge: Answering the Objection
Ghazali refutes the doubt about knowledge and proves its eternity through analogy and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the eternity of God's knowledge by showing that one unchanging attribute can encompass all temporal events, using analogy and logical proof.

Core Idea
One eternal knowledge covers past, present, and future without change.

In this passage, Ghazali provides a detailed refutation of the objection that divine attributes must be temporal if they relate to temporal events. He focuses on knowledge (al-ilm), demonstrating that a single unchanging knowledge can know the world's past, present, and future states. He then extends the reasoning to hearing and sight. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for the doctrine of eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a general answer, then explanation, example, analogy, and final proof.
The Arabic uses technical terms; don't get stuck on every word.
2
Identify the core claim
Ghazali asserts that God's knowledge is one attribute that does not change, though its objects (temporal events) change. Find where he states this clearly.
Look for 'وهذا العلم صفة واحدة'.
3
Examine the human analogy
He gives an example of a person who continuously knows Zayd's arrival. Work through the analogy: before, during, and after sunrise. How does it mirror God's knowledge?
This analogy is crucial for understanding the argument.
4
Follow the logical proof
Ghazali argues that differences in states of one thing are not greater than differences between distinct essences. Since one knowledge can cover many essences, it can cover many states. Trace this reasoning.
He also references Jahm's position to strengthen his point.
5
Apply to hearing and sight
Ghazali briefly extends the same reasoning to divine hearing (sam') and sight (basar). Note how the principle remains the same: the attribute is eternal, only the perceived objects are temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أنّنا نقول : مهما حلَلْنا الشبهة في هذه الصفات الثلاث .. انتهضَ منه دليلٌ مستقل على إبطال كونه محلاً للحوادث؛ إذ لم يذهب إليه هذه الشبهة، فإذا انكشفت .. كان القول بها باطلاً كالقول ذاهب إلا بسبب بأنه محل للألوان وغيرها، مما لا يدلُّ دليل على الاتصاف بها، فنقول : دفع شبهة حدوث الباري تعالى في الأزل عَلِمَ بوجود العالم في وقت وجوده، وهذا العلم صفةٌ واحدة؛ مقتضاها في الأزل: العلم بأن العالم يكون من بعد، وعند الوجود: العلم بأنه كائنٌ، وبعده: العلم بأنه كان، وهذه الأحوال تتعاقب على العالم ويكون مكشوفاً الله تعالى بتيك الصفة، وهي لم تتغير، وإنما المتغير أحوال العالم. وإيضاحه بمثال: وهو أنا إذا فرضنا للواحد منا علماً بقدوم زيد عند طلوع الشمس، وحصل له هذا العلم قبل الطلوع ولم ينعدم، بل بقي ولم يخلق له علم آخر عند الطلوع .. فما حال هذا الشخص عند الطلوع؛ أيكون بقدوم زيد أو غير عالم؟ ومحال أن يكون غير عالم؛ لأنه قدرَ بقاءُ العلم بالقدوم عند الطلوع، وقد علم الآن الطلوع؛ فيلزمه بالضرورة أن يكون عالماً بالقدوم، فلو دام عند انقضاء الطلوع .. فلا بد أن يكون عالماً بأنه كان قد قدم، والعلم الواحد أفادَ الإحاطة بأنه سيكون وأنه كائن وأنه قد كان؛ فهكذا ينبغي أن يُفهم علم الله القديمُ الموجب للإحاطة بالحوادث، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يقاس السمع والبصرُ، فإنَّ كلَّ واحد منهما صفةٌ يتضحُ بها المرئي والمسموع عند الوجود من غير حدوث تيك الصفة ولا حدوث أمر فيها، وإنما الحادث المسموع والمرئي. والدليل القاطع على هذا: أن الاختلاف بين أحوال شيء واحد في انقسامه إلى الذي كان ويكون وهو كائن.. لا يزيد على الاختلاف بين الذوات المختلفة، ومعلوم أن العلم لا يتعدَّدُ بتعدد الذوات، فكيف يتعدد بتعدد أحوالِ ذات واحدة؟ وإذا جاز علم واحدٌ يفيد الإحاطة بذوات مختلفة متباينة .. فمن أين يستحيل أن يكونَ علم واحد يفيد إحاطة بأحوال ذات واحدة بالإضافة إلى الماضي والمستقبل؟ ولا شك في أن جهماً ينفي النهاية عن معلومات الله تعالى، ثم لا يثبت علوماً لا نهاية لها؛ فيلزمه أن يعترف بعلم واحد يتعلق بمعلومات مختلفة، فكيف يستبعد ذلك في أحوال معلوم واحد؟
The answer: We say: whenever we resolve the doubt concerning these three attributes, an independent proof emerges for invalidating His being a locus for temporal events; for this doubt did not lead to that [position]. So when it is removed, the affirmation [of temporality] becomes false, just as the affirmation [of His being a locus for accidents like colors] is false, since there is no proof for being described by them. So we say: Repelling the doubt concerning the temporality of the Creator – He knew, in pre-eternity, the existence of the world at the time of its existence, and this knowledge is one attribute; its implication in pre-eternity is knowledge that the world will be, at the time of existence is knowledge that it is happening, and after it is knowledge that it was. These states befall the world, and it is disclosed to God Most High by that attribute, which does not change; rather, the changing thing is the states of the world. Its clarification by an example: Suppose we assume for one of us knowledge of Zayd's arrival at sunrise, and this knowledge occurred before sunrise and did not cease, but remained, and no other knowledge was created for him at sunrise. Then what is the state of this person at sunrise? Is he knowledgeable of Zayd's arrival or not? It is impossible that he is not knowledgeable, because the knowledge of arrival persisted at sunrise, and he now knows the sunrise; therefore he necessarily knows the arrival. If it continues at the end of sunrise, then he must know that he had arrived. So one knowledge provided comprehension that he will be, that he is, and that he was. Thus should be understood God's eternal knowledge that necessitates comprehension of temporal events. And upon this, hearing and sight should be analogized; for each of them is an attribute by which the seen and the heard become clear at the time of existence, without the occurrence of that attribute or any occurrence in it; rather, what occurs is the heard and the seen. The decisive proof for this: that the difference between the states of one thing in its division into what was, will be, and is, does not exceed the difference between distinct essences. And it is known that knowledge does not multiply with the multiplicity of essences, so how could it multiply with the multiplicity of states of one essence? And if it is possible that one knowledge yields comprehension of different and distinct essences, then from where is it impossible that one knowledge yields comprehension of the states of one essence with respect to past and future? And there is no doubt that Jahm denies an end to God's objects of knowledge, yet does not posit infinite knowledges; so he must acknowledge one knowledge that relates to different objects of knowledge. So how could he consider it unlikely concerning the states of a single object of knowledge?
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the earlier objections about the temporality of divine attributes, focusing on knowledge (ilm) as the key example. Ghazali first states that by removing the doubt about knowledge, an independent proof emerges that God is not a locus for temporal events (mahall al-hawadith). Then he explains that God's knowledge is one eternal attribute that encompasses all temporal events without changing: in pre-eternity it knows the world will exist, at the moment of existence it knows it is existing, and after existence it knows it existed. The attribute remains the same; only the states of the world change. He illustrates with human knowledge: if a person knows before sunrise that Zayd will arrive at sunrise, and that knowledge persists, then at sunrise he knows Zayd is arriving, and after sunrise he knows Zayd arrived—all from one continuous knowledge. So God's single knowledge comprehends past, present, and future. He then extends this to hearing (sam') and sight (basar): they also are eternal attributes that perceive their objects at the time they occur, without the attributes themselves temporally occurring. Finally, Ghazali gives a logical proof: differences in states of one thing are not greater than differences between distinct essences. Since one knowledge can cover multiple distinct essences, it can certainly cover multiple states of one essence. He notes that even Jahm (Jahm ibn Safwan), who denies an end to God's objects of knowledge, does not posit infinite knowledges, so he must accept one knowledge relating to different objects; thus it is not far-fetched to apply the same to states of one object.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge changes with time, because it knows different things at different times.
Correction: Ghazali insists that knowledge itself is unchanging; only the objects known undergo temporal change. The same eternal knowledge encompasses all temporal phases.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how does God's single eternal knowledge relate to past, present, and future events?

B) God's knowledge changes as events occur.
C) One knowledge comprehends all temporal states without change.
D) God only knows events after they happen.

Key Takeaway
God's single eternal knowledge encompasses all temporal events without changing, as shown by analogy and logical proof.

God's Knowledge: One Attribute Encompassing All Time
Ghazali defines and illustrates divine knowledge as one eternal attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is a single eternal attribute that encompasses all temporal states without change.

Core Idea
One unchanging divine attribute covers past, present, and future.

Ghazali introduces a foundational concept in Islamic theology: God's knowledge is not multiple or changing. Instead, it is a single, eternal attribute that fully encompasses every event across time. This lesson unpacks his concise argument and human analogy to clarify how one knowledge can cover 'will be,' 'is,' and 'was.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of Ghazali's argument.
Notice the three temporal states: before existence, at existence, after existence.
2
Identify the three implications
Find where Ghazali lists what the one attribute implies in pre-eternity, at existence, and after existence.
These are: knowledge that something will be, that it is being, and that it was.
3
Work through the analogy
Consider the example of knowing Zayd's arrival at sunrise. See how one persistent knowledge covers future, present, and past.
This illustrates how God's knowledge works without change.
4
Extend to hearing and sight
Notice that Ghazali applies the same principle to divine hearing and sight: they are unchanging attributes that disclose temporal objects.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Summarize the main point: God's attributes do not change; only the objects of knowledge or perception change.
This guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة واحدة؛ مقتضاها في الأزل: العلم بأن العالم يكون من بعد، وعند الوجود: العلم بأنه كائن، وبعده: العلم بأنه كان، وهذه الأحوال تتعاقب على العالم ويكون مكشوفاً لله تعالى بتلك الصفة، وهي لم تتغير، وإنما المتغير أحوال العالم. وإيضاحه بمثال: وهو أنا إذا فرضنا للواحد منا علماً بقدوم زيد عند طلوع الشمس، وحصل له هذا العلم قبل الطلوع ولم ينعدم، بل بقي ولم يخلق له علم آخر عند الطلوع.. فما حال هذا الشخص عند الطلوع؛ أيكون بقدوم زيد أو غير عالم؟ ومحال أن يكون غير عالم؛ لأنه قدر بقاء العلم بالقدوم عند الطلوع، وقد علم الآن الطلوع؛ فيلزمه بالضرورة أن يكون عالماً بالقدوم، فلو دام عند انقضاء الطلوع.. فلا بد أن يكون عالماً بأنه كان قد قدم، والعلم الواحد أفاد الإحاطة بأنه سيكون وأنه كائن وأنه قد كان؛ فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم علم الله القديم الموجب للإحاطة بالحوادث. وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يقاس السمع والبصر، فإن كل واحد منهما صفة يتضح بها المرئي والمسموع عند الوجود من غير حدوث تلك الصفة ولا حدوث أمر فيها، وإنما الحادث المسموع والمرئي.
One attribute; its implication in pre-eternity is knowledge that the world will exist later, and at the time of existence: knowledge that it is existing, and afterwards: knowledge that it was. These states alternate upon the world, and it is disclosed to God Almighty by that attribute, and it does not change; only the states of the world change. Its clarification with an example: that we suppose for one of us knowledge of the arrival of Zayd at sunrise. This knowledge occurs before sunrise and does not cease, but remains, and no other knowledge is created for him at sunrise. So what is the state of this person at sunrise? Does he know of Zayd's arrival or not? It is impossible that he does not know, because he has the persistence of knowledge of arrival at sunrise, and he now knows the sunrise; thus necessarily he knows the arrival. If it persists at the end of sunrise, then he must know that he had arrived. One single knowledge provided encompassment that he will be, that he is being, and that he had been. This is how the eternal knowledge of God should be understood, which necessitates encompassment of events. And analogously should hearing and sight be understood; for each of them is an attribute by which the seen and the heard become evident at the time of existence, without that attribute occurring or any thing occurring in it; only the heard and the seen are originated.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage defines and explains God's knowledge as a single, eternal attribute that encompasses all temporal states—past, present, and future—without itself changing. Ghazali first states the three implications of that one attribute: in pre-eternity it is knowledge that the world will exist, at the moment of existence it is knowledge that it exists, and after existence it is knowledge that it existed. The attribute itself remains unchanged; only the states of the world change. To clarify, he provides a human analogy: if a person knows Zayd will arrive at sunrise, and that knowledge persists from before sunrise through sunrise and beyond, then one single knowledge covers the future ('will come'), the present ('is coming') and the past ('came'). This illustrates how God's eternal knowledge encompasses all temporal events. Ghazali then extends the same principle to hearing and sight: these attributes likewise disclose their objects at the time of existence without themselves undergoing change.

The Proof That One Knowledge Covers Multiple States
Ghazali provides a proof for the unity of divine knowledge across temporal states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that one divine knowledge can encompass all temporal states of an object, based on the principle that the difference among states is less than among distinct essences.

Core Idea
One knowledge covers all states of a thing because states differ less than essences.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a concise but powerful proof that God's knowledge is not multiplied by the multiplicity of temporal states. He argues by comparison: if a single knowledge can encompass entirely different essences, then it is even more fitting that it encompasses the varying states of a single essence. This refutes any claim that God's knowledge changes or is multiple due to past, present, and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is concise and relies on analogy.
2
Identify the key components
The passage has a premise (difference among states is not greater than among essences), a known fact (knowledge is not multiplied by essences), and an analogy (if one knowledge covers different essences, it can cover states).
Also note the refutation of Jahm's inconsistency.
3
Understand the refutation of Jahm
Ghazali points out that Jahm admits one knowledge for multiple objects of knowledge but denies it for states of one object. This is inconsistent.
Jahm here refers to Jahm ibn Safwan, associated with Mu'tazili-like positions.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof defends the unity and simplicity of God's knowledge. How does it refute the idea that God's knowledge changes with time?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل القاطع على هذا: أن الاختلاف بين أحوال شيء واحد في انقسامه إلى الذي كان ويكون وهو كائن.. لا يزيد على الاختلاف بين الذوات المختلفة، ومعلوم أن العلم لا يتعدد بتعدد الذوات، فكيف يتعدد بتعدد أحوال ذات واحدة؟ وإذا جاز علم واحد يفيد الإحاطة بذوات مختلفة متباينة.. فمن أين يستحيل أن يكون علم واحد يفيد إحاطة بأحوال ذات واحدة بالإضافة إلى الماضي والمستقبل؟ ولا شك في أن جهماً ينفي النهاية عن معلومات الله تعالى، ثم لا يثبت علوماً لا نهاية لها؛ فيلزمه أن يعترف بعلم واحد يتعلق بمعلومات مختلفة، فكيف يستبعد ذلك في أحوال معلوم واحد؟
And the conclusive proof of this: that the difference between states of one thing in its division into what was, will be, and is being... does not exceed the difference between different essences. And it is known that knowledge is not multiplied by the multiplicity of essences, so how can it be multiplied by the multiplicity of states of one essence? If a single knowledge that provides encompassment of different, distinct essences is permissible, then from where is it impossible that there be a single knowledge providing encompassment of the states of one essence in relation to the past and future? There is no doubt that Jahm negates finitude from God's objects of knowledge, yet he does not affirm infinite knowledges; so he must acknowledge a single knowledge related to different objects of knowledge. So how can that be deemed improbable concerning the states of a single object of knowledge?
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the unity of God's knowledge across different temporal states. Ghazali argues that the variation among states (past, present, future) of a single thing is not greater than the variation among different essences. Since it is accepted that one knowledge can cover multiple distinct essences (as even Jahm admits), then it is even more plausible that one knowledge covers multiple states of one essence. He refutes Jahm (a Mu'tazili figure) who denies that God's knowledge is infinite in number but admits one knowledge for different objects—so consistency requires accepting one knowledge for different states of the same object.

Refutation of the Claim That Knowledge Occurs
Ghazali refutes the objection that God's knowledge occurs with each event.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the claim that God's knowledge is temporally originated, and why it leads to absurd consequences like infinite regress or a single knowledge with two objects.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge occurred with each event, it would lead to either an infinite chain of knowledges or a single knowledge knowing two things at once.

Imam Ghazali continues his defense of God's eternal knowledge by tackling a key objection: if God knows events as they happen, does that mean His knowledge changes or occurs in time? In this passage, he shows that the very idea of 'occurring knowledge' unravels logically, forcing the objector into impossible positions. Read carefully, as the argument is tight and hinges on subtle distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation without trying to analyze deeply. Let the argument's flow register.
The passage is dense; reading it once through helps orientation.
2
Identify the conditional hypothesis
Notice that Ghazali starts with a conditional: 'if knowledge of every event occurs for Him.' This sets up a hypothetical scenario to be examined.
The entire refutation depends on what follows from this hypothesis.
3
Follow the branching logic
Trace the two main branches: the occurring knowledge is either known to God or not. Each branch leads to an impossibility. Under the 'known' branch, there are two sub-branches leading to infinite regress or one knowledge with two objects.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'إما أن يكون معلوماً أو غير معلوم' and then 'فإما أن يفتقر إلى علم آخر'.
4
Understand the conclusion
Ghazali ends by asserting that allowing one knowledge with two objects is no more problematic than what the opponent already concedes. Thus the objection fails.
The final rhetorical question underscores the inconsistency of the objector's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولكم بحدوث العلم يحققه: أنه لو حدث له علم بكل حادث.. لكان ذلك العلم يوجب العلم بالعلم لا يخلو: إما أن يكون معلوماً، أو غير معلوم. الحادث: فيلزم التسلسل أو الدور فإن لم يكن معلوماً.. فهو محال؛ لأنه حادث، وإن جاز حادث لا يعلمه مع أنه في ذاته وأولى بأن يكون متضحاً له.. فبأن يجوز ألا يعلم الحوادث المباينة لذاته أولى. وإن كان معلوماً: فإما أن يفتقر إلى علم آخر، وكذا العلم الآخر يفتقر إلى علوم أخر لا نهاية لها، وذلك محال، وإما أن يعلم الحادث والعلم بالحادث بنفس ذلك العلم، فتكون ذات العلم واحدة ولها معلومان: أحدهما: ذاته، والآخر: ذات الحادث؛ فيلزم منه ـ لا محالة ـ تجويز علم واحد يتعلق بمعلومين مختلفين، فكيف لا يجوز علم واحد متعلق بأحوال معلوم واحد مع اتحاد العلم وتنزهه عن التغير؟! وهذا ما لا مخرج منه
Your statement about the occurrence of knowledge [our reply] its verification: that if knowledge of every event occurs for Him, then that knowledge would necessitate knowledge of the knowledge. It cannot be but that it is either known or unknown. If the event [knowledge] is not known, it is impossible because it is an event. And if an event that He does not know is permissible, even though it is in His essence and more deserving to be evident to Him, then it is more fitting that He not know events distinct from His essence. And if it is known, then either it requires another knowledge, and likewise that other knowledge requires infinite knowledges, which is impossible; or the event and the knowledge of the event are known by the same knowledge, so the essence of the knowledge is one and it has two knowns: one is its own self, and the other is the essence of the event. This necessarily entails allowing one knowledge related to two different knowns. So why not allow one knowledge related to states of one known, given the unity of knowledge and its transcendence above change? This is inescapable.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage answers the objection that God's knowledge of events occurs (i.e., is originated in time) rather than being eternal. Ghazali begins by considering the hypothesis that a new knowledge occurs for each event. If so, that knowledge itself is an event, and must be either known to God or not known. If not known, that is absurd because God knows everything, especially what is in His own essence. If known, then either it requires another knowledge (leading to infinite regress) or it is known by the same knowledge that knows the event itself. If the latter, then one knowledge has two objects: itself and the event. This is no more problematic than the previously conceded point that one knowledge can cover multiple states of one thing. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
حدوث العلم
التسلسل
الدور
معلومان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's knowledge is one and eternal, it cannot include temporal events; but Ghazali argues that one knowledge can encompass multiple objects or states.
Correction: Ghazali shows that one knowledge can have two objects (itself and an event), so it can likewise include temporal events without changing.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali argue follows if God's knowledge occurs with each event?
A) That knowledge would be multiple
B) That it leads to infinite regress or one knowledge with two objects
C) That God would become ignorant
D) That events would be eternal

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the objection of 'occurring knowledge' leads to either an infinite regress or a single knowledge with two objects, neither of which undermines God's eternal knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you truly believed that Allah's knowledge encompasses every detail of your life, even the event you are reading now, without any change or addition, how would your anxiety about the future be affected? What specific worry can you surrender today because His knowledge is whole and unchanging?

In this passage, Ghazali points out that an event occurring in God's essence must be known to Him. ?
The opponent's view would require an infinite chain of knowledges, which is impossible. Consider your own justifications and excuses for your actions. Are there areas where you rely on endless reasoning to avoid taking responsibility? What is one excuse you will drop this week, trusting in Allah's single, complete knowledge of your state?

Divine Will and Refutation of the Karamiyya
Ghazali recaps the necessity of eternal will and refutes the Karamiyya on originated bringing-into-being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an originated will in God is impossible and how Ghazali refutes the Karamiyya's claim of an originated bringing-into-being.

Core Idea
God's will is eternal, not originated; otherwise infinite regress follows.

Ghazali continues his discussion of divine attributes by addressing the will. He first reiterates the impossibility of an originated will in God, which would lead to infinite regress. Then he turns to refute the Karamiyya sect, who claim that God originates a bringing-into-being in His essence at the moment of creation. This passage lays out the core argument and the beginning of Ghazali's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from divine will to the specific refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the three aspects of impossibility for the sound interpretation are not listed here.
2
Identify the two main sections
The passage first summarises the argument about divine will, then moves to refute the Karamiyya. Mark where the shift occurs.
3
Analyze the argument against originated will
Understand why an originated will is impossible: it would require either no cause (absurd) or another will (infinite regress). Hence God's will must be eternal.
This connects to the earlier proof that the world is created, not eternal.
4
Examine the refutation of the Karamiyya
The Karamiyya claim an originated bringing-into-being in God. Ghazali asks what specifies it to that time, leading to a regress of specifiers—the same problem as the Mu'tazila's originated will.
The refutation is partial; it also mentions a variant claim (the word 'Be' as a sound) which is impossible for three unlisted reasons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإرادة.. فقد ذكرنا أن حدوثها بغير إرادة أخرى محال، وحدوثها بإرادة يتسلسل إلى غير نهاية، وأن تعلق الإرادة القديمة بالأحداث غير محال، ويستحيل أن تتعلق الإرادة بالقديم، فلم يكن العالم قديماً؛ لأن الإرادة تعلقت بإحداثه لا بوجوده في القدم، وقد سبق إيضاح ذلك. وكذلك الكرامي إن قال: يُحدث في ذاته إيجاداً في حال حدوث العالم، فبذلك يحصل حدوث العالم في ذلك الوقت.. فيقال له: وما الذي خصص الإيجاد الحادث في ذاته بذلك الوقت؟ فيحتاج إلى مخصص آخر، فيلزمهم في الإيجاد ما لزم المعتزلة في الإرادة الحادثة. ومن قال منهم: إن ذلك الإيجاد هو قوله: (وكن) وهو صوت، فهو محال من ثلاثة أوجه:
As for will... we have mentioned that its occurrence without another will is impossible, and its occurrence by a will would lead to infinite regress, and that the attachment of the eternal will to events is not impossible, and it is impossible for will to be attached to the eternal. So the world was not eternal, because will was attached to its bringing into existence, not to its existence in pre-eternity. And that has been clarified before. Likewise, if the Karamiyya say: He brings into existence in His essence a bringing-into-being at the time of the world's origination, so thereby the emergence of the world occurs at that time... then it is said to him: What specified that originated bringing-into-being in His essence for that particular time? It would require another specifier, and thus they fall into what the Mu'tazila fell into concerning the originated will. And whoever among them says: That bringing-into-being is His word 'Be' (kun), which is a sound, then it is impossible from three aspects:
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage addresses divine will and refutes the Karamiyya sect. Ghazali first reiterates that an originated will (i.e., coming into existence in God) is impossible because it would require either no cause (absurd) or another will (infinite regress). The eternal will's attachment to temporal events is not impossible, and it cannot attach to the eternal, so the world is created, not eternal. The Karamiyya claim that God originates in His essence a bringing-into-being (إيجاد) at the moment of creation. Ghazali asks what specifies that bringing-into-being to that time, leading to a regress of specifiers—the same problem faced by the Mu'tazila regarding an originated will. Some Karamiyya say this bringing-into-being is the word 'Be' (كن), which is a sound. Ghazali begins to refute it from three aspects, but the text cuts off before enumerating them.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الكرامي
الإيجاد
التسلسل
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will could be originated at the moment of creation.
Correction: Ghazali shows that an originated will would require either no cause (impossible) or an infinite chain of wills, so God's will must be eternal.

Checkpoint
What problem does Ghazali point out in the Karamiyya's claim of an originated 'إيجاد' in God's essence?
A) It contradicts scripture
B) It requires a specifier for the time of occurrence, leading to regress
C) It implies God is composite
D) It denies God's power

Key Takeaway
God's will is eternal; any originated will leads to absurdity, and the Karamiyya's claim of an originated bringing-into-being suffers the same problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you living as if God's will for you is fixed and eternal, or as if He is constantly changing His plans based on your actions? What needs to change in your heart today to align with the reality of His eternal will?
?
The Karamiyya thought God's act of creation required a temporal addition to His essence. Do you sometimes act as if God needs to be 'activated' by your worship or repentance? How does Ghazali's correction free you from that error?
?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, then nothing happens apart from His decree. Does this make you feel a sense of security or a sense of fatalism? How will you respond to the next trial you face with this truth firmly in mind?

Three Objections Against the Pre-Eternity of Allah's Speech
Ghazali presents three objections against the pre-eternity of divine speech and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against the pre-eternity of Allah's speech and why Ghazali dismisses them as misunderstandings of divine power.

Core Idea
Divine speech is not sound or letters; it is a metaphor for absolute power.

Opponents of the pre-eternity of Allah's speech often mistakenly imagine it to be composed of sounds and letters like human speech. This leads to several logical absurdities, which Ghazali enumerates and then sharply refutes. In this lesson, we will examine these objections and see how Ghazali turns them back on the critics, exposing the depth of their confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's powerful rhetoric and logical progression come through.
Notice Ghazali's tone shifts from logical argument to sharp rebuke.
2
Identify the three objections
Find each objection: (1) sound cannot subsist in Allah's essence, (2) the command 'Be' leads to infinite regress, (3) addressing 'Be' to non-existence or existence is absurd.
Each objection targets the idea that divine speech is literal sound.
3
Understand Ghazali's refutation
Ghazali does not answer each objection separately; rather, he argues that the whole approach is misguided because divine speech is a metaphor for power. He dismisses the objections as stemming from lack of intellect.
He quotes the Qur'an to support his point.
4
Reflect on the key insight
The central insight is that divine attributes are not like human attributes. Attempting to apply physical constraints to Allah leads to absurdities.
This lesson guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدها : استحالة قيام الصوت بذاته
والآخر : أن قوله : (كُن) حادث أيضاً ، فإن حدث من غير أن يقول (كُن) .. فليحدث العالم من غير أن يقول له : (كُن) ، فإن افتقر قوله : (كُن) إلى قول آخر .. افتقرَ القولُ الآخرُ إلى ثالث ، والثالث إلى الرابع ، ويتسلسل إلى غير نهاية
ثم لا ينبغي أن يناظر من انتهى عقله إلى أن يقول : يحدث في ذاته بعدد كل حادث في كل وقتِ قوله : (كُن) ، فتجتمع آلاف آلاف أصوات في كل لحظة ، ومعلوم أن النون والكاف لا يمكن النطق بهما في وقت واحد ، بل أن تكون النون بعد الكاف ؛ لأن الجمع بين الحرفين محال ، وإن جمع ولم يرتب .. لم يكن قولاً مفهوماً ولا كلاماً ، وكما يستحيل الجمع بين حرفين مختلفين .. فكذلك بين حرفين متماثلين ، ولا يعقل في آن واحدٍ ألفُ ألفِ كافٍ كما لا يعقلُ الكاف والنون ، فهؤلاء حقهم أن يسترزقوا الله تعالى عقلاً ، فهو أهم لهم من الاشتغال بالنظر !
والثالث : أن قوله : (كُن) خطاب مع العالم في حالة العدم ، أو في حالة الوجود ؟ فإن كان في حالة العدم.. فالمعدوم لا يفهم الخطاب ، فكيف يمتثل بأن يتكوّن بقوله : (كُن) ؟ وإن كان في حالة الوجود.. فالكائن كيف يقال له : (كُن) ؟
فانظر ماذا يفعلُ الله تعالى بمن ضل عن سبيله ، فقد انتهى ركاكة عقولهم إلى أن لم يفهموا المعنيّ بقوله تعالى : {إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ فَيَكُونُ} وأنه كناية عن نفاذ القدرة وكمالها ، حتى انجرَّ بهم إلى هذه المخازي ، نعوذ بالله من الخزي والفضيحة يوم الفزع الأكبر ، يومَ تُكشفُ الضمائر ، وتبلى السرائر ، فيكشف إذ ذاك ستر الله تعالى عن خبائث الجهال ، ويقال للجاهل الذي اعتقد في الله تعالى وفي صفاته غير الرأي السديد : {لَقَدْ كُنْتَ فِي غَفْلَةٍ مِنْ هَذَا فَكَشَفْنَا عَنْكَ غِطَاءَكَ فَبَصَرُكَ الْيَوْمَ حَدِيدٌ}
One of them: the impossibility of sound subsisting in His essence. The other: that His saying 'Be' is also temporal, for if it occurs without His saying 'Be'... then let the world occur without His saying 'Be' to it; for if His saying 'Be' depends on another saying... then that other saying depends on a third, and the third on a fourth, and it chains infinitely. Then it is not fitting to argue with one whose intellect has ended up saying: He creates in His essence with every temporal event at every time His saying 'Be', so hundreds of thousands of sounds accumulate in every moment, and it is known that the 'nūn' and 'kāf' cannot be pronounced at one time, rather the 'nūn' must be after the 'kāf'; because combining two letters is impossible, and if they are combined without order... it would not be an understandable utterance nor speech. Just as combining two different letters is impossible... so too between two identical letters, and a hundred thousand 'kāf's cannot be conceived in one moment just as 'kāf' and 'nūn' cannot be conceived. These people deserve to ask Allah Almighty for intellect, for that is more important for them than occupying themselves with theoretical investigation! The third: that His saying 'Be' is an address to the world in a state of non-existence or in a state of existence? If it is in a state of non-existence... the non-existent does not understand speech, so how does it comply by becoming through His saying 'Be'? And if it is in a state of existence... how is the existent told 'Be'? So see what Allah Almighty does with those who stray from His path; their intellects have reached such stupidity that they do not understand the meaning of His saying 'When We intend something, We say to it 'Be' and it becomes' and that it is a metaphor for the penetration and perfection of power, until they have been dragged into these disgraceful things. We seek refuge with Allah from disgrace and scandal on the Day of the Great Terror, the day when consciences are disclosed and secrets are examined, then the veil of Allah Almighty will be removed from the foulnesses of the ignorant, and it will be said to the ignorant who believed about Allah Almighty and His attributes other than the correct opinion: 'Indeed, you were in heedlessness of this, so We have removed your cover from you, and your sight today is sharp.'
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents three objections that opponents raise against the doctrine that Allah's attribute of speech (kalam) is pre-eternal, assuming divine speech is composed of sound and letters. The first objection asserts that sound cannot subsist in Allah's essence, as sound is an accident that does not inhere in the Creator. The second objection argues that if the word 'Be' (kun) is a created thing, then there is an infinite regress: to create the world, Allah would need to say 'Be', but that utterance itself would need another 'Be', and so on. Ghazali adds a sarcastic remark about those who imagine multiple simultaneous utterances in Allah's essence, pointing out that letters like kāf and nūn cannot be pronounced together. The third objection questions the intelligibility of addressing 'Be' to a non-existent entity (which cannot hear) or to an existing entity (which already exists). Ghazali then rebukes the objectors harshly, stating that they fail to understand that the Qur'anic phrase 'When We intend something, We say to it 'Be' and it becomes' is a metaphor for the perfection of divine power, not a literal command. He warns of the disgrace they will face in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
كلام
كن
صوت
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is composed of sounds and letters like human speech, and thus must be temporal.
Correction: Divine speech is an attribute of Allah that is pre-eternal and not composed of physical elements; the Qur'anic expression 'Be' is a metaphor for immediate creation by will.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection against the pre-eternity of Allah's speech?
A) Sound cannot subsist in Allah's essence.
B) The word 'Be' leads to an infinite regress.
C) The word 'Be' is addressed to a non-existent being.
D) The word 'Be' implies temporality.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that objections to the pre-eternity of divine speech stem from a mistaken anthropomorphic view, and the true meaning is that Allah's power is absolute and beyond physical limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read Ghazali's warning about those who insist on a literal interpretation of 'Be'. Where in your own faith do you cling to a literal understanding that may limit Allah's transcendence? What would it cost you to surrender that literal image for a deeper trust in His absolute power?
?
Ghazali says these objectors 'deserve to ask Allah for intellect'. When you examine your own beliefs about divine attributes, are there areas where you rely on human analogies rather than accepting that Allah's speech is beyond our comprehension? How can you actively train your mind to transcend anthropomorphic thinking?
?
The objection about addressing the non-existent reveals a failure to grasp that Allah's power creates instantly. Reflect: Do you ever doubt that Allah's command is sufficient for your own needs, waiting for visible signs before trusting? What is one area of your life where you need to believe in the power of 'Be' without demanding prior evidence?

Divine Speech as Eternal Attribute of Meaning
Ghazali refutes objections by distinguishing between audible and internal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute of meaning (kalam nafs), not temporal sounds or letters.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal meaning, not a temporal sound.

Ghazali tackles a common objection: how can verses like 'So take off your sandals' or 'We sent Noah' be eternal when they refer to specific events? He clarifies that divine speech is not composed of sounds and letters but is an eternal attribute of meaning, called 'speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs). This passage lays out the distinction and defends the pre-eternity of Allah's speech through clear reasoning and a relatable example.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly. Notice how Ghazali moves from stating his position to addressing objections and then to explaining the nature of command.
The passage is dense; don't worry if you don't grasp everything at first.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where Ghazali distinguishes between audible speech (sound) and internal speech (kalam nafs). This is the core of his defense.
He explicitly says the objection is based on equating speech with sound, which is impossible for Allah.
3
Analyze the example of the report about Noah
Ghazali uses the report of sending Noah to show that the meaning is eternal even though the wording changes from 'We are sending' to 'We sent'.
This illustrates that the underlying meaning does not vary with time.
4
Understand the example of the father and child
Ghazali gives a detailed analogy: a father can have an eternal demand in his soul for his child to seek knowledge, even before the child exists. This shows commands can be eternal.
This analogy is crucial for grasping how Allah's commands are eternal.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Ghazali's argument matters: it preserves the divine attribute of speech as eternal and unchangeable, consistent with Allah's perfection.
This connects to the broader Islamic creed about Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام.. فهو قديم ، وما استبعدوه من قوله تعالى : {فَاخْلَعْ نَعْلَيْكَ} ، وقوله سبحانه : {إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ} .. استبعاد مستنده تقديرهم الكلام صوتاً ، وهو محال فيه ، وليس بمحال إذا فُهِمَ كلامُ النفس ؛ فإنا نقول : قام بذات الله تعالى خبر عن إرسال نوح ؛ العبارة عنه قبل إرساله : (إنا نرسله) ، وبعد إرساله : (إنا أرسلنا) ، فاللفظ يختلفُ باختلاف الأحوال ، والمعنى القائم بذاته تعالى لا يختلف ؛ فإن حقيقته أنه خبر متعلق بمخيرٍ ، ذلك الخبرُ هو إرسال نوح في الوقت المعلوم ، وذلك لا يختلف باختلاف الأحوال كما سبق في العلم ، وكذلك قوله تعالى {فَاخْلَعْ نَعْلَيْكَ} لفظ يدلُّ على أمر ، والأمر اقتضاء وطلب يقوم بذات الأمر ، وليس من شرط قيامه به أن يكون المأمور موجوداً ، ولكن يجوز أن يقوم بذاته قبل وجود المأمور ، فإذا وُجد المأمور .. كان مأموراً بذلك الاقتضاء بعينه من غير تجدُّدِ اقتضاء آخر
وكم من شخص ليس له ولد ويقوم بذاته اقتضاء طلب العلم منه على تقدير وجوده ؛ إذ يقدِّرُ في نفسه أن يقول لولده : اطلب العلم ، وهذا الاقتضاء يتنجز في نفسه على تقدير الوجود ، فلو وُجِدَ الولد ، وخلق له عقل ، وخلق له علم بما في نفس الأب من غير تقدير صياغة لفظ مسموع ، وقدر بقاء ذلك الاقتضاء إلى وجوده.. لعلم الابن أنه مأمور من جهة الأب بطلب العلم من غير استئناف اقتضاء متجدد في النفس ، بل يبقى ذلك الاقتضاء
نعم ؛ العادة جاريةٌ بأن الابن لا يحدث له علم إلا بلفظ يدلُّ على الاقتضاء الباطن ، فيكون قوله بلسانه : (اطلب العلم) دلالة على الاقتضاء الذي في ذاته ، سواء حدث في الوقت أو كان قائماً بذاته قبل وجود ولده
As for speech... it is eternal. And what they found improbable from His saying 'So take off your sandals' and His saying 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people'... its improbability is based on their assumption that speech is sound, which is impossible with respect to Him, but it is not impossible when understood as speech of the soul (kalam al-nafs). For we say: there subsists in the essence of Allah Almighty a report about the sending of Noah; the expression concerning it before his sending is 'We are sending him', and after his sending is 'We sent him'. So the wording varies according to circumstances, but the meaning subsisting in His essence does not vary. For its reality is that it is a report related to something reported, that report being the sending of Noah at the known time, and that does not vary with the variation of circumstances, as previously in knowledge. Likewise His saying 'So take off your sandals' is wording that indicates a command. And the command is a demand (iqtida') and request that subsists in the essence of the commander. It is not a condition of its subsisting in him that the commanded person exist. Rather, it is permissible for it to subsist in his essence before the existence of the commanded person. Then when the commanded person exists, he is commanded by that same demand without any renewal of another demand. How many a person who has no child yet there subsists in his essence a demand for seeking knowledge from him on the assumption of his existence, such that he envisions in himself saying to his child 'Seek knowledge'! This demand is actualized in his soul on the assumption of existence. So if the child came to exist, and intellect was created for him, and knowledge was created for him of what is in the father's soul without assuming the formulation of an audible word, and that demand remained until his existence, then the son would know that he is commanded from the father's side to seek knowledge without a new demand renewed in the soul. Rather, that demand persists. Yes, the custom is that the child does not come to know except through a word that indicates the inner demand. So his statement with his tongue 'Seek knowledge' is an indication of the demand that is in his essence, whether it occurred at the time or was subsisting in his essence before the existence of his child.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defends the pre-eternity of Allah's speech by clarifying that divine speech is not composed of sounds and letters but is an eternal attribute of meaning, called 'speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs). Ghazali addresses the objection that verses like 'So take off your sandals' and 'We sent Noah' imply temporality, because they refer to specific events. He explains that these are expressions of a single eternal meaning: the report about sending Noah is one eternal meaning; the wording changes ('We are sending' vs. 'We sent') but the underlying reality does not. Similarly, a command (amr) is a demand (iqtida') or request that subsists in the commander's soul. It does not require the existence of the commanded person; it can be eternal in the soul. Ghazali gives an example: a father who has no child can still have in his soul a demand for his child to seek knowledge, on the assumption of the child's existence. If the child later comes into existence, the child is commanded by that same eternal demand without needing a new demand. The spoken words are merely indicators (dalalat) of that internal demand, which is the essence of the command. Thus, Allah's commands and reports are eternal attributes of meaning, not temporal sounds.

Key Terms
كلام النفس
اقتضاء
خبر
أمر
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that divine speech must be temporal because it addresses specific events in time.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the meaning (kalam nafs) is eternal and unchanging; only the wording varies. The example of the father's eternal command illustrates how a command can be pre-eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the nature of Allah's speech?
A) It is composed of sounds and letters.
B) It is an eternal attribute of meaning (kalam nafs).
C) It is created in time.
D) It is identical with Allah's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal attribute of meaning, not temporal sounds—as clarified by Ghazali's distinction between kalam nafs and audible expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah’s command to you existed before you were born. Does this change how you view the obligation to obey? What command of Allah have you been neglecting, thinking it applies to others or is outdated?
?
The father’s demand for his future child—do you treat Allah’s commands as if they come from a distant past or as if they are directed at you right now? What would change if you truly believed that His speech is eternally addressing you?
?
Ghazali uses the analogy of a father's inner demand. Have you ever felt a nagging sense of obligation from Allah without hearing an audible voice? How do you respond to that inner call?

The Eternity of Allah's Speech: Meaning vs. Expression
Ghazali explains that Allah's speech is eternal, not composed of sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali distinguishes between eternal meaning and created expressions in divine speech, and why this distinction resolves doubts about the origination of Allah's speech.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is an eternal meaning, not created sounds.

Ghazali here refutes the doubt that Allah's speech might be originated by clarifying its true nature. He argues that speech is not sound but a meaning subsisting in the self (kalam nafsi), which is eternal. The expressions we read in the Quran are created indications of that eternal meaning. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Begin by noting that Ghazali opens by stating that speech (الكلام) is eternal (قديم). This is the thesis he will defend against the doubt of origination.
The doubt arises from Quranic verses that seem to imply change or temporality.
2
Understand the doubt
The objectors point to verses like 'Take off your shoes' and 'We sent Noah' as evidence that speech changes. Ghazali explains that their assumption is that speech is sound, which would be impossible for Allah.
3
Grasp the distinction between meaning and expression
Ghazali introduces the concept of 'speech of the self' (كلام النفس). The meaning (المعنى) in Allah's essence is eternal and does not change, while the expressions (الألفاظ) vary according to circumstances.
The example of news about sending Noah: the expression changes tense, but the eternal meaning remains the same.
4
Follow the analogy of command
A command (الأمر) is a demand subsisting in the commander's essence. It does not require the existence of the commanded. Ghazali gives the analogy of a father who has a demand for his future son to seek knowledge.
This shows that a demand can exist before the commanded person exists.
5
Conclude: expressions are created, meaning is eternal
The spoken expressions are merely indications of the inner demand. They are created (حادثة), while the indicated meaning (المدلول) is eternal (قديم). This resolves the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع شبهة حدوث وأما الكلام.. فهو قديم ، وما استبعدوه من قوله تعالى : ﴿ فَأَخْلَع الكلام القديم نعليك ) ، وقوله سبحانه : (إِنَّا أَرْسَلْنَا نُوحًا إِلَى قَوْمِهِ ) .. استبعاد مستنده تقديرهم الكلام صوتاً ، وهو محال فيه ، وليس بمحال إذا فُهِمَ كلامُ النفس ؛ فإنا نقول : قام بذات الله تعالى خبر عن إرسال نوح ؛ العبارة عنه قبل إرساله : ( إنا نرسله ) ، وبعد إرساله : ( إنا أرسلنا ) ، فاللفظ يختلفُ باختلاف الأحوال ، والمعنى القائم بذاته تعالى لا يختلف ؛ فإن حقيقته أنه خبر متعلق بمخيرٍ ، ذلك الخبرُ هو إرسال نوح في الوقت المعلوم ، وذلك لا يختلف باختلاف الأحوال كما سبق في العلم ، وكذلك قوله تعالى فاخلع نعليك ) لفظ يدلُّ على أمر ، والأمر اقتضاء وطلب يقوم بذات الأمر ، وليس من شرط قيامه به أن يكون المأمور موجوداً ، ولكن يجوز أن يقوم بذاته قبل وجود المأمور ، فإذا وُجد المأمور .. كان مأموراً بذلك الاقتضاء بعينه من غير تجدُّدِ اقتضاء آخر وكثير من الناس ليس له ولد ويقوم بذاته اقتضاء طلب العلم منه على تقدير وجوده ؛ إذ يقدِّرُ في نفسه أن يقول لولده : اطلب العلم ، وهذا الاقتضاء يتنجز في نفسه على تقدير الوجود ، فلو وُجِدَ الولد ، وخلق له عقل ، وخلق له علم بما في نفس الأب من غير تقدير صياغة لفظ مسموع ، وقدر بقاء ذلك الاقتضاء إلى وجوده.. لعلم الابن أنه مأمور من جهة الأب بطلب العلم من غير استئناف اقتضاء متجدد في النفس ، بل يبقى ذلك الاقتضاء نعم ؛ العادة جاريةٌ بأن الابن لا يحدث له علم إلا بلفظ يدلُّ على الاقتضاء الباطن ، فيكون قوله بلسانه : اطلب العلم ) دلالة على الاقتضاء الذي في ذاته ، سواء حدث في الوقت أو كان قائماً بذاته قبل وجود ولده ؛ فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم قيام الأمر بذات الله تعالى ، فتكون الألفاظ الدالة عليه حادثة والمدلول قديماً
Refuting the doubt of origination: As for speech, it is eternal. And what they considered unlikely from His statement: 'Take off your shoes' and His statement: 'Indeed, We sent Noah to his people' — their unlikelihood is based on their assumption that speech is sound, which is impossible of Him. But it is not impossible if understood as speech of the self. For we say: There subsists in the essence of Allah, Exalted, an information about the sending of Noah; the expression before his sending is 'We will send him', and after his sending is 'We sent him'. So the utterance differs according to circumstances, but the meaning subsisting in His essence does not differ. For its reality is that it is an information relating to something informed about, that information being the sending of Noah at the known time, and that does not differ according to circumstances, as preceded in the case of knowledge. Similarly, His statement 'Take off your shoes' is an expression indicating a command, and a command is a demand and request that subsists in the essence of the commander. It is not a condition of its subsistence in Him that the commanded person exist. Rather, it is permissible that it subsist in His essence before the existence of the commanded person. When the commanded person comes into existence, he is commanded by that same demand without any renewal of another demand. And many a person has no child, yet there subsists in his essence a demand for seeking knowledge from him on the supposition of his existence. For he imagines in himself saying to his son: 'Seek knowledge.' This demand becomes realized in himself on the supposition of existence. So if the child comes into existence, and mind is created for him, and knowledge is created for him of what is in his father's self without the assumption of forming a heard utterance, and it is supposed that that demand remains until his existence, then the son would know that he is commanded by the father to seek knowledge without any renewed demand in the self. Rather, that demand remains. Yes, the custom is that the son does not acquire knowledge except through an utterance indicating the inner demand. So his saying with his tongue 'Seek knowledge' is an indication of the demand that is in his essence, whether it occurs at that time or was subsisting in his essence before the existence of his child. Thus should one understand the subsistence of the command in the essence of Allah, Exalted. So the expressions indicating it are created, and the indicated is eternal.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the eternity of Allah's speech (kalam) against the doubt that it might be originated (hadith). Ghazali begins by stating that speech (kalam) is eternal, and he refutes the objection based on Quranic verses such as 'Take off your shoes' and 'We sent Noah'. The objectors assume that speech must be sound, which would imply change and origination. Ghazali explains that Allah's speech is not composed of sounds but is rather a meaning subsisting in the self (kalam nafsi), known as the 'speech of the self'. He uses the example of news (khabar) about sending Noah: the expression changes from future to past tense depending on when it is uttered, but the eternal meaning (ma'na) in Allah's essence does not change; it is a single information about the sending of Noah at the known time, just as His knowledge is eternal. Similarly, command (amr) is a demand (iqtida') and request (talab) that subsists in the essence of the commander. It does not require the existence of the commanded person at the time of the command. Rather, it can exist in the essence before the commanded comes into existence, and when the commanded appears, he is commanded by that same eternal demand without any new demand. Ghazali then provides an analogy: a father without a child can have in his essence a demand for his future son to seek knowledge. He imagines telling his son 'Seek knowledge.' This demand is real in his self on the supposition of the son's future existence. If the son then comes into existence, and the son has knowledge of what is in his father's mind without a spoken word, the son would know he is commanded by the father's eternal demand. In normal custom, the son only knows through a spoken expression, but that expression is merely an indication (dalala) of the inner demand. Thus, expressions (alfaz) are created (haditha), while the indicated meaning (madlul) is eternal (qadim). This is how we should understand the command subsisting in Allah's essence.

Misconception: Allah's speech consists of sounds and letters like human speech.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that Allah's speech is an eternal meaning (kalam nafsi), not composed of sounds; the expressions are created.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the nature of Allah's speech (kalam)?
A) It is composed of eternal sounds and letters.
B) It is a single eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, not composed of sounds.
C) It is created anew each time He speaks.
D) It is only a metaphor and not a real attribute.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal meaning; its expressions are created and changeable, but the inner meaning remains immutable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Allah's commands subsist in His essence before we are born, and that they are eternal demands. How does knowing that Allah's command for you to pray, give charity, and obey Him existed before you were born change your sense of responsibility when you delay or neglect these commands? What would it mean to live as if His command is already present and awaiting your response?
?
Ghazali contrasts the changing expressions with the eternal meaning. When you recite the Quran or hear its verses, do you sometimes focus more on the sound or the ritual than on the eternal meaning that subsists in Allah's essence? What practical step can you take today to connect more deeply with the meaning of a verse you often recite?
?
The father in the analogy had a demand for his non-existent son to seek knowledge. Consider: Allah's demand on you is such that even before you were born, He willed for you to be Muslim. Does that truth humble you to the point of feeling a personal debt to Him that you can never repay? How would you respond concretely to that debt in your next act of worship?

How Can Allah Be a Commander Without Anyone to Command?
Ghazali addresses the objection that Allah cannot be a commander in eternity without a commanded person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between the eternal meaning of command and the linguistic application of the name, and why the objection fails.

Core Idea
Allah's command is an eternal meaning; the name applies because the commanded is known.

Ghazali now tackles a subtle objection: if Allah is eternally a commander, then who was He commanding before creation? The objector sees a contradiction. But Ghazali shows this objection confuses two different aspects: the underlying meaning of command and the linguistic name. This lesson unpacks his elegant solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening line of the passage, which states the objection: Do you claim Allah is a commander in eternity? Then if yes, how can a command exist without a commanded person? If no, then the command would originate, implying change.
Notice the two horns of the dilemma: either an absurdity (command without subject) or a change in Allah.
2
Identify the two aspects of the answer
Ghazali explains that the answer involves two aspects: the meaning (ma'na) of command, and the linguistic application of the name (ism). The meaning is clear: the eternal demand is intelligible even before the commanded exists. The name application is a verbal matter.
This distinction is key to resolving the objection.
3
Examine the analogy with 'powerful'
Ghazali draws an analogy: just as Allah is called 'powerful' (qadir) before any object of power (maqdur) exists, because what is required is a known object, not an existing one; similarly, 'commander' requires only a known commanded person.
The phrase 'known of existence' (ma'lum al-wujud) is crucial.
4
Understand the conclusion
The command necessitates a commanded person that is known, not existing. The non-existent (ma'dum) is known of existence before it exists. Thus, calling Allah 'commander' in eternity is valid.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is essentially complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : أفتقولون : إن الله تعالى في الأزل آمر ناهِ؟ فإن قلتم : إنه [تحريجة؟] آمر .. فكيف يكون أمرٌ لا مأمور له ؟ وإن قلتم : لا .. فقد صار أمراً بعد أن لم يكن ؟ قلنا : اختلف الأصحابُ في جواب هذا ؛ والمختار أن نقول : هذا نظر يتعلَّق أحد طرفيه بالمعنى ، والآخر بإطلاق الاسم من حيث اللغة ؛ فأما حظ المعنى . . فقد انكشف ، وهو أن الاقتضاء القديم معقول وإن كان سابقاً على وجود المأمور ؛ كما في حق الولد يبقى أن يقال : اسمُ الأمر ينطلق عليه بعد فهم المأمور ووجوده أم ينطلق عليه قبله ؟ وهذا أمر لفظي لا ينبغي للناظر أن يشتغل بأمثاله ، ولكن الحق أنه يجوز إطلاقه عليه كما جوزوا تسمية الله تعالى قادراً قبل وجود المقدور ، ولم يستبعدوا قادراً ليس له مقدور موجود ، بل قالوا : القادر يستدعي مقدوراً معلوماً لا موجوداً .. فكذلك الأمر يستدعي مأموراً معلوماً لا موجوداً ، والمعدومُ معلومُ الوجودِ قبل الوجود ، بل يستدعي الأمر مأموراً به كما يستدعي مأموراً ، ويستدعي أيضاً أمراً (۲) ، والمأمور به يكون
If it is said: Do you say that Allah, Exalted, in eternity is a commander and a prohibiter? If you say that He is a commander... then how can there be a command with no one commanded? And if you say no, then it became a command after it was not? We say: The companions differed regarding the answer to this. The chosen view is to say: This is a consideration, one side of which pertains to the meaning, and the other to the application of the name according to language. As for the aspect of meaning, it has been uncovered: the eternal demand is intelligible even if it precedes the existence of the commanded, as in the case of the son. It remains to be said: Is the name 'command' applied to it after understanding and existence of the commanded, or is it applied before? This is a verbal matter (amr lafzi) that the inquirer should not occupy himself with. Rather, the truth is that it is permissible to apply it to Him, just as they permitted calling Allah 'powerful' before the existence of the object of power, and they did not deem improbable a powerful being with no existing object of power. Rather, they said: the powerful necessitates an object of power that is known, not existing. Similarly, the command necessitates a commanded person that is known, not existing. And the non-existent is known of existence before existence. Rather, the command necessitates a thing commanded just as it necessitates a commanded person, and it also necessitates a commander, and the thing commanded is...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawab) regarding the attribute of command. The objector asks: Do you claim that Allah is a commander and prohibiter in eternity (before creation)? If you say yes, how can there be a command without a commanded person? If you say no, then His command would originate after not existing, implying change. Ghazali explains that the companions of the school differed on the answer. The chosen (mukhtar) answer is to distinguish between the meaning (ma'na) and the linguistic application of the name (ism). As for the meaning, it has been clarified: the eternal demand (iqtida' qadim) is intelligible (ma'qul) even if it precedes the existence of the commanded, just as in the analogy of the father and son. Then the remaining issue is purely verbal (amr lafzi): does the name 'command' (amr) apply before the commanded exists? Ghazali states that one should not preoccupy with such verbal disputes. The correct view is that it is permissible to apply the name, just as they allowed calling Allah 'powerful' (qadir) before the existence of any object of power (maqdur). They argued that qadir necessitates a maqdur that is known (ma'lum), not necessarily existing (mawjud). Similarly, the command (amr) necessitates a commanded person (ma'mur) that is known, not existing. The non-existent (ma'dum) is known of existence before it exists. Furthermore, the command necessitates a thing commanded (ma'mur bihi) just as it necessitates a commanded person, and it also necessitates a commander (amir). The passage ends mid-sentence due to an editorial footnote, but the argument is essentially complete: the command exists eternally as a meaning, and the name can be applied anticipatorily.

How does Ghazali respond to the objection that Allah cannot be called a commander in eternity?
A) He says that Allah is not a commander until the commanded exists.
B) He says the command is an eternal meaning, and the name can be applied because the commanded is known to exist.
C) He says that the objection is invalid because Allah is beyond such categories.
D) He says that the command is created and not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the eternal command is an intelligible meaning, and the name 'commander' applies because the commanded is known to exist, not because it currently exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey Allah's commands, do you sometimes feel that your obedience is pointless because you don't see immediate results? How does knowing that Allah's command is eternal and based on His knowledge of your existence before you were born change your perspective on the significance of your actions?
?
The analogy of power shows that Allah's ability is not dependent on existing objects. In what area of your life do you doubt Allah's ability because you don't see the evidence yet? Will you trust His command and power even when the outcome is not visible?
?
This lesson teaches that Allah's command is real and meaningful even when no one is yet commanded. If you were the only person in existence, would you still feel the weight of His command over your life? How does that affect your sense of accountability today?
