The Eternal Command Subsisting in God's Essence
Ghazali explains the subsistence of the eternal command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eternal command of God subsists in His essence, distinguishing between the temporal wording and the eternal meaning.

Core Idea
God's command is eternal in meaning, requiring known creatures, not present ones.

Imam al-Ghazali begins by clarifying that the divine command is not a temporal event but an eternal attribute. He explains that while the words we use are created, the meaning they indicate—God's eternal requirement—is not. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding God's command as an eternal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage starts with 'Thus it should be understood...' indicating a conclusion from prior discussion.
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage distinguishes between the words (alfāẓ) which are temporally originated (ḥāditha) and the meaning (maʿnā) which is eternal (qadīm). Underline these terms.
3
Understand the condition for the command
Ghazali explains that the existence of the meaning does not require the presence of the commanded person (maʾmūr), but only that the commanded person be known to exist (i.e., possible). Ask yourself: what would make a command impossible?
The exception is if the commanded person is impossible in existence, then such a requirement is not attributed to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم قيام الأمر بذات الله تعالى، فتكون الألفاظ الدالة عليه حادثة والمدلول قديماً، ووجود ذلك المدلول لا يستدعي وجود المأمور، بل يتصوَّرُ وجوده مهما كان المأمور مقدَّرَ الوجودِ، فإن كان مستحيل الوجود.. فربما لا يتصوَّرُ وجودُ الاقتضاء ممَّنْ يعلم استحالة وجوده؛ فلذلك لا نقول: إن الله تعالى يقوم بذاته اقتضاء فعل ممَّن يستحيل وجوده، بل ممَّنْ عَلِمَ وجوده، وذلك غير محال.

فإن قيل: أفتقولون: إن الله تعالى في الأزل آمر ناهِ؛ فإن قلتم: إنَّه آمر.. فكيف يكون أمرٌ لا مأمور له؟ وإن قلتم: لا.. فقد صار أمراً بعد أن لم يكن؟

قلنا: اختلف الأصحابُ في جواب هذا؛ والمختار أن نقول: هذا نظر يتعلَّق أحد طرفيه بالمعنى، والآخر بإطلاق الاسم من حيث اللغة؛ فأما حظ المعنى فقد انكشف، وهو أن الاقتضاء القديم معقول وإن كان سابقاً على وجود المأمور؛ كما في حق الولد.

يبقى أن يقال: اسمُ الأمر ينطلق عليه بعد فهم المأمور ووجوده أم ينطلق عليه قبله؟ وهذا أمر لفظي لا ينبغي للناظر أن يشتغل بأمثاله، ولكن الحق أنه يجوز إطلاقه عليه كما جوزوا تسمية الله تعالى قادراً قبل وجود المقدور، ولم يستبعدوا قادراً ليس له مقدور موجود، بل قالوا: القادر يستدعي مقدوراً معلوماً لا موجوداً.. فكذلك الأمر يستدعي مأموراً معلوماً لا موجوداً، والمعدومُ معلومُ الوجودِ قبل الوجود، بل يستدعي الأمر مأموراً به كما يستدعي مأموراً، ويستدعي أيضاً أمراً، والمأمور به يكون معدوماً، ولا يقال: إنه كيف يكون أمراً من غير مأمور به، بل يقال: له مأمور به هو معلوم وليس يشترط كونه موجوداً، بل يشترط كونه معدوماً.

بل من أمر ولده على سبيل الوصية بأمر ثم توفي، فأتى الولد بما أوصي به.. يقال: امتثل أمر والده، والأمرُ معدوم، والأمر في نفسه معدوم، ونحن هذا نطلقُ اسم امتثال الأمر، فإذا لم يستدع كون المأمور ممتثلاً للأمر لا وجود الأمر ولا وجود الأمر ولم يستدع كونُ الأمر أمراً وجود المأمور به.. فمن أين يستدعي وجود المأمور؟

فقد انكشف من هذا حظ اللفظ والمعنى جميعاً، ولا نظر إلا فيهما.
Thus it should be understood that the command subsists in the essence of God, Exalted is He. So the words indicating it are temporally originated, while the meaning indicated is eternal. The existence of that meaning does not require the existence of the commanded person; rather its existence can be conceived whenever the commanded person is assumed to exist. If it is impossible in existence... then perhaps the existence of the requirement from one who knows the impossibility of its existence cannot be conceived. Therefore we do not say: God, Exalted is He, has in His essence a requirement for an action from one whose existence is impossible, but rather from one whose existence He knows; and that is not impossible.

If it is said: Do you say that God, Exalted is He, is in eternity a commander and a forbiddener? If you say: He is a commander... then how can there be a command with no commanded person? And if you say: no... then He became a commander after not having been?

We say: The companions differed in answering this; the chosen answer is to say: This inquiry relates one side to the meaning, and the other to the application of the name from the linguistic perspective. As for the share of meaning, it has been uncovered: the eternal requirement is intelligible even if it precedes the existence of the commanded person, just as in the case of the child.

It remains to be said: Is the name 'command' applied to it after understanding the commanded person and his existence, or is it applied before that? This is a verbal matter which the investigator should not occupy himself with. But the truth is that it is permissible to apply it to Him, just as they permitted calling God, Exalted is He, 'powerful' (qādir) before the existence of the object of power (maqdūr), and they did not deem it far-fetched that there be a powerful one with no existent object of power. Rather they said: The powerful one requires an object of power that is known, not existent. Similarly, the command requires a commanded person that is known, not existent. The non-existent is known to exist before existence. Rather the command requires a commanded thing just as it requires a commanded person, and it also requires a commander. The commanded thing is non-existent. And it is not said: How can there be a command without a commanded thing? Rather it is said: It has a commanded thing that is known; it is not required that it be existent, but rather it is required that it be non-existent.

Indeed, if someone commands his son by a command as a bequest, then dies, and the son does what he was commanded... it is said: He carried out his father's command, even though the command is non-existent and the command in itself is non-existent. Yet we apply the name 'carrying out of the command'. So if it does not require the existence of the commander (amr) nor the existence of the command (amr), nor does it require that the command (amr) being a command require the existence of the commanded thing... from where would it require the existence of the commanded person?

Thus from this the share of both the wording and the meaning has been uncovered, and there is no consideration except in them.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the divine attribute of command (amr) and its eternal subsistence (qiyām) in God's essence. Ghazali begins by explaining how the command subsists: the verbal expressions are temporally originated, but the meaning (the eternal requirement) is eternal. This meaning does not require the commanded person (ma'mūr) to exist at the same time; its existence is conceivable as long as the commanded person is assumed to exist, even if only in the future. If the commanded person is impossible in existence (like an action from a non-existent agent), then such a requirement is not attributed to God. So God's command is directed only to those whose existence He knows.

Then an objection is raised: Do you say that God is a commander (āmir) and forbiddener (nāhi) in eternity? If yes, how can there be a command without a commanded person? If no, then He became a commander after not having been, implying change. Ghazali says the companions differed on the answer, and the chosen answer is to distinguish between meaning and linguistic name. The meaning (the eternal requirement) is intelligible before the commanded person exists, like the case of a child (meaning a parent's command for the child's future action is understood before the child acts).

The remaining issue is whether the name 'command' applies before the commanded person's existence or after. This is deemed a verbal matter not worth deep investigation, but the truth is it is permissible to apply it before, just as God is called 'powerful' (qādir) before the existence of any object of power (maqdūr). The powerful requires a known object of power, not an existent one. Similarly, the command requires a known commanded person, not an existent one. The commanded thing (ma'mūr bihi) is non-existent, but it is a condition that it be non-existent (i.e., not yet actual).

Ghazali gives an analogy: a father's last will command after his death; the command is non-existent, yet we say the son carried out his father's command. So the existence of the commander (the father) or the command (as a present reality) is not required for the act of compliance. Thus, it is clear that neither the wording nor the meaning require the commanded person to exist for the command to be validly attributed in eternity.

Correction: Ghazali shows that the meaning of command only requires the person to be known as possible, not existent.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the existence of the eternal command (the meaning) require regarding the commanded person?
A) The commanded person must exist at the same time.
B) The commanded person must be known to be possible in existence.
C) The commanded person must be eternal.
D) The commanded person must be already created.

Key Takeaway
God's command is an eternal meaning that does not depend on the existence of the commanded person at the moment of command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's command is an eternal meaning that does not require your present existence. Do you live as though God's commands are eternally binding on you, or do you act as if they are distant and conditional? What would change if you truly felt the weight of an eternal command directed specifically at you today?
?
Ghazali says the command is only for one whose existence God knows to be possible. Since you exist, God's command applies to you. Is there a command of God you have been avoiding, thinking it does not apply to you or is outdated? How might your life look if you accepted that command as an eternal requirement from the One who knows you fully?
?
The passage distinguishes between the eternal meaning and the temporal words. Are you more focused on the outer form of religion (words, rituals) than the eternal meaning they signify? What would it mean for you to uncover the eternal command behind the words and respond to it today?

The Objection and the Answer: Meaning vs. Name
Ghazali presents and answers an objection about the eternal command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against an eternal command without a present addressee and Ghazali's answer distinguishing between the rational meaning and the linguistic name.

Core Idea
An eternal command with no commanded person is intelligible when meaning and name are separated.

A natural objection arises: how can God be called 'commander' if there is no one to command? Ghazali presents the differing views of his companions and then offers the chosen answer: the objection mixes up the eternal meaning with the temporal application of the name. This lesson explores that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection presents a dilemma: either God is commander in eternity with no one to command, which seems absurd, or He becomes commander later, implying change. Identify both horns of the dilemma.
2
Understand the chosen answer
Ghazali says the chosen answer distinguishes between the share of meaning (ḥaẓẓ al-maʿnā) and the application of the name (iṭlāq al-ism). The rational meaning is understood; the verbal issue is secondary.
Notice the analogy with the case of a child: a parent's command for a future action is understood before the child acts.
3
Recognize the verbal nature
Ghazali dismisses the question of when the name 'command' applies as a verbal matter (amr lafẓī) and advises not to dwell on it. Focus on the meaning that is already clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهكذا ينبغي أن يفهم قيام الأمر بذات الله تعالى، فتكون الألفاظ الدالة عليه حادثة والمدلول قديماً، ووجود ذلك المدلول لا يستدعي وجود المأمور، بل يتصوَّرُ وجوده مهما كان المأمور مقدَّرَ الوجودِ، فإن كان مستحيل الوجود.. فربما لا يتصوَّرُ وجودُ الاقتضاء ممَّنْ يعلم استحالة وجوده؛ فلذلك لا نقول: إن الله تعالى يقوم بذاته اقتضاء فعل ممَّن يستحيل وجوده، بل ممَّنْ عَلِمَ وجوده، وذلك غير محال.

فإن قيل: أفتقولون: إن الله تعالى في الأزل آمر ناهِ؛ فإن قلتم: إنَّه آمر.. فكيف يكون أمرٌ لا مأمور له؟ وإن قلتم: لا.. فقد صار أمراً بعد أن لم يكن؟

قلنا: اختلف الأصحابُ في جواب هذا؛ والمختار أن نقول: هذا نظر يتعلَّق أحد طرفيه بالمعنى، والآخر بإطلاق الاسم من حيث اللغة؛ فأما حظ المعنى فقد انكشف، وهو أن الاقتضاء القديم معقول وإن كان سابقاً على وجود المأمور؛ كما في حق الولد.

يبقى أن يقال: اسمُ الأمر ينطلق عليه بعد فهم المأمور ووجوده أم ينطلق عليه قبله؟ وهذا أمر لفظي لا ينبغي للناظر أن يشتغل بأمثاله، ولكن الحق أنه يجوز إطلاقه عليه كما جوزوا تسمية الله تعالى قادراً قبل وجود المقدور، ولم يستبعدوا قادراً ليس له مقدور موجود، بل قالوا: القادر يستدعي مقدوراً معلوماً لا موجوداً.. فكذلك الأمر يستدعي مأموراً معلوماً لا موجوداً، والمعدومُ معلومُ الوجودِ قبل الوجود، بل يستدعي الأمر مأموراً به كما يستدعي مأموراً، ويستدعي أيضاً أمراً، والمأمور به يكون معدوماً، ولا يقال: إنه كيف يكون أمراً من غير مأمور به، بل يقال: له مأمور به هو معلوم وليس يشترط كونه موجوداً، بل يشترط كونه معدوماً.

بل من أمر ولده على سبيل الوصية بأمر ثم توفي، فأتى الولد بما أوصي به.. يقال: امتثل أمر والده، والأمرُ معدوم، والأمر في نفسه معدوم، ونحن هذا نطلقُ اسم امتثال الأمر، فإذا لم يستدع كون المأمور ممتثلاً للأمر لا وجود الأمر ولا وجود الأمر ولم يستدع كونُ الأمر أمراً وجود المأمور به.. فمن أين يستدعي وجود المأمور؟

فقد انكشف من هذا حظ اللفظ والمعنى جميعاً، ولا نظر إلا فيهما.
Thus it should be understood that the command subsists in the essence of God, Exalted is He. So the words indicating it are temporally originated, while the meaning indicated is eternal. The existence of that meaning does not require the existence of the commanded person; rather its existence can be conceived whenever the commanded person is assumed to exist. If it is impossible in existence... then perhaps the existence of the requirement from one who knows the impossibility of its existence cannot be conceived. Therefore we do not say: God, Exalted is He, has in His essence a requirement for an action from one whose existence is impossible, but rather from one whose existence He knows; and that is not impossible.

If it is said: Do you say that God, Exalted is He, is in eternity a commander and a forbiddener? If you say: He is a commander... then how can there be a command with no commanded person? And if you say: no... then He became a commander after not having been?

We say: The companions differed in answering this; the chosen answer is to say: This inquiry relates one side to the meaning, and the other to the application of the name from the linguistic perspective. As for the share of meaning, it has been uncovered: the eternal requirement is intelligible even if it precedes the existence of the commanded person, just as in the case of the child.

It remains to be said: Is the name 'command' applied to it after understanding the commanded person and his existence, or is it applied before that? This is a verbal matter which the investigator should not occupy himself with. But the truth is that it is permissible to apply it to Him, just as they permitted calling God, Exalted is He, 'powerful' (qādir) before the existence of the object of power (maqdūr), and they did not deem it far-fetched that there be a powerful one with no existent object of power. Rather they said: The powerful one requires an object of power that is known, not existent. Similarly, the command requires a commanded person that is known, not existent. The non-existent is known to exist before existence. Rather the command requires a commanded thing just as it requires a commanded person, and it also requires a commander. The commanded thing is non-existent. And it is not said: How can there be a command without a commanded thing? Rather it is said: It has a commanded thing that is known; it is not required that it be existent, but rather it is required that it be non-existent.

Indeed, if someone commands his son by a command as a bequest, then dies, and the son does what he was commanded... it is said: He carried out his father's command, even though the command is non-existent and the command in itself is non-existent. Yet we apply the name 'carrying out of the command'. So if it does not require the existence of the commander (amr) nor the existence of the command (amr), nor does it require that the command (amr) being a command require the existence of the commanded thing... from where would it require the existence of the commanded person?

Thus from this the share of both the wording and the meaning has been uncovered, and there is no consideration except in them.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the divine attribute of command (amr) and its eternal subsistence (qiyām) in God's essence. Ghazali begins by explaining how the command subsists: the verbal expressions are temporally originated, but the meaning (the eternal requirement) is eternal. This meaning does not require the commanded person (ma'mūr) to exist at the same time; its existence is conceivable as long as the commanded person is assumed to exist, even if only in the future. If the commanded person is impossible in existence (like an action from a non-existent agent), then such a requirement is not attributed to God. So God's command is directed only to those whose existence He knows.

Then an objection is raised: Do you say that God is a commander (āmir) and forbiddener (nāhi) in eternity? If yes, how can there be a command without a commanded person? If no, then He became a commander after not having been, implying change. Ghazali says the companions differed on the answer, and the chosen answer is to distinguish between meaning and linguistic name. The meaning (the eternal requirement) is intelligible before the commanded person exists, like the case of a child (meaning a parent's command for the child's future action is understood before the child acts).

The remaining issue is whether the name 'command' applies before the commanded person's existence or after. This is deemed a verbal matter not worth deep investigation, but the truth is it is permissible to apply it before, just as God is called 'powerful' (qādir) before the existence of any object of power (maqdūr). The powerful requires a known object of power, not an existent one. Similarly, the command requires a known commanded person, not an existent one. The commanded thing (ma'mūr bihi) is non-existent, but it is a condition that it be non-existent (i.e., not yet actual).

Ghazali gives an analogy: a father's last will command after his death; the command is non-existent, yet we say the son carried out his father's command. So the existence of the commander (the father) or the command (as a present reality) is not required for the act of compliance. Thus, it is clear that neither the wording nor the meaning require the commanded person to exist for the command to be validly attributed in eternity.

Key Terms
الأمر
المأمور
آمر
المعنى
اللفظ
الاقتضاء القديم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if there is no commanded person, the term 'commander' cannot be applied at all.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the meaning is still valid; the name is a separate issue.

Checkpoint
What is the 'share of meaning' (ḥaẓẓ al-maʿnā) that Ghazali says has been uncovered?
A) The command requires a present commanded person.
B) The eternal requirement is intelligible before the commanded person exists.
C) The name 'command' only applies after the commanded person exists.
D) The companions all agreed on one answer.

Key Takeaway
The objection conflates the rational meaning of command (eternal) with the linguistic name (time-bound); Ghazali separates them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced God's eternal attributes to their temporal manifestations, making His commands dependent on your existence? How does separating the eternal meaning from the temporal name change your understanding of His sovereignty?
?
When you hear 'God commands you,' do you instinctively feel the weight of an eternal requirement or just a present instruction? Which of these would make you more accountable, and why?
?
If the application of the name 'command' is a verbal matter, why do you still treat it as if it adds or removes something from God? What does this reveal about your attachment to labels rather than meanings?

The Analogy with Power and the Will Example
Ghazali uses analogy and example to support his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Ghazali uses the attribute of power and a father's will to prove that the name 'commander' is valid before the existence of the commanded person.

Core Idea
Just as God is 'powerful' before any object of power, He is 'commander' before any commanded person.

Having distinguished meaning from name, Ghazali now provides a powerful analogy and a real-world example to solidify the point. He compares the attribute of command to the attribute of power (qudra), showing that both names apply before their objects exist. He then illustrates with a father's command via a will, which is considered a valid command even after the father's death. This lesson cements the linguistic permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy with power
Ghazali draws a parallel: God is called 'powerful' (qādir) before any object of power (maqdūr) exists. The term requires a known object, not an existent one. Similarly, 'commander' (āmir) requires a known commanded person, not an existent one.
2
Understand the implications
From this analogy, the command requires a commanded thing (maʾmūr bihī) as well as a commanded person. Both are known but non-existent at the time of the eternal command.
Ghazali reverses the objection: the commanded thing must be non-existent, otherwise it would already have been done.
3
Examine the will example
A father commands his son via a will, then dies. The son later carries out the command, and we say he obeyed his father's command. Yet the command (as a present reality) is non-existent. This shows that the name 'command' can apply even when the commander and command are absent.
This demonstrates that the existence of the commander or the command as a present entity is not required.

The Eternity of Essential Attribute Names
Ghazali states the fourth ruling on derived names and highlights the dispute over action-derived names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that names derived from the seven essential attributes are eternally true of Allah, while those derived from actions are subject to scholarly disagreement due to their temporal implications.

Core Idea
Essential attribute names apply eternally; action-derived names do not.

Ghazali begins by clarifying a key ruling about the names of Allah. He distinguishes between names that come from the seven essential attributes (like Knowing, Powerful) and those derived from His actions (like Creator, Provider). The former apply eternally; the latter are debated among scholars. In this lesson, we explore this distinction and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the ruling, then the exception about actions.
The rulings are concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the two categories of derived names
Find in the passage the seven essential attributes (living, knowing, etc.) and the action-derived names (Provider, Creator, etc.). Observe how Ghazali treats them differently.
3
Understand the dispute about action-derived names
Ghazali says scholars differ on whether names like al-Rāziq apply in eternity. He hints that when the matter is clarified, disagreement becomes impossible—meaning it is clear that they are not eternal.
This connects to the broader kalām debate about temporal vs. eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الرابع: أن الأسامي المشتقة من هذه الصفات السبع صادقة عليه أزلاً وأبداً؛ فهو في القدم كان حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً، وأما ما يُشتق له من الأفعال؛ كالرازق والخالق والمعز والمذل.. فقد اختلف في أنه يصدق في الأزل أم لا، وهذا إذا كشف الغطاء عنه.. تبيَّنَ استحالة الخلاف فيه.

والقول الجامع: أن الأسامي التي يسمَّى بها الله سبحانه وتعالى أربعة أقسام:
الأول: ألا يدل إلا على ذاته؛ كالموجود، وهذا صادق أزلاً وأبداً.
الثاني: ما يدلُّ على الذات مع زيادة سلب؛ كالقديم، فإنه يدلُّ على وجود غير مسبوق بعدم أزلاً، وكالباقي؛ فإنه يدلُّ على الوجود وسلب العدم عنه أبداً، وكالواحد؛ فإنه يدلُّ على الوجود وسلبِ الشريك، وكالغني؛ فإنه يدلُّ على الوجود وسلْبِ الحاجة، فهذا أيضاً يصدق أزلاً وأبداً؛ لأنَّ ما يسلب عنه يسلب لذاته، فيلازم الذات على الدوام.
The fourth ruling: That the names derived from these seven attributes are true of Him eternally and perpetually. So in pre-eternity He was living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking. As for what is derived for Him from actions, such as the Provider (al-Rāziq), the Creator (al-Khāliq), the Exalter (al-Muʿizz), the Abaser (al-Mudhill)... they differed as to whether it is true in eternity or not. And this, if the covering is removed from it, reveals the impossibility of disagreement concerning it.

And the comprehensive statement: The names by which God, Exalted is He, is named are four divisions:
First: That which indicates only His essence, such as 'the Existent' (al-Mawjūd). This is true eternally and perpetually.
Second: That which indicates the essence with an added negation; such as 'the Eternal' (al-Qadīm), for it indicates an existence not preceded by non-existence in eternity; and 'the Everlasting' (al-Bāqī), for it indicates existence and the negation of non-existence from Him perpetually; and 'the One' (al-Wāḥid), for it indicates existence and the negation of a partner; and 'the Self-Sufficient' (al-Ghanī), for it indicates existence and the negation of need. This also is true eternally and perpetually, because what is negated from Him is negated due to His essence, so it accompanies the essence perpetually.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine names. Ghazali first states the fourth ruling: the names derived from the seven essential attributes (life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, will?) Actually he mentions the seven attributes: hayy, 'ālim, qādir, samī', baṣīr, mutakallim. He says these names are eternally and perpetually true of God. However, names derived from actions (like al-Rāziq, al-Khāliq, al-Muʿizz, al-Mudhill) are the subject of disagreement: are they applicable in eternity? Ghazali suggests that once the matter is clarified, the disagreement is impossible (i.e., it becomes clear they are not applicable in eternity because they imply the occurrence of the action).

Then he gives a comprehensive classification of all the names of God into four categories. He describes the first two:
1. Names that indicate only the essence, like 'al-Mawjūd' (the Existent). These are true eternally and perpetually.
2. Names that indicate the essence along with an added negation (salb), such as:
   - al-Qadīm (the Eternal): indicates existence not preceded by non-existence.
   - al-Bāqī (the Everlasting): indicates existence with the negation of non-existence perpetually.
   - al-Wāḥid (the One): indicates existence with the negation of a partner.
   - al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient): indicates existence with the negation of need.
These also are true eternally and perpetually because the negated qualities are negated due to the essence itself, so they accompany the essence always.

The passage ends before describing the third and fourth categories, but the classification is clear as far as it goes.

الأسامي المشتقة
الصفات السبع
الأفعال
الأزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all derived names (e.g., al-Rāziq) are eternally applicable like those from essential attributes.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes: essential attribute names are eternal; action-derived names are not, because they imply temporal occurrence of the action.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following is true about names derived from actions (e.g., al-Rāziq)?
A) They are applicable in eternity just like names derived from essential attributes.
B) Their applicability in eternity is a matter of disagreement that is impossible to resolve.
C) They are not applicable in eternity because they imply the occurrence of the action in time.
D) They are applicable only if the action is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The seven essential attribute names are eternally true of Allah, but action-derived names only apply when the action occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Rāziq, do you realize that this name only applies because He provides at this moment? How does that awareness change your reliance on Him for each provision, rather than assuming it automatically?
?
Allah's essential names (like al-ʿAlīm, al-Qādir) are eternally true. Can you name a situation today where you felt unknown or powerless? How does the reality that Allah has eternally known you and had power over you give you confidence to act?
?
Ghazali says action-derived names are not eternally true because they depend on creation. Is your relationship with Allah built more on what He does for you (action names) or on who He is (essential names)? What would happen if He temporarily withheld an action—would your faith remain as strong?

Classification of Divine Names: First Two Types
Ghazali divides divine names into four categories and explains the first two: essence-only and essence-with-negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of divine names into four types, with detailed explanation of the first two: essence-only names and essence-plus-negation names.

Core Idea
Divine names are either essence-only or include a negation; both are eternal.

After addressing derived names, Ghazali provides a comprehensive classification of all divine names into four categories. In this lesson, we study the first two: names that indicate only the essence (like al-Mawjood) and names that indicate the essence along with a negation (like al-Qadeem, al-Baaqi). These names are eternally true because the negations follow from the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic starting from 'والقول الجامع' through to the end. Compare with the translation. Notice the four-part division and the detailed explanation of the first two types.
The passage is a classification; pay attention to the structure 'الأول' and 'الثاني'.
2
Identify the four divisions introduced
Ghazali says names are of four divisions (أربعة أقسام). The passage only describes the first two in detail. Note them as a framework.
The full classification is a key tool for understanding divine names in kalām.
3
Understand the first type: essence-only names
The first division is names that indicate only the essence (dhāt), with the example al-Mawjood (the Existent). These are true eternally and perpetually because existence is essential.
This type is the simplest: no additional meaning beyond existence.
4
Understand the second type: essence-with-negation names
The second division includes names that indicate the essence with an added negation (salb). Examples: al-Qadeem (negation of non-existence before), al-Baaqi (negation of non-existence after), al-Wāḥid (negation of partner), al-Ghanī (negation of need). These are also eternal because the negated qualities are impossible for Allah due to His essence.
Each of these names carries a specific negation that always remains true.

?

?
You affirm that Allah is al-Wāḥid (the One) with no partner. But examine your heart: is there any person, desire, or fear that you elevate to a level of authority in your life that rivals His command? What will you do today to tear down that hidden partner?
?
The names al-Qadīm and al-Bāqī remind you that Allah's existence is independent of time—He always was and always will be. How does this truth challenge your anxiety about the future or your regret over the past? Will you entrust both to Him fully starting now?

The Four Categories of Divine Names
Ghazali classifies divine names into four categories and resolves a dispute about action-derived names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of divine names into four categories and his resolution of the dispute regarding whether action-derived names apply to God in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Divine names are either essential, negative, attribute-based, or action-related, and the last applies potentially from eternity.

This passage from Imam al-Ghazali's al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad tackles a crucial question: how can God be called 'Creator' before creation exists? To answer this, Ghazali first classifies all divine names into four distinct kinds. He then resolves a long-standing dispute among theologians about the fourth category—names derived from actions—by introducing a subtle distinction that both sides can accept. As you read, notice how Ghazali uses an everyday analogy about a sword to illuminate a profound theological point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to get a general sense of the argument.
The passage has two parts: a classification and a resolution.
2
Identify the four categories
Look for the four divisions Ghazali lists: names indicating only essence, names indicating essence with negation, names indicating essence with an attribute, and names indicating essence with relation to an action. Notice that the first three are agreed upon, while the fourth is disputed.
The first three are always true in pre-eternity; only the fourth is debated.
3
Examine the dispute about action-names
Focus on the fourth category. One group says 'Creator' must apply in pre-eternity to avoid change in God. Another says it cannot apply because no creation existed. Why do they disagree?
4
Understand the sword analogy
Ghazali uses the example of a sword called 'sharp' (ṣārim) both in the scabbard and while cutting, but with different meanings. Relate this to 'Creator': God is 'Creator' in pre-eternity because He has the power to create, not because creation actually happened.
This analogy resolves the dispute by distinguishing potential from actual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الرابع : الأسامي المشتقة من هذه الصفات السبع صادقة عليه سبحانه ازلا وأبداً؛ فهو في القدم كان حياً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً متكلماً، وأما ما يُشتق له من الأفعال؛ كالرازق والخالق والمعز والمذل . . فقد اختلف في أنه يصدق في الأزل أم لا، وهذا إذا كشف الغطاء عنه .. تبيَّنَ استحالة الخلاف فيه · أساميه سبحانه أربعة أقسام : الأول : ألا يدل إلا على ذاته ؛ كالموجود، وهذا صادق أزلاً وأبداً الثاني : ما يدلُّ على الذات مع زيادة سلب ؛ كالقديم، فإنه يدلُّ على وجود غير مسبوق بعدم أزلاً، وكالباقي؛ فإنه يدلُّ على الوجود وسلب العدم عنه أبداً، وكالواحد؛ فإنه يدلُّ على الوجود وسلبِ الشريك وكالغني؛ فإنه يدلُّ على الوجود وسلْبِ الحاجة، فهذا أيضاً يصدق أزلاً وأبداً؛ لأنَّ ما يسلب عنه يسلب لذاته، فيلازم الذات على الدوام . الثالث : ما يدلُّ على الوجود وصفةٍ زائدة من صفات المعنى ؛ كالحي والقادر والمتكلم والعالم والمريد والسميع والبصير، وما يرجع إلى هذه الصفات السبع؛ كالآمر والناهي والخبير ونظائره، فذلك أيضاً يصدق عليه أزلاً وأبداً عند من يعتقد قدَمَ جميع الصفات الرابع : ما يدلُّ على الوجود مع إضافة إلى فعل من أفعاله ؛ كالجواد والرازق والخالق والمُعِز والمذل وأمثاله، وهذا مختلف فيه؛ فقال قوم : صادق أزلاً؛ إذ لو لم يصدق .. لكان اتصافه به موجباً للتغير، وقال قوم : لا يصدق؛ إذ لا خلق في الأزل، فكيف يكون خالقاً ؟ والكاشف للغطاء عن هذا : أن السيف في الغمد يسمى صارماً وعند حصول القطع به، وفي تيك الحالة على الاقتران يسمى صارماً، وهما بمعنيين مختلفين؛ فهو في الغمد صارمٌ بالقوة، وعند حصول القطع صارم بالفعل وكذلك الماء في الكوز يسمى مُرْوِياً، وعند الشرب يسمى مروياً، وهما إطلاقان مختلفان؛ فمعنى تسمية السيف في الغمد صارماً : أن الصفة التي يحصلُ بها القطع موجودة في السيف، فليس امتناع القطع في الحال لقصور في ذات السيف وحدَّتِهِ واستعداده، بل لأمر آخر وراء ذاته؛ فبالمعنى الذي يُسمّى السيفُ به في الغمد صارماً .. يصدقُ اسم الخالق على الله عز وجل في الأزل؛ فإن الخلق إذا جرى بالفعل .. لم يكن لتجدُّدِ أمرٍ في الذات لم يكن، بل كلُّ ما يشترط لتحقيق الفعل موجود في الأزل، وبالمعنى الذي يطلق حالة مباشرة القطع للسيف اسم الصارم .. لا يصدقُ في الأزل، فهذا حظ المعنى، فقد ظهرَ أَنَّ مَنْ قال : إنه لا يصدقُ في الأزل هذا الاسم.. فهو محق وأراد به المعنى الثاني، ومن قال : يصدقُ .. فهو محق وأراد به المعنى الأول، وإذا كُشف الغطاء عن هذا الوجه .. ارتفع الخلاف
The Fourth Ruling: The derived names from these seven attributes are true of Him, exalted, eternally and forever; so in pre-eternity He was living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, speaking. As for what is derived for Him from actions, such as the Provider, the Creator, the Honourer, the Abaser... it is disagreed whether it is true in pre-eternity or not. And when the covering is removed from this, the impossibility of disagreement in it becomes clear. His names, exalted, are four divisions: The First: That which indicates only His essence, such as 'the Existent'. And this is true eternally and forever. The Second: That which indicates the essence with an increase of negation, such as 'the Eternal' – for it indicates an existence not preceded by non-existence eternally; and 'the Everlasting' – for it indicates existence and the negation of non-existence from it forever; and 'the One' – for it indicates existence and the negation of a partner; and 'the Self-Sufficient' – for it indicates existence and the negation of need. So this also is true eternally and forever, because that which is negated from Him is negated due to His essence, so it accompanies the essence perpetually. The Third: That which indicates existence and an additional attribute from the attributes of meaning, such as 'the Living', 'the Powerful', 'the Speaking', 'the Knowing', 'the Willing', 'the Hearing', 'the Seeing', and what reverts to these seven attributes, such as 'the Commander', 'the Prohibiter', 'the Aware' and their likes. So that also is true of Him eternally and forever according to one who believes in the pre-eternity of all attributes. The Fourth: That which indicates existence with an addition to an action of His actions, such as 'the Generous', 'the Provider', 'the Creator', 'the Honourer', 'the Abaser' and their examples. And this is disagreed about: one group said it is true in pre-eternity, for if it were not true, then His being described with it would necessitate change; another group said it is not true, for there is no creation in pre-eternity, so how could He be a Creator? And the remover of the covering from this is that the sword in the scabbard is called 'sharp' (ṣārim) and when cutting occurs with it, and in that state upon connection it is called 'sharp', and they are two different meanings; so it is 'sharp' in potentiality (bi-l-quwwa) in the scabbard, and when cutting occurs it is 'sharp' in actuality (bi-l-fiʿl). Similarly, water in the jug is called 'irrigating' (murwī), and when drinking occurs it is called 'irrigating', and they are two different usages. So the meaning of calling the sword in the scabbard 'sharp' is that the attribute by which cutting occurs exists in the sword, so the prevention of cutting at that moment is not due to a deficiency in the sword's essence, its edge, and its readiness, but due to something else beyond its essence. So by the meaning by which the sword in the scabbard is called 'sharp', the name 'Creator' applies to God Almighty in pre-eternity; for when creation occurs in actuality, it is not due to a renewal of something in the essence that was not, rather everything required for the realization of the action exists in pre-eternity. And by the meaning that is applied at the moment of direct cutting to the sword as 'sharp', it does not apply in pre-eternity. This is the share of the meaning. So it has become clear that whoever said that this name does not apply in pre-eternity is correct, and he meant the second meaning; and whoever said it applies is correct, and he meant the first meaning. And when the covering is removed from this aspect, the disagreement is resolved.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a classification and resolution of a dispute. It begins with the Fourth Ruling, which concerns the divine names derived from the seven essential attributes (life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech). Ghazali asserts that names like Living, Knowing, etc., apply to God eternally, but names derived from actions (e.g., Creator, Provider) are disputed. He then categorizes all divine names into four types: (1) names indicating only the essence (e.g., 'Existent'), always true; (2) names indicating the essence with a negation (e.g., 'Eternal' negates non-existence), always true since the negation follows from the essence; (3) names indicating the essence plus an attribute of meaning (e.g., 'Powerful'), true eternally for those who affirm the eternity of attributes; (4) names indicating the essence plus reference to an action (e.g., 'Creator'). The fourth type is disputed. One group argues they must apply in pre-eternity to avoid change, another says they cannot because the action (e.g., creating) occurs in time. Ghazali resolves this with a powerful analogy: a sword in its scabbard is called 'sharp' (ṣārim) by virtue of possessing the capacity to cut, though it is not cutting; when it actually cuts, it is 'sharp' in a different sense. Similarly, water in a jug is called 'irrigating' because it has the property to quench thirst, though it is not being drunk. So 'Creator' can apply to God in pre-eternity in the sense that He possesses the power and will to create (the attribute exists), but not in the sense of actually performing the act of creation at a particular time. Both parties are therefore correct if they mean different senses. Once this distinction is understood, the disagreement disappears.

Key Terms
الصفات السبع
أسامي المشتقة
الذات
السلب
الإضافة إلى الفعل
صارم بالقوة
صارم بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that names like 'Creator' cannot apply to God in pre-eternity because creation did not yet exist.
Correction: Ghazali explains that such names apply in the sense of potentiality: God possesses the power and will to create, so He is 'Creator' from eternity in the same way a sword in its scabbard is 'sharp'. The actual act of creation occurs in time, but the attribute is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the name 'Creator' apply to God in pre-eternity?
A) Because creation actually occurred from eternity
B) Because God possesses the attribute of power to create
C) Because the name 'Creator' has no connection to actions
D) Because the dispute is purely linguistic

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the dispute about action-derived names by distinguishing two senses: potential (eternal) and actual (temporal), using the analogy of a sword's sharpness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's name 'al-Khāliq', do you think of it as a past event or as an eternal reality that gives meaning to your existence right now? How would your gratitude change if you truly lived as if God was 'the Creator' before you were born, and is still creating in your life?
?
Ghazali shows that even a dispute among scholars can be resolved by careful distinction. Is there a confusion in your own faith—perhaps about God's control over your life or His response to your prayers—where you are failing to see that the truth might apply in two different senses? What would it take for you to seek that clarity?
?
If you believe that God's power to create was present from eternity, then your very existence was already in His knowledge and will before time began. Does that thought humble you or make you feel more accountable? How will you let this truth shape your actions today?

Action-Based Divine Names: Potentiality vs. Actuality
Ghazali presents the fourth category of divine names and resolves the dispute using the sword-water analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali resolves the dispute over the eternal applicability of action-based divine names by distinguishing between potentiality (quwwa) and actuality (fiʿl).

Core Idea
Action-based names apply to God eternally in potentiality, not in actuality.

The fourth category of divine names—those like 'Creator' and 'Provider' that relate to actions—sparked a debate: do they apply to God in pre-eternity? Ghazali navigates this by introducing a brilliant analogy of a sword in its sheath. This lesson unpacks his resolution, showing how the dispute dissolves once we distinguish between having the power to act and actually performing the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali presents two opposing views before giving his own solution.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find the statement by the first group ('a group said: it is true eternally') and the second group ('a group said: it is not true'). Understand their reasoning.
The first group fears implying change in God; the second group insists on the absence of creation in pre-eternity.
3
Analyze the sword-water analogy
Ghazali compares the name 'Creator' to a sword called 'sharp' both in the sheath (potential) and during cutting (actual). Similarly, water in a jug is 'irrigating' (potential) and when drunk 'irrigated' (actual). Understand how this distinction applies to God.
The sword in the sheath has the attribute of sharpness, so it is called sharp in potentiality.
4
Apply the analogy to resolve the dispute
See how Ghazali says that the name 'Creator' applies to God in pre-eternity by the first meaning (potentiality) but not by the second (actuality). Both parties are correct when understood properly.
This reconciles the two views without implying change in God's essence.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this distinction between potentiality and actuality is used elsewhere in Islamic theology. Reflect on the importance of precise language in resolving theological disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : ما يدلُّ على الوجود مع إضافة إلى فعل من أفعاله ؛ كالجواد والرازق والخالق والمُعِز والمذل وأمثاله ، وهذا مختلف فيه ؛ فقال قوم : صادق أزلاً ؛ إذ لو لم يصدق .. لكان اتصافه به موجباً للتغير ، وقال قوم : لا يصدق ؛ إذ لا خلق في الأزل ، فكيف يكون خالقاً ؟ والكاشف للغطاء عن هذا : أن السيف في الغمد يسمى صارماً وعند حصول القطع به ، وفي تلك الحالة على الاقتران يسمى صارماً ، وهما بمعنيين مختلفين ؛ فهو في الغمد صارمٌ بالقوة ، وعند حصول القطع صارم بالفعل وكذلك الماء في الكوز يسمى مُرْوِياً، وعند الشرب يسمى مروياً ، وهما إطلاقان مختلفان ؛ فمعنى تسمية السيف في الغمد صارماً : أن الصفة التي يحصلُ بها القطع موجودة في السيف ، فليس امتناع القطع في الحال لقصور في ذات السيف وحدَّتِهِ واستعداده ، بل لأمر آخر وراء ذاته ؛ فبالمعنى الذي يُسمّى السيفُ به في الغمد صارماً .. يصدقُ اسم الخالق على الله عز وجل في الأزل ؛ فإن الخلق إذا جرى بالفعل .. لم يكن لتجدُّدِ أمرٍ في الذات لم يكن ، بل كلُّ ما يشترط لتحقيق الفعل موجود في الأزل ، وبالمعنى الذي يطلق حالة مباشرة القطع للسيف اسم الصارم .. لا يصدقُ في الأزل ، فهذا حظ المعنى ، فقد ظهرَ أَنَّ مَنْ قال : إنه لا يصدقُ في الأزل هذا الاسم.. فهو محق وأراد به المعنى الثاني ، ومن قال : يصدقُ .. فهو محق وأراد به المعنى الأول ، وإذا كُشف الغطاء عن هذا الوجه .. ارتفع الخلاف (۱) (۱) ولإمام المتكلمين الفخر الرازي كلام نفيس في هذا البحث عقده في كلامه عن البسملة في التفسير الكبير )
The fourth: that which indicates existence with an addition to an action from His actions; like al-Jawad (the Generous), al-Raziq (the Provider), al-Khaliq (the Creator), al-Mu'izz (the Honorer), al-Mudhill (the Abaser) and their likes. And this is disputed; so a group said: it is true eternally; because if it were not true... then His being described by it would necessitate change. And a group said: it is not true; because there is no creation in pre-eternity, so how could He be Creator? And the unveiler of the cover over this is: that the sword in the sheath is called 'sharp' (ṣārim) and when cutting occurs with it, and in that state upon conjunction it is called 'sharp', and they are two different meanings; so it is in the sheath sharp by potentiality (bil-quwwa), and when cutting occurs it is sharp by actuality (bil-fi'l). Likewise water in the jug is called 'irrigating' (murwi), and when drunk it is called 'irrigated' (murwiyyan), and they are two different applications. So the meaning of naming the sword in the sheath 'sharp' is that the attribute by which cutting occurs is present in the sword; so the prevention of cutting at the moment is not due to a deficiency in the essence of the sword and its sharpness and its readiness, but due to something else beyond its essence. So by the meaning by which the sword is called 'sharp' in the sheath... the name 'Creator' applies to Allah, Mighty and Majestic, in pre-eternity; for creation, when it occurs actually... was not due to the renewal of something in the essence that was not, rather everything that is required for the realization of the act is present in pre-eternity. And by the meaning that is applied in the state of direct cutting of the sword, the name 'sharp'... does not apply in pre-eternity. So this is the share of the meaning. So it has become apparent that he who said: 'It does not apply in pre-eternity, this name'... is correct and intended the second meaning, and he who said: 'It applies'... is correct and intended the first meaning. And when the cover is removed from this aspect... the disagreement is resolved. (1) (1) And for the Imam of the theologians, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, there is a precious discussion on this research which he included in his speech about the Basmala in al-Tafsir al-Kabir.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage classifies the fourth category of divine names—those that indicate existence in relation to an action—and resolves the dispute over whether such names apply eternally. Ghazali first presents the two opposing views: some say the names are true eternally, otherwise it would imply change in God; others say they are not true eternally because the act (e.g., creation) did not occur in pre-eternity. Ghazali then introduces an analogy of a sword in a sheath and water in a jug to distinguish between potentiality (quwwa) and actuality (fiʿl). The sword is called sharp in the sheath because it has the capacity to cut, even if not actually cutting. Similarly, the name 'Creator' applies to God in pre-eternity because all conditions for creation are present in His essence, even if the act itself is not yet actual. Thus, Ghazali reconciles both sides: those who deny eternal applicability are referring to the actual performance of the act, while those who affirm it are referring to the essential attribute of power. The dispute is resolved by recognizing this distinction. The footnote mentions that Fakhr al-Din al-Razi has a valuable discussion on this topic in his commentary on the Qur'an.

al-quwwa
al-fi'l
ism al-khāliq
ṣifāt al-maʿnā
ṣārim
murwi

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali denies the eternal applicability of action-based names outright.
Correction: Ghazali actually affirms it in the sense of potentiality while denying it in the sense of actuality. Both views are reconciled by this distinction.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali's sword analogy illustrate about the name 'Creator' in pre-eternity?
A) The name 'Creator' does not apply to God in any sense in pre-eternity.
B) The name 'Creator' applies to God in pre-eternity in the sense of potentiality (quwwa).
C) The name 'Creator' applies to God only after the act of creation occurs.
D) The name 'Creator' implies change in God's essence when creation occurs.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the dispute over action-based divine names by distinguishing potentiality (attribute present) from actuality (act performed), showing that both opposing views are correct in their respective senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God is al-Khaliq, but do you live as though His power to create in your life is only 'actual' when you see results? Are you waiting for Him to act, forgetting that the potential for your transformation is already eternally present in Him? What would change today if you truly trusted that His attributes are fully there before any change occurs?
?
The sword is sharp in the sheath even when it is not cutting. In your life, God's attributes like al-Raziq (the Provider) are present in potentiality even when provision seems delayed. Are you restless in the sheath, doubting the sharpness? How can you rest in the certainty that His power and will are already fully there, even when the act is not yet seen?
?
Ghazali resolves the dispute by uncovering two meanings. Is there an area of your faith where you hold only one meaning—either seeing God's action only in the past or only in the future—missing the eternal present of His potential? Will you ask Him to remove the cover from your heart so you can see His attributes with both eyes, not just one?

The Seven Claims Regarding Divine Actions
Ghazali lists seven claims about actions of Allah that he will defend.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's foundational principle that all divine acts are permissible (jā'iz) and the seven specific claims about Allah's actions that he will defend in this section.

Core Idea
Allah's actions are not bound by obligation; reason cannot dictate what He must do.

Ghazali opens the third pole of his work by establishing a crucial principle: nothing about Allah's actions is obligatory. He then lists seven specific claims that he will defend in the coming discussion. This enumeration sets the stage for a deeper exploration of divine freedom and the limits of human reason in determining what Allah must or must not do. Read carefully to grasp the full scope of Ghazali's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the heading 'The Third Pole' and the list format.
This passage is a programmatic statement of Ghazali's position.
2
Identify the general principle
Find the sentence that states the general principle about all divine actions. What key term does Ghazali use to describe them?
Key term: جائز (permissible).
3
List the seven claims
Read through the seven claims Ghazali enumerates. Try to paraphrase each one in your own words. Notice how they challenge common assumptions about divine justice and obligation.
The claims range from burdening servants to sending messengers.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Review the key terms (e.g., جائز, واجب, تكليف) and see how they appear in the passage. Understanding these terms is essential for following Ghazali's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الثالث
في أفعال الله تعالى
وجملة أفعال الله تعالى جائزة لا يُوصفُ شيءٌ منها بالوجوب ، وندعي لا يجب على الله
في هذا القطب سبعة أمورٍ : ندعي : أنَّه
يجوز الله تبارك وتعالى ألا يكلف عباده ، وأنه يجوز أن
يكلفهم ما لا يُطاق ، وأنه يجوز منه إيلام العباد بغير عوض وجناية ، وأنه لا يجب عليه رعاية الأصلح لهم ، وأنه لا يجب عليه ثواب الطاعة وعقاب المعصية ، وأن العبد لا يجب عليه شيء بالعقل بل بالشرع ، وأنه لا يجب على الله تعالى بعثة الرسل ، وأنه لو بعثَ .. لم يكن قبيحاً ولا محالاً ، بل أمكن إظهار صدقهم بالمعجزة .
The Third Pole: On the Actions of Allah, may He be exalted. The totality of Allah's actions are permissible; none of them is described as obligatory. And we assert—nothing is obligatory upon Allah in this pole—seven matters: We assert that Allah, blessed and exalted, may not burden His servants, and that He may burden them with what they cannot bear, and that it is permissible for Him to inflict pain on servants without compensation or sin, and that it is not obligatory upon Him to observe what is most beneficial for them, and that it is not obligatory upon Him to reward obedience or punish disobedience, and that the servant is not obligated to anything by reason but rather by revelation, and that it is not obligatory upon Allah to send messengers, and that if He did send them, it would not be ugly nor impossible; rather, it is possible to demonstrate their truthfulness through miracles.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage introduces the third pole of the book, concerning the actions of Allah. Ghazali begins by stating the general principle: all divine acts are permissible (jā'iz), none are obligatory. He then enumerates seven specific claims he will defend: (1) Allah may choose not to impose obligations on His servants; (2) He may impose obligations beyond their capacity; (3) He may inflict pain without compensation or prior sin; (4) He is not required to do what is most beneficial for His creatures; (5) He is not obliged to reward obedience or punish disobedience; (6) servants are not obligated by reason alone, but only through revelation; (7) sending messengers is not obligatory upon Allah, but if He sends them, it is not impossible or ugly, and their truthfulness can be shown through miracles. This list sets the stage for the subsequent discussion on the meanings of obligatory, good, and evil.

عقاب
بعثة الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can determine what Allah must do, and He is bound to act in certain ways.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that all divine actions are permissible and that nothing is obligatory upon Allah. Reason cannot impose obligations on God; only revelation informs us of His commands and prohibitions for humans.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, which of the following is NOT one of the seven claims he will defend in this section?
A) Allah may not impose obligations on servants.
B) Allah must always do what is most beneficial for His creatures.
C) Allah may inflict pain without compensation.
D) The servant is not obligated by reason alone.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali begins the third pole by asserting that all divine actions are permissible and not subject to rational obligation, and he lists seven specific claims he will defend.

The lesson asserts that Allah may inflict pain without compensation. Have you ever been angry or confused because of suffering you or a loved one faced? How does this truth confront your expectation that God must be fair by your standards?

Since reason alone cannot obligate you, only revelation can, how much do you truly submit to the Qur'an and Sunnah in areas where your intellect objects? Can you name one command you have resisted because it seemed unreasonable to you?

The Impossibility of Benefit in Taklīf
Ghazali challenges the notion of benefit in taklīf with a logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's logical argument that divine obligation (taklīf) cannot have inherent benefit because benefit means removal of hardship, while taklīf itself imposes hardship.

Core Idea
Taklīf is hardship itself; benefit cannot be found in its own opposite.

Imam al-Ghazali opens this passage by engaging with an opponent who claims that taklīf has benefit. He challenges them to define a fourth meaning beyond his three, then explains that even if creation and obligation benefit others, that does not necessitate God acting for His own benefit. This sets the stage for his core argument: benefit is removal of hardship, but taklīf is the imposition of hardship.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening logical challenge
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali challenges the opponent to define a fourth meaning of necessity. Notice that he does not outright deny benefit in creation but questions its relevance.
2
Understand the distinction between benefit for others vs for God
Ghazali concedes that creation and obligation may have benefit for the created, but argues that this does not make them necessary for God. Focus on the phrase 'ما فيه فائدة غيره لم يجب عليه'.
This is the pivotal logical move.
3
Grasp the conclusion that no necessity is derived
Reflect on the final sentence 'وهذا لا مخرج عنه أبداً' – Ghazali asserts that this point is inescapable. The opponent cannot escape the fact that benefit for others imposes no obligation on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الوجوب إلا المعاني الثلاثة وهي منعدمة؟ فإن أردتم معنى رابعاً ففسروه أولاً ثم اذكروا علته، فإنا ربما لا ننكر أن للخلق في الخلق فائدة وكذا في التكليف، ولكن ما فيه فائدة غيره لم يجب عليه إذا لم يكن له فائدة في فائدة غيره، وهذا لا مخرج عنه أبداً. خلق الإنسان في كبد على أنا نقول: إنما يستقيم هذا الكلام في الخلق لا في التكليف. ولا يستقيم في الخلق الموجود، بل في أن يخلقهم في الجنة منعمين من غير غم وحزن وألم، وأما هذا الخلق الموجود فالعقلاء كلهم قد تمنوا العدم؛ وقال بعضهم: ليتني كنت نسياً منسياً، وقال آخر: ليتني لم أك شيئاً، وقال آخر: ليتني كنت هذه التبنة وقد رفعها من الأرض، وقال آخر يشير إلى طائر: ليتني كنت ذلك الطائر، وهذا قول الأنبياء والأولياء وهم العقلاء، وبعضهم يتمنى عدم الخلق، وبعضهم يتمنى عدم التكليف بأن يكون جماداً أو طائراً. كيف نثبت قائلة التكليف والفائدة نفي الكلفة؟ فليت شعري؛ كيف يستجيز العاقل أن يقول: للخلق في التكليف فائدة وإنما معنى الفائدة نفي الكلفة، والتكليف في عينه إلزام كلفة، وهو ألم، وإن نظر إلى الثواب فهو الفائدة، وكان قادراً على إيصاله إليهم بغير تكليف؟
From obligation except the three meanings? If you intend a fourth meaning, then explain it first and then mention its cause. For perhaps we do not deny that creation has a benefit in creation, and likewise in obligation. But what has benefit for another is not necessary for Him if He has no benefit in the benefit of another. This has no way out. 'Man was created in hardship' — but we say: This statement only applies to creation, not to obligation. And it does not apply to the existing creation, but rather to creating them in Paradise as blissful without grief, sorrow, or pain. As for this existing creation, all the rational ones have wished for non-existence. Some said: 'Would that I were forgotten oblivion.' Another said: 'Would that I were not a thing.' Another said: 'Would that I were this straw that has been lifted from the ground.' Another, pointing to a bird, said: 'Would that I were that bird.' This is the saying of the prophets and saints, and they are the rational ones. Some wish for non-existence of creation, and some wish for non-existence of obligation, to be a lifeless object or a bird. How can one establish the saying that obligation and benefit negate hardship? Would that I knew: how can a rational person dare to say: 'In creation there is benefit in obligation, and the meaning of benefit is the removal of hardship, while obligation in itself is the imposition of hardship, which is pain'? And if one looks to reward, it is the benefit, and He was able to convey it to them without obligation?
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument against those who claim that there is benefit (fā'ida) in divine obligation (taklīf). Ghazali begins by referring to a previous claim that necessity implies only three meanings; if someone proposes a fourth, they must define it first. He concedes that creation and obligation could have benefit for the created, but argues that if something benefits another, it does not become necessary for God if He derives no benefit from it. Then he quotes the Quranic phrase 'Man was created in hardship' (90:4) and clarifies that this applies to the act of creating, not to the state of being obligated. The existing creation, however, is so full of hardship that all rational people—including prophets and saints—have wished for non-existence, citing various statements. Some wished never to have been created, others wished to be without obligation. He then asks how anyone can claim that obligation has benefit when benefit means removal of hardship, yet obligation itself is the imposition of hardship and pain. Even if reward is considered the benefit, God could have given reward directly without obligation. Thus the claim of benefit in taklīf is incoherent.

Key Terms
تكليف
فائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any good outcome counts as benefit, making taklīf beneficial because it leads to reward.
Correction: Ghazali argues that benefit properly means removal of hardship. Taklīf itself is hardship, so it cannot be a benefit. Even reward, which could be a benefit, could have been given directly without obligation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does benefit for another not make an action necessary for God?
A) Because God is not subject to benefit.
B) Because if God has no benefit in that benefit, it does not become incumbent.
C) Because benefit is only for created beings.
D) Because taklīf has no benefit at all.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that the supposed benefit in taklīf cannot impose necessity on God, because benefit for others does not obligate Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself believing that God owes you something because you have obeyed or suffered? How does Ghazali's argument challenge that feeling, and what would it mean for you to fully accept that God is not obligated by your benefit?
?
Think of a time you felt resentment toward God over a hardship. Was there an unspoken assumption that because the hardship didn't benefit you, God had no right to impose it? How does this lesson expose that assumption as a misunderstanding of who God is?
?
If taklīf brings no benefit to God and its benefit to you is not what obligates Him, then why does He command you? Let that question unsettle you: what does it reveal about your motives in worship?

The Wishes of the Wise for Non-Existence
Ghazali cites the wishes of the wise for non-existence to refute benefit in taklīf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the widespread desire for non-existence among the wise, including prophets and saints, to argue that taklīf cannot be inherently beneficial.

Core Idea
Even the wise wish they had never been created or obligated, proving taklīf is not a benefit.

After his logical argument, Ghazali turns to empirical evidence: the testimony of rational people, including prophets and saints, who have wished for non-existence or at least freedom from obligation. He quotes their statements and then concludes that claiming benefit in taklīf is absurd because benefit means removal of hardship, yet taklīf is hardship itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse reference
Ghazali quotes 'Man was created in hardship' (90:4) and clarifies that it applies to creation, not obligation, and only to a hypothetical creation in Paradise without suffering.
2
Examine the quotations from the wise
Read the various sayings from prophets and saints who wished for non-existence, to be forgotten, or to be a bird. Notice that these are rational people, the 'uqalā'.
These wishes are evidence that the existing creation with obligation is burdensome.
3
Understand the two types of wishes
Ghazali distinguishes those who wish for non-existence of creation altogether and those who wish for non-existence of obligation (to be a lifeless object or bird).
4
Grasp the rhetorical conclusion
Read the final rhetorical question: how can anyone claim benefit in taklīf when benefit means removal of hardship, yet taklīf imposes hardship? Even reward could have been given directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الوجوب إلا المعاني الثلاثة وهي منعدمة؟ فإن أردتم معنى رابعاً ففسروه أولاً ثم اذكروا علته، فإنا ربما لا ننكر أن للخلق في الخلق فائدة وكذا في التكليف، ولكن ما فيه فائدة غيره لم يجب عليه إذا لم يكن له فائدة في فائدة غيره، وهذا لا مخرج عنه أبداً. خلق الإنسان في كبد على أنا نقول: إنما يستقيم هذا الكلام في الخلق لا في التكليف. ولا يستقيم في الخلق الموجود، بل في أن يخلقهم في الجنة منعمين من غير غم وحزن وألم، وأما هذا الخلق الموجود فالعقلاء كلهم قد تمنوا العدم؛ وقال بعضهم: ليتني كنت نسياً منسياً، وقال آخر: ليتني لم أك شيئاً، وقال آخر: ليتني كنت هذه التبنة وقد رفعها من الأرض، وقال آخر يشير إلى طائر: ليتني كنت ذلك الطائر، وهذا قول الأنبياء والأولياء وهم العقلاء، وبعضهم يتمنى عدم الخلق، وبعضهم يتمنى عدم التكليف بأن يكون جماداً أو طائراً. كيف نثبت قائلة التكليف والفائدة نفي الكلفة؟ فليت شعري؛ كيف يستجيز العاقل أن يقول: للخلق في التكليف فائدة وإنما معنى الفائدة نفي الكلفة، والتكليف في عينه إلزام كلفة، وهو ألم، وإن نظر إلى الثواب فهو الفائدة، وكان قادراً على إيصاله إليهم بغير تكليف؟
From obligation except the three meanings? If you intend a fourth meaning, then explain it first and then mention its cause. For perhaps we do not deny that creation has a benefit in creation, and likewise in obligation. But what has benefit for another is not necessary for Him if He has no benefit in the benefit of another. This has no way out. 'Man was created in hardship' — but we say: This statement only applies to creation, not to obligation. And it does not apply to the existing creation, but rather to creating them in Paradise as blissful without grief, sorrow, or pain. As for this existing creation, all the rational ones have wished for non-existence. Some said: 'Would that I were forgotten oblivion.' Another said: 'Would that I were not a thing.' Another said: 'Would that I were this straw that has been lifted from the ground.' Another, pointing to a bird, said: 'Would that I were that bird.' This is the saying of the prophets and saints, and they are the rational ones. Some wish for non-existence of creation, and some wish for non-existence of obligation, to be a lifeless object or a bird. How can one establish the saying that obligation and benefit negate hardship? Would that I knew: how can a rational person dare to say: 'In creation there is benefit in obligation, and the meaning of benefit is the removal of hardship, while obligation in itself is the imposition of hardship, which is pain'? And if one looks to reward, it is the benefit, and He was able to convey it to them without obligation?
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a polemical argument against those who claim that there is benefit (fā'ida) in divine obligation (taklīf). Ghazali begins by referring to a previous claim that necessity implies only three meanings; if someone proposes a fourth, they must define it first. He concedes that creation and obligation could have benefit for the created, but argues that if something benefits another, it does not become necessary for God if He derives no benefit from it. Then he quotes the Quranic phrase 'Man was created in hardship' (90:4) and clarifies that this applies to the act of creating, not to the state of being obligated. The existing creation, however, is so full of hardship that all rational people—including prophets and saints—have wished for non-existence, citing various statements. Some wished never to have been created, others wished to be without obligation. He then asks how anyone can claim that obligation has benefit when benefit means removal of hardship, yet obligation itself is the imposition of hardship and pain. Even if reward is considered the benefit, God could have given reward directly without obligation. Thus the claim of benefit in taklīf is incoherent.

تكليف
فائدة
كلفة
ثواب
خلق
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that wishing for non-existence is sinful or a sign of ingratitude.
Correction: Ghazali attributes these wishes to prophets and saints, the most rational people, showing that such wishes are natural responses to the hardship of taklīf, not sins.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do some of the wise wish for regarding obligation?
A) They wish for more obligation to gain more reward.
B) They wish for non-existence of obligation, to be a lifeless object or bird.
C) They wish to become angels.
D) They wish to be created in Paradise immediately.

Key Takeaway
The widespread desire for non-existence among the wise shows that taklīf is not a benefit; rather, it is a burden that even prophets wished to avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely complained about the difficulty of religious obligations. After reading Ghazali's argument, do you still think God's commands are meant to bring you worldly benefit? Or do you now see them as a test you must endure for the afterlife? How will this change the way you approach your daily worship starting today?
?
Even prophets and saints wished to be free from obligation. Have you ever wished you were not accountable? What does that reveal about your true desire—do you want ease more than closeness to God? Take a moment to honestly assess your intention in fulfilling obligations.
?
If God could have given you Paradise without any effort, but instead chose to test you with hardships, does that make you feel resentment or gratitude? How can you shift your mindset from seeing taklīf as a burden to seeing it as an opportunity for eternal reward that you could not have earned otherwise? Be specific about one change you will make.

Refutation of Merit-Based Reward
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazilī objection that reward is deserved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the Mu'tazilī claim that reward is deserved, and why he considers such a view arrogant and a rejection of God's grace.

Core Idea
Claiming reward is deserved is arrogance that rejects God's grace.

In this passage, Ghazali confronts a central Mu'tazilī objection: that reward in the afterlife is deserved by human effort, not a free gift. He responds not with a technical argument but with a moral and spiritual condemnation, calling such thinking arrogant and irrational. We'll read his fiery answer and unpack its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The Mu'tazilī claim: reward (thawāb) if by desert (istihqāq) is too elevated to be a mere quibble; rather it is by grace (imtinān) and initiation (ibtidā').
Note that they argue desert enhances reward, not diminishes it.
2
Analyze Ghazali's answer
Ghazali's response is emotional: he says such thinking stems from arrogance, refusing to accept God's favors. He declares that seeking refuge from such a mind is more important than seeking refuge from Satan.
Observe the use of hyperbole: the one who thinks this is 'too base to be addressed'.
3
Identify the rhetorical questions
Ghazali asks: how can one be considered rational if this enters his mind? And who finds eternal Paradise burdensome without prior toil is contemptible.
These questions serve to shame the opponent.
4
Understand the concession
Ghazali says all this is only if it is conceded that reward after obligation is deserved; he then promises to show the opposite later.
This reveals Ghazali's strategy: granting the premise for argument then disproving it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: الثواب إذا كان باستحقاق كان أرفع من أن يكون تحريجة: الثواب بعد التكليف يكون بالامتنان والابتداء. والجواب: أن الاستعاذة بالله تعالى من عقل ينتهي إلى التكبر على الله عز وجل والترفع عن احتمال مننه، وتقدير اللذة في الخروج من نعمته أولى من الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان الرجيم. وليت شعري؛ كيف يعد من العقلاء من يخطر بباله مثل هذه التحريجة؟! ومن يستثقل المقام أبد الآباد في الجنة من غير تقدم تعب الجهل شيئاً وتكليف أخس من أن يخاطب أو يناظر، هذا لو سلم أن الثواب بعد التكليف يكون مستحقاً، وسنبين نقيضه.
If it is said: Reward—if it is by desert—is higher than to be a quibble: reward after obligation is by grace and initiation. The answer: That seeking refuge with God Almighty from a mind that ends in arrogance towards God Almighty and raising oneself above accepting His favors, and considering pleasure in departing from His grace is more deserving of seeking refuge with God from the accursed Satan. Would that I knew: how can one be counted among the rational when such a quibble enters his mind?! And whoever finds it burdensome to dwell forever in Paradise without preceding any toil of ignorance or obligation is too base to be addressed or debated. This is if it is conceded that reward after obligation is deserved; and we will show its opposite.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objection, attributed to the Mu'tazila, states that if reward (thawāb) is by desert (istihqāq), it is too elevated to be a mere quibble; rather, reward after obligation is by grace (imtinān) and initiation (ibtidā'). In other words, they argue that reward is deserved, not a gratuitous favor. Ghazali's answer is sharp: he says that such a mindset is arrogant—it refuses to accept God's favors and considers pleasure in departing from His grace. He then asks rhetorically how anyone rational could entertain such a quibble. He adds that anyone who finds eternal residence in Paradise burdensome unless preceded by toil and obligation is too base to be addressed. He then explicitly states that this is only conceded for the sake of argument, and he will later show the opposite—that reward is not deserved.

ابتداء
