Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward being deserved (istihqāq) actually elevates God's grace rather than diminishes it.
Correction: Ghazali argues that claiming desert leads to arrogance and a refusal to accept God's free favor; it is not a valid theological position.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali respond to the claim that reward is deserved (mustahaqq)?
A) He agrees but says it is still by grace.
B) He says that such a claim leads to arrogance and a rejection of God's grace.
C) He argues that reward is not given in the afterlife.
D) He says that the Mu'tazila are correct.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the Mu'tazilī claim by showing it stems from arrogance and fails to recognize that all reward is ultimately a gift of divine grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself feeling entitled to God's reward because of your worship? Imagine standing before Him after a lifetime of prayer, yet realizing that every moment of it was only possible by His grace. Does the thought that your deeds give you no claim on Him make you shrink or grow? What will you do today to kill that hidden sense of entitlement?
?
If someone told you that your salvation is entirely by grace and not by any merit of yours, would you feel relieved or offended? Why? Examine your heart: is there a part of you that wants to 'earn' Paradise so you can feel you 'deserve' it? How does Ghazali's rebuke of the 'rational' quibble challenge that desire?
?
Consider the phrase 'seeking refuge with God from a mind that ends in arrogance towards God.' When you think about your good deeds, do you ever mentally compare yourself to others? What would it mean for you to truly depend on God's grace alone, not your deeds? Write a short prayer asking God to free you from the arrogance of imagining you have a claim on Him.

The Source of Obedience: God's Grace Alone
Ghazali questions the origin of human obedience and asserts it is solely from God's grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that human obedience and its reward are entirely dependent on God's grace, not on any independent human capacity.

Core Idea
Human obedience has no independent source; it is entirely from God's grace.

Ghazali asks a piercing rhetorical question: where does the obedience that earns reward come from? He answers with a cascade of causes, all traceable back to God's grace. This passage dismantles the notion that humans can claim any credit for their good deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's rhetorical style come through before you interpret.
Notice the Quranic quotation (24:21) embedded in the argument.
2
Identify the rhetorical question and implied answer
Find Ghazali's opening question: 'From where did the servant find obedience?' Then notice how he builds the answer through a series of rhetorical sub-questions.
The implied answer is that everything comes from God.
3
Trace the chain of causes
Ghazali lists causes: God's existence, power, will, soundness of limbs, presence of means. See how each points back to God's grace.
This chain refutes any claim of independent human agency.
4
Understand the concluding warning
Ghazali warns that denying this is a departure from the instinct of reason. He advises praying for reason for the opponent rather than debating.
This shows the gravity of the error from Ghazali's perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ليت شعري؛ الطاعة التي بها يستحق الثواب من أين وجدها العبد؟ ولولا فضل الله عليكم ورحمته ما زكى منكم من أحد أبداً. وهل لها سبب سوى وجوده وقدرته وإرادته وصحة أعضائه وحضور أسبابه؟ لمد أبدا وهل لكل ذلك مصدر إلا فضل الله ونعمته؟ فنعوذ بالله من الانخلاع عن غريزة العقل بالكلية؛ فإن هذا الكلام من ذلك النمط، فينبغي أن يسترزق الله تعالى عقلاً لصاحبه، ولا يشتغل بمناظرته.
Then would that I knew: the obedience by which reward is deserved—from where did the servant find it? And were it not for the grace of God upon you and His mercy, none of you would ever be pure. And has it any cause besides His existence, His power, His will, soundness of limbs, and presence of its means? Ever? And has all that any source except the grace of God and His favor? So we seek refuge with God from completely tearing away from the instinct of reason; for this speech is of that kind. So one should ask God Almighty for reason for its owner, and not engage in debating him.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a further argument against the Mu'tazilī position. Ghazali asks rhetorically where the servant finds the obedience (ṭā'a) by which reward is deserved. He quotes the Quran (24:21) to emphasize that without God's grace and mercy, no one would be pure. He then enumerates the causes of obedience: God's existence, power, will, the soundness of limbs, and the presence of means—all are from God. He asks whether all these have any source except God's grace and favor. He then concludes that any speech contrary to this is a departure from the instinct of reason, and one should pray for reason for its speaker rather than engage in debate.

Misconception: That human actions are independent and not entirely dependent on God's grace.
Correction: Ghazali asserts that obedience and its causes all originate solely from God's grace.

Checkpoint

B) God's grace, power, and will.
C) The angels' assistance.
D) The Prophet's intercession.

Key Takeaway
Obedience and its reward are solely from God's grace; any claim of independence is a departure from reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you truly recognize that even your ability to move your limbs and your will to obey are gifts from God? How would your humility change if you constantly reminded yourself of this?
?
If every good deed you have ever done is solely from God's grace, what do you have to boast about? What does that mean for how you view others who struggle to obey?
?
Right now, think of a time you felt proud of your obedience. Can you trace every element of that act—your existence, your health, your intention, the opportunity—back to God's favor? Let that realization lead you to immediate thanks and a resolve to never claim credit again.

Reward is by Grace, Not Entitlement
Ghazali refutes reward by entitlement and argues it is by grace.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that divine reward is a matter of grace (imtinan) rather than entitlement (istihqaq), and how he grounds this in the dependence of all human action on God's prior grace.

Core Idea
Every good deed comes from God, so reward cannot be owed.

Imam al-Ghazali responds to a rationalist objection that reward must be deserved. He turns the tables by showing that even the obedience that supposedly 'earns' reward is itself a gift from God. Thus all reward is grace, not a right.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the style: an objection followed by a sharp response.
The passage includes rhetorical questions and strong language.
2
Identify the objection and Ghazali's response
Find where the objector claims reward is by entitlement. Ghazali rejects this and instead asserts reward is by grace. Notice how he characterizes the objector.
Ghazali uses sarcasm and strong rebuke.
3
Trace the argument from the source of obedience
Ghazali asks: where does the obedience that supposedly earns reward come from? Follow his reasoning: it depends on God's existence, power, will, and provision. Conclude that all is from God, so reward is grace.
This is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الثوابُ إذا كان باستحقاق .. كان الدَّ وأرفع من أن يكونَ أن يكون تحريجة : الثواب بعد بالامتنان والابتداء والجواب : أن الاستعاذة بالله تعالى من عقل ينتهي إلى التكبر على الله عز وجل والترفع عن احتمال مننه ، وتقدير اللذة في الخروج من نعمته . . أولى من الاستعاذة بالله من الشيطان الرجيم الاستحقاق الذ وأرفع وليت شعري ؛ كيف يُعَدُّ من العقلاء من يخطر بباله مثل هذه صاحب هذه التحريجة ما ترك من الوساوس ؟! فمن يستثقلُ المُقام أبد الآباد في الجنة من غير تقدم تعب الجهل شيئاً وتكليف .. أخس من أن يخاطب أو يناظر ، هذا لو سُلَّمَ أَنَّ الثوابَ بعد التكليف يكون مستحقاً ، وسنبين نقيضه ثم ليت شعري ؛ الطاعةُ التي بها يستحقُ الثواب من أين وجدها العبد ؟ وَلَوْلَا فَضْلُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُم ورحمته [؟] وهل لها سبب سوى وجوده وقدرته وإرادته وصحة أعضائه وحضور أسبابه ؟ [؟] وهل لكل ذلك مصدر إلا فضل الله ونعمته ؟! فنعوذ بالله من الانخلاع عن غريزة العقل بالكلية ؛ فإنَّ هذا الكلام من ذلك النمط ، فينبغي أن يسترزق الله تعالى عقلاً لصاحبه ، ولا يشتغل بمناظرته
If it is said: Reward, if it is by entitlement... he was lower and higher than to be a sophistry: reward after is by grace and initiative. The answer: That seeking refuge in Allah from an intellect that ends in arrogance towards Allah and haughtiness from bearing His favors, and considering pleasure in departing from His blessing... is more fitting than seeking refuge in Allah from the accursed Satan. The entitlement which is higher. Would that I knew; how is he considered among the rational ones who conceives such a thing? The owner of this sophistry has not left any whisperings?! So whoever finds eternal abode in Paradise burdensome without prior hardship of ignorance and obligation... is too base to be addressed or debated, this if it is granted that reward after obligation is entitled, and we will show its opposite. Then would that I knew: obedience by which reward is entitled, from where did the servant find it? And if not for God's grace upon you and His mercy [?]. And does it have any cause other than His existence, power, will, soundness of limbs, and presence of its causes? [?] And is there any source for all that except God's grace and favor?! So we seek refuge in Allah from complete detachment from the instinct of intellect; for this speech is of that pattern, so one should ask God for intellect for its owner, and not occupy himself with debating him.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a series of rhetorical questions. Ghazali begins by reporting an objection: if reward is by entitlement, then it would be low and high [meaning contradictory or absurd]—a mere sophism that reward is after [obligation] by grace and initiative. Then he gives the answer: seeking refuge in Allah from an intellect that leads to arrogance and contempt for His favors is more important than seeking refuge from Satan. He then scolds the objector as not being rational, questioning how a rational person could think that eternal abode in Paradise without prior hardship is burdensome. He says such a person is too base to argue with. He then grants for argument's sake that reward after obligation is entitled but promises to show its opposite. He then asks: from where does the servant obtain the obedience that entitles reward? Without God's grace and mercy, it cannot exist. All causes—existence, power, will, health, opportunities—come from God. Therefore, all reward is by grace. He concludes by seeking refuge from losing reason entirely and advises not to engage such a person in debate.

استحقاق
امتنان
ابتداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Ghazali denies any connection between obedience and reward, or that reward is random.
Correction: Ghazali affirms reward is by God's grace, not arbitrary; it is connected to obedience but obedience itself is from God.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's argument, why cannot reward be by entitlement?
A) Because entitlement requires prior hardship which is absent
B) Because obedience itself comes from God's grace, not from the servant independently
C) Because God is too high to grant reward based on merit
D) Because the Mu'tazila denied it

Key Takeaway
Reward is entirely from God's grace because even the obedience that could warrant it is itself a divine gift.

?

?
If every good deed you do is only possible because of God's grace, what happens to your pride? Where does that leave your sense of self-achievement?
?
Imagine living each day fully aware that even your faith is a gift. How would that change the way you pray, give charity, or seek forgiveness? What would you ask God for right now?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Futility Objection
Ghazali refutes the claim that obligating the impossible has no benefit and is futile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the claim that obligating the impossible is futile, and how divine commands can have benefit beyond the servant's compliance.

Core Idea
Divine commands have hidden benefits even when compliance is impossible.

The objection that obligating the impossible is futile seems rational, but Ghazali shows it rests on an assumption. In this passage, he dissects the claim and provides examples from sacred history to demonstrate that commands can have benefit beyond the act of compliance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its structure
Identify the objector's three claims: no benefit, therefore futile, futile impossible for God.
Notice the concise logical chain.
2
Understand Ghazali's denial of the first premise
Ghazali does not concede that there is no benefit; he suggests there might be a hidden benefit known to God.
The benefit is not limited to compliance and reward.
3
Learn from the examples
Examine the examples of Abraham's command to sacrifice (abrogated) and Abu Jahl's command to believe (unfulfilled). Both show benefit in the command itself.
These examples illustrate the broader concept of benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحريجة: فإن لم يكن فإن قيل: فهو مما لا فائدة فيه، وما لا فائدة فيه فهو عبث، والعبث على الله تعالى محال؟ قلنا: هذه ثلاث دعاوى: الأولى: أنه لا فائدة فيه، ولا نسلم; فلعل فيه فائدة للعباد اطلع الله تعالى عليها، فليست الفائدة هي الامتثال والثواب عليه، بل ربما يكون في إظهار الأمر وما يتبعه من اعتقاد التكليف فائدة; فقد يُنسخ الأمر قبل الامتثال; كما أمر إبراهيم عليه السلام بذبح ولده ثم نسخه قبل الامتثال، وأمر أبا جهل بالإيمان وأخبر أنه لا يؤمن، وخلاف خبره محال.
Objection: If it is said: It has no benefit in it, and whatever has no benefit is futile, and futility is impossible for God? We say: These are three claims: The first: That there is no benefit in it; we do not concede that. Perhaps there is a benefit for the servants that God has revealed. The benefit is not (limited to) compliance and the reward upon it; rather, perhaps there is benefit in the manifestation of the command and what follows it of the belief in obligation. Indeed, the command may be abrogated before compliance, as God commanded Abraham to slaughter his son and then abrogated it before compliance, and He commanded Abu Jahl to believe and informed that he would not believe, and contradicting His information is impossible.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an objection and its answer. The objector argues that obligating the impossible has no benefit, hence it is futile (ʿabath), which is impossible for God. The answer denies the premise: there may be a benefit known to God. The benefit is not restricted to the servant's compliance and reward; rather, the very act of commanding and the subsequent belief in obligation can be a benefit. Two examples are given: the command to Abraham to sacrifice his son, which was abrogated before compliance, and the command to Abu Jahl to believe, which God knew he would not fulfill. In both cases, the command had a benefit even though compliance did not occur.

Key Terms
عبث
فائدة
امتثال
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that if a command is not fulfilled, it has no benefit or purpose.
Correction: Commands can have benefit in themselves, such as testing obedience, manifesting divine wisdom, or establishing a legal framework, even if not ultimately performed.

Checkpoint
What example does Ghazali give to show that a command can have benefit even without compliance?
A) Commanding angels to prostrate
B) Commanding Abraham to sacrifice his son, later abrogated
C) Commanding Moses to strike the sea
D) Commanding Adam to not eat from the tree

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that divine commands can have benefit even when compliance is impossible, as seen in the abrogated command to Abraham and the command to Abu Jahl.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a religious command as pointless because you felt it was impossible to fulfill? How does this lesson challenge that attitude, and what hidden benefit might Allah intend for you in that command?
?
The command to Abraham was abrogated, yet it produced immense benefit. Think of a time you obeyed a command without seeing the outcome—what did you gain from the act of obedience itself?
?
Abu Jahl was commanded to believe despite Allah's knowledge that he would not. Are there commands you ignore because you assume they won't make a difference? How can you shift your focus from outcome to the intrinsic value of obedience?

The Second Proof: Commanding the Impossible is not Intrinsically Impossible
Ghazali presents the second proof and discusses the nature of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that commanding what God knows will not happen is impossible due to another (not in itself), and why this distinction is crucial for the theological problem of divine obligation.

Core Idea
Even impossibility due to another prevents occurrence just as intrinsic impossibility does.

Ghazali now presents a second, more intricate proof to address the objection that commanding the impossible (تكليف المحال) is itself impossible. He uses the specific case of Abu Jahl, who was commanded to believe despite God's knowledge that he would not. This leads him to a key distinction: the impossibility here is not because the commanded act is inherently absurd, but because God's prior knowledge makes its occurrence impossible. Follow along as he unpacks this distinction and its implications for human ability and divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the example
Start by reading the entire Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Ghazali begins with the concrete case of Abu Jahl: God commanded him to believe, knew he wouldn't, and informed through the Prophet that he wouldn't. This sets up the problem.
The apparent paradox: Abu Jahl is commanded to believe that he will not believe.
2
Distinguish the two types of impossibility
Focus on Ghazali's 'verification' (تحريره) where he distinguishes between محال لذاته (impossible in itself) and محال لغيره (impossible due to another). Understand how he applies this to the Abu Jahl case: the impossibility of his believing is due to God's knowledge, not an intrinsic contradiction.
This distinction is the core of the proof.
3
Examine the discussion of ability (قدرة)
See how Ghazali addresses the issue of human ability. He contrasts the Ash'ari view (ability exists only at the moment of the act, so the disbeliever had ability only for disbelief) with the Mu'tazili view (ability exists but is conditional). Note his argument that both positions still lead to commanding what is impossible due to another.
This shows the universality of the problem.
4
Reflect on the supporting quotations
The passage includes quotations from Sadr al-Shari'ah, Imam al-Haramayn, and al-Taftazani. Consider how each reinforces or elaborates on Ghazali's point. For example, Sadr al-Shari'ah argues that commanding the act is actually commanding the intention, which is possible.
These quotes may be later additions but are now part of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني في المسألة ولا محيص لأحد عنه : أن الله تعالى كلَّف أبا جهل أن يؤمنَ ، وعلم أنه لا يؤمن ، وأخبر عنه بأنه لا يؤمنُ ، فكأنه أمر بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن ، إذ كان من قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه لا يؤمن ، وكان هو مأموراً بتصديقه .. فقد قيل له : صدق بأنك لا تصدق ! وهذا محال (٢) وتحريره: أن خلاف المعلوم محال وقوعه ، ولكن ليس محالاً لذاته بل هو محال لغيره ، والمحالُ لغيره في امتناع الوقوع كالمحال لذاته ، ومن قال : إن الكفار الذين لم يؤمنوا ما كانوا مأمورين بالإيمان .. فقد جحد الشرع ، ومن قال : كان الإيمان منهم متصوراً مع علم الله سبحانه وتعالى الإخبار بكفره في القرآن الكريم، وهو جزء مما يطالب الإيمان به ، على أن التمثيل بأبي جهل لا يبعد إن قلنا : إن بعض أي التنزيل نزل في حقه ومبيناً بختم أمره على الكفر . وهذا مبني على مذهب الأشاعرة في ذلك ، وإلا .. فجمهور أهل السنة أن هذا ليس بتكليف المحال أصلًا بأنه لا يقعُ .. فقد أنكر العقل؛ فقد اضطر كلُّ فريق إلى القول بتصور الأمر بما لا يتصور امتثاله، وكان للكافر عليه قدرة ولا يغني عن هذا قول القائل : إنه كان مقدوراً عليه أما على أصلنا .. فلا قدرة قبل الفعل ، ولم تكن لهم قدرة إلا على الكفر الذي صدر منهم (١) وأما عند المعتزلة .. فلا يمتنع وجود القدرة ، ولكن القدرة غير كافية لوقوع المقدور ، بل له شرط كالإرادة وغيرها ، ومن شروطه ألا ينقلب علم الله تعالى جهلاً ، والقدرة لا ترادُ لعينها ، بل ليتيسر الفعل بها ، يتيسر فعل يؤدي إلى انقلاب العلم جهلاً ؟! فكيف عليه ما هو فاستبان أن هذا واقع في ثبوت التكليف بما هو محال لغيره ، فكذا يقاس محال لذاته ؛ إذ لا فرق بينهما في إمكان التلفظ ، ولا في تصورِ الاقتضاء ، ولا في الاستقباح والاستحسان (٢) قال صدر الشريعة المحبوبي : ( فإن قيل : التكليف بالمحال لازم على تقدير التوسط أيضاً ؛ لأن العبد غير قادر على إيجاد الفعل ، بل يوجد بخلق الله ، فيكون التكليف بالفعل تكليفاً بالمحال ؟ قلنا : نعم ، لكن للعبد قصد اختياري، فالمراد بالتكليف بالحركة التكليف بالقصد إليها ... ، أو التكليف بالحركة بناء على قدرته على سببها الموصل إليها غالباً وهو القصد ) . ( التلويح إلى كشف حقائق التنقيح ٩ ( ١ / ٤٢٠ ) . قال المؤلف في ( المستصفى : (۲۹۸/۱ ) : ( ومن جوز تكليف ما لا يطاق عقلاً .. فإنه يمنعه شرعاً ؛ لقوله تعالى : ( لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا). (۲) قال إمام الحرمين : ( وقد نطقت آي من كتاب الله تعالى بالاستعاذة من تكليف ما لا طاقة به ؛ فقال تعالى : (رَبَّنَا وَلَا تُحَمِّلْنَا مَا لَا طَاقَةَ لَنَا بِهِ ) ، فلو لم يكن ذلك ممكناً .. لما ساعت الاستعانة منه ) . ( الإرشاد » ( ص ٢٢٨ ) . وقال السعد التفتازاني : ( ثم عدم التكليف بما ليس في الوسع متفق عليه ؛ لقوله تعالى : لا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ، والأمر في قوله تعالى : ( أَنْبِتُونِي بِأَسْمَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ )
The second proof concerning the issue, from which there is no escape for anyone: that God Almighty obligated (كلَّف) Abu Jahl to believe, and He knew that he would not believe, and He informed that he would not believe. So it is as if He commanded him to believe that he would not believe, since it was from the statement of the Messenger of God ﷺ that he would not believe, and he was obligated to believe him. So it was said to him: 'Affirm that you do not affirm!' This is impossible (محال) (٢). And its verification: that the occurrence of what is contrary to what is known [by God] is impossible, but it is not impossible in itself (محال لذاته) but rather impossible due to another (محال لغيره). And what is impossible due to another in prevention of occurrence is like what is impossible in itself. And whoever says that the disbelievers who did not believe were not obligated to believe has denied the Law. And whoever says that belief was conceivable from them while God Almighty informed of their disbelief in the Noble Qur'an—and it is part of what one is required to believe in—then the example of Abu Jahl is not far if we say that some verses of revelation were revealed concerning him and clarified the conclusion of his affair with disbelief. This is based on the doctrine of the Ash'aris in that matter; otherwise, the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah [hold] that this is not commanding the impossible (تكليف المحال) at all—that it does not occur. Indeed, he has denied reason; for every group was forced to affirm the conception of commanding what cannot be conceived of being complied with. And the disbeliever had ability (قدرة) over it. And the saying of one who says: 'It was possible for him' does not suffice for this. As for our principle (أصلنا), there is no ability before the act, and they had no ability except for the disbelief that emanated from them (١). As for the Mu'tazila, the existence of ability is not impossible, but ability is not sufficient for the occurrence of the object of ability; rather, it has a condition such as will and others. And among its conditions is that God's knowledge not change into ignorance. And ability is not desired for itself, but so that the act may be facilitated by it. How can an act be facilitated that leads to the change of knowledge into ignorance? So it became clear that this occurs in confirming the obligation (تكليف) of what is impossible due to another. Thus, what is impossible in itself is measured accordingly, for there is no difference between them in the possibility of utterance, nor in the conception of requirement, nor in deeming bad or good (٢). Sadr al-Shari'ah al-Mahbubi said: 'If it is said: Commanding the impossible is necessary also on the assumption of intermediation, because the servant is not able to bring about the act; rather, it comes into existence by God's creation, so commanding the act is commanding the impossible? We say: Yes, but the servant has an intended choice; what is meant by commanding the movement is commanding the intention towards it... or commanding the movement based on his ability over its cause that usually leads to it, which is intention.' (al-Talwih ila Kashf Haqa'iq al-Tanqih, vol. 1, p. 420). The author said in al-Mustasfa (1/298): 'Whoever allows commanding what cannot be borne rationally, then he prevents it legally because of His statement: Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' (2) Imam al-Haramayn said: 'Verses from the Book of God Almighty have spoken of seeking refuge from commanding what is beyond capacity; He said: Our Lord, do not burden us with what we cannot bear. If that were not possible, seeking refuge from it would not be beneficial.' (al-Irshad, p. 228). And al-Sa'd al-Taftazani said: 'Then the absence of commanding what is not within capacity is agreed upon because of His statement: Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity, and the command in His statement: Inform Me of the names of these...'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against the claim that commanding the impossible (تكليف المحال) is impossible. Ghazali begins with the example of Abu Jahl: God commanded him to believe, but knew he would not believe, and informed through the Prophet that he would not. Thus, effectively, Abu Jahl was commanded to believe that he would not believe—an apparent contradiction. Ghazali then clarifies that the impossibility here is not intrinsic (محال لذاته) but due to another factor (محال لغيره)—namely, God's knowledge that it will not occur. However, in terms of prevention of occurrence, both types of impossibility are equivalent. He refutes two extreme positions: those who deny that disbelievers were commanded to believe (which contradicts the Law) and those who claim belief was possible despite God's knowledge (which denies reason). He then addresses the issue of human ability (قدرة): on the Ash'ari view, ability exists only at the moment of the act, so the disbeliever had ability only for disbelief. On the Mu'tazili view, ability exists but is insufficient without conditions, one of which is that God's knowledge must not change. But since God knows the disbeliever will not believe, the ability cannot lead to belief. Thus, both schools face the problem of commanding what is impossible due to another. Ghazali concludes that this type of impossibility is analogous to intrinsic impossibility in terms of verbal expression, conceptual understanding, and moral evaluation. He then includes supporting quotations: from Sadr al-Shari'ah arguing that commanding the act is actually commanding the intention (which is possible), from Imam al-Haramayn noting that the Qur'anic prayer for relief from impossible burdens implies its possibility, and from al-Taftazani affirming that commanding what is beyond capacity is agreed to be impermissible but that does not affect the core argument about metaphysical possibility.

Key Terms
محال لذاته (impossible in itself)
محال لغيره (impossible due to another)
تكليف المحال (commanding the impossible)
قدرة (ability/power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that an impossibility due to another (محال لغيره) is less severe or not really impossible in terms of occurrence.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says: 'والمحال لغيره في امتناع الوقوع كالمحال لذاته' — what is impossible due to another is in prevention of occurrence like what is impossible in itself. Both are equally impossible to occur.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, the impossibility of what God knows will not occur is classified as:
A) Impossible in itself (محال لذاته)
B) Impossible due to another (محال لغيره)
C) Not impossible at all
D) Impossible only for the disbeliever

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that commanding what God knows will not happen is impossible due to another, not in itself, but this distinction does not lessen its impossibility of occurrence, thereby defending the coherence of divine obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Like Abu Jahl, every soul is commanded to believe, though God knows some will not. If you were to stand before Him today, what evidence would you show that your belief is not just a claim but a reality that your entire being strives to uphold—even when it seems impossible?
?
Ghazali’s proof shows that impossibility due to another (like divine knowledge) does not excuse us from obligation. When you say 'I can't' to a religious duty—praying on time, giving up a sin—is that truly an intrinsic impossibility, or a failure of will? What would it mean to own that impossibility as your own?
?
If you truly believe that God’s command is real even when your own ability fails, how does that change the way you approach your weaknesses? Will you continue to make excuses, or will you beg Him for the ability you know you lack?

Refuting the Objection of Contradicting Wisdom
Ghazali responds to the claim that harming the innocent contradicts God's wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's response to the claim that divine wisdom is contradicted by innocent suffering, and why he restricts the meaning of wisdom to knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Wisdom is defined as knowledge of order and power to arrange, not moral justification.

Ghazali now addresses a second objection from the denier: that allowing harm contradicts God's attribute of wisdom. Rather than accepting the opponent's vague notion of wisdom, Ghazali defines it precisely based on his earlier framework. By doing so, he shows that the alleged contradiction dissolves, and any other meaning of wisdom reduces to an empty word.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if they claim... we say: if wisdom is intended as... then... and if intended otherwise...'
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the two scenarios.
2
Identify the two meanings of wisdom
Ghazali presents two possible interpretations of 'wisdom'. The first is his own definition: knowledge of the order of things and power to arrange them. The second is any other meaning proposed by the opponent.
The first meaning is the only one Ghazali accepts as meaningful.
3
Understand why the objection fails
Under Ghazali's definition, allowing harm does not contradict wisdom because it does not undermine God's knowledge or power. The opponent's alternative meaning is dismissed as a meaningless word.
This shows that the objection depends on an unstated, non-technical definition of wisdom.
4
Reflect on the theological method
Notice how Ghazali uses definitional precision to neutralize objections. He does not deny wisdom; he clarifies what it means in the context of divine attributes.
This is a common Ash'ari strategy: define terms rigorously before debating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن زعموا أن تركه يناقض كونه حكيما.. فنقول: الحكمة إن أريد بها العلم بنظام الأمور والقدرة على ترتيبها كما سبق فليس في هذا ما يناقضه. وإن أريد بها أمر آخر.. فليس يجب له عندنا من الحكمة إلا ما ذكرناه، وما وراء ذلك لفظ لا معنى له.
And if they claim that refraining from it contradicts His being wise, we say: wisdom, if intended by it knowledge of the order of things and ability to arrange them as previously, then there is nothing in this that contradicts it. And if intended by it something else, then nothing is obligatory for Him of wisdom according to us except what we mentioned, and beyond that is a word with no meaning.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection: that allowing innocent suffering contradicts God's attribute of wisdom (ḥikma). Ghazali defines wisdom as knowledge of the proper order of things and the power to arrange them accordingly—a definition he has established earlier. He argues that under this definition, there is no contradiction, as the act does not undermine God's knowledge or power. If the opponent intends a different meaning of wisdom, Ghazali responds that the only wisdom God is obligated to have is what has been defined; any additional conception is meaningless.

Key Terms
الحكمة
العلم
النظام
القدرة
الترتيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'wisdom' implies God must always act for a greater good that humans can comprehend.
Correction: Ghazali defines wisdom as knowledge of order and power to arrange, not as a moral principle requiring justification. The objection fails because it imports a foreign concept of wisdom.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali define wisdom as in this passage?
A) The ability to create the best possible world
B) Knowledge of the order of things and power to arrange them
C) Always acting for a greater good
D) A perfect moral sense

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the objection fails because wisdom, properly defined as knowledge of order and power to arrange, is not contradicted by allowing harm; any other meaning is irrelevant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you feel innocent suffering is unjust, does your faith in God's wisdom remain, or do you find yourself demanding an explanation? What does your reaction reveal about your true definition of wisdom?
?
If God's wisdom is not measured by human standards of fairness, how should this change the way you respond to personal trials that seem meaningless? Will you still trust His order and arrangement?
?
Think of a recent hardship that you struggled to accept. Now, with Ghazali's definition of wisdom as knowledge and power, can you humbly admit that your own limited understanding is not the measure? What would it take for you to genuinely say, 'There is nothing in this that contradicts His wisdom'?
